“ During the past few years Eczema has become of very
frequent occurrence, especially in children. In the treat-
ment of this troublesome affection the usual medicinal
remedies failed in many cases to give relief, and I was very
pleased to have a new preparation, named ‘ Oleac,’ put into
my hands for trial. I can recall to my mind three eases of
children in which the usual remedies had no beneficial
effect, and the application of ‘ Oleac ’ according to the
printed directions removed all the irritation of the skin.
I have also heard of many adults using the ‘ Oleac ’ with
great benefit. —JAMES SMEAL, Surgeon, &c., Ararat ”

THE RED PAGE.
LET ’S suppose, for the sake of example,
that you would like an income of .#2,000 a
year. Of course the supposition is merely
hypothetical. Now see how Max Pember-
ton earns that income : his last novel, The House
Under the Sea, gives a oapital clue.
To begin: Pemberton has eight other novels
on sale, —most of them past their youth, but
all selling, and some selling well. Guess his
average income from their royalties at #BOO
per an. Then the new book should be
worth #4OO for serial rights, considering Pem-
berton s reputation and the prosperity of the
magazines that welcome him in England and
America. “Advance on royalties” Should be
another #4OO ; which leaves #4OO for the value of
further work during a year—probably not much
beyond the mark. Discount the whole, and say
Pemberton’s income is #l5OO only : how does he
earn it ?
In the first place, by Keeping On. As Kipling
has pointed out, one book rarely makes money for
the writer of fiction. It is like emptying earth to
fill a pond : you dump in a cart-load, and nothing
is seen : another disappears in the same way :
with the third you may get a little mound to show
above the water ; but it takes half-a-dozen cart-
loads before you view an'v substantial fruit of
your labour. A new writer usually succeeds
nowadays by dint of persistent advertisement:
every new book enlarges his audience, sends
readers in search of. his old books, and
prepares readers for his books forthcoming :
when he has p finished half-a-dozen he is begin-
ning to be known, and “his name” sells his
product.
Provided that his books have selling quality—
that they suit purchasers. Here is Pemberton’s
second point : “he gives his public what they
want.” \ English fiction is bought mainly by
semi-literate people who want excitement of one
kind or another : Pemberton, with his eye on
his income, occupies himself with no high theories
of Art, takes no trouble about style, but—lie
provides excitement. His stories are generally
“rattling” and readable; and, for momentary
relaxation, they are not to be despised even by
superior readers such as—you, for example * upon
a'niubd, even the Hundred Best Books (as Jerome
suggests) become fatiguing. With a disli of strong
sensation provided, .the aye age reader does not
cavil about artistic sauce.
But Pemberton, in avoiding the Scylla of
writing too well, takes care not to be wrecked on
the Charybdis of writing too badly. He writes
on the average reader’s level, but he expresses
himself a trifle better than the average reader c an
express himself. And his ideas certainly do not
come either to the average reader or to the average
writer. His plot is usually fresh and striking
(improbability, the possibility admitted, does not
matter). Then, having found his sensational plot,
Pemberton stir.-* it up. He does not make the
interest wait on the last step of a climax ; his plot
falls downstairs at every step/or nearly falls and
recovers by a hairbreadth ; and so the story keeps
alive.
The plot of The House Under the Sea seems
bald enough when put in a line or two. In a
little-known group of Pacific islands the land
extending beneath the sea is hollow with crevasses
and caves, says Pemberton. A clever madman
has bottled up the openings and made the caves
habitable. At his command is a gang of despe-
radoes who act the part of wreckers, lure unsus-
picious vessels ashore with a deceitful beacon,
murder the crews, and divide the booty with
their chief. The madman, sane enough in
some ways, has married a Sweet Girl,
and holds her a prisoned witness of his dreadful
deeds. Comes a Noble Sailor; . outwits the
pirates; brings death to the madman; and rescues
the Sweet Girl (for himself). Good sensational
material; and Pemberton improves it witli a
miasmatic vapour that covers the islands and
sends everybody to sleep or death ; by an octopus;
and by other enlivening trimmings.
But the real art of the story, for the sensational
novelist’s audience, comes in the cast of parts.
The Noble Sailor is always popular—and he is
British The book is to sell in U.S.A.—and
the Sweet Girl is American. But the low,
despicable Madman is a Hungarian; and his
villainous, piratical crew are “fat Germans,
sleek Frenchmen, Greeks, niggers’’—without
a true - born Englishman among them. If
Pemberton were a German novelist, he would
write of brutal English ; if he were French, he
might write of dogs of America ; but, as his book
is to sell in Britain and America—and Australia,
he panders to the national bias of his audience.
Every bad man is a “ foreigner ” ; every good
man is “ one of the blood.” Add that the book is
thoroughly “ moral ” (in the conventional sense),
with even a twang of conventional religion ; and
you can now go away and establish our initial
hypothesis, returning for congratulations when-
ever you like.
♦
Here, m the tribe of recent novels, comes one
really worth paying half-a-crown for (and the ex-
penditure of half-a-crown cannot lightly be re-
recommended these-times). It is The Sentimental
Warrior, by Edgar Jepson (Grant Richards)—
written well and cleverly. A baronet makes
an early marriage which, later, he deems a
blunder, and so consigns his son to obscurity.
But the son has his father’s will-to-power, and
presently he attains power—marrying a countess
and compelling paternal recognition. The
value of the book comes in the recital
of the son’s struggle, in the charming
incidental characters and comments, and
in the fresh and refreshing point of view.
Fancy: the hero commits a burglary, justifying
himself by the argument that he is aristocrat and
artist, and needs bourgeois gold to make a
loving-cup for his bride. The burglary is
quite successful; the gold is won; the loving-
cup is made; —and nothing follows ! Not the
ghost of a Nemesis haunts the- hero’s happy
ending: the police have no “clue”; “conscience”
does n’t waggle an admonitory finger; there is
simply a successful burglary, with Jepson’s tacit
endorsement. We do not say whether Jepson’s
attitude is moral or immoral; but it is a new
attitude of the English novel, and interesting.
Some phrases and fragments—
... he learned from them a perpetual distrust of the
ornamental, the folly of trying to pass beyond the limits
of his medium, the worthlessness of work that was not
spontaneous.
. . . pickle-stinking shops. . . [A pretty compound J
pickle is a fine word : the short i pricks and the kl
tingles to reinforce the flavour. Marcel Prbvost refers to
a finite class of the Eternal as “ little pickles of senti-
mentality”—which is just the phrase for that kind of
girl-1
. . . the disgusting soul of the tradesman . . .
. . . that happy balance of faculties, emotions, and
desires which only comes from having dined and drunk
judiciously.
And still we have n’t mentioned Enid, a chuckling
study of the floppy artistic female with the pas-
sionate soul. For Enid—consult Our Advertiser,
the Geo. Robertson Proprietary, Ltd.
We like Morley Roberts’ stories—we like his
philosophy—breezy and manly, not very subtle,
but not smug like Besant’s. And we like his
keen aliveness to Woman and her possibilities,
which are limited and (as Brantdme, who knew,
affirms) increase in proportion to her size, but
(a- Brantdme urges, who knew) are never
altogether to be contemned. Morley docs not
set the ladies at a distance and weave chivalrous
fancies about their imaginary virtues; for, as a
man of experience, he knows the ladies do not
like that kind of thing. Woman has no
objection to being respected, but chiefly she
desires to be hugged; and Roberts hugs
her, in a literary manner of speaking, and
with reasonable attention to Propriety and the
bookstalls of that stern British censor, W. H.
Smith. A modicum of hugging, in phases and
phrases which vary to the same end, is necessary
to the normal novel; and Morley Roberts, being
a warm, stimulating kind of author, whose blood-
corpuscles have not turned, like Mr. Casaubon’s,
to commas and semi-colons, fills his measure
generously.
Immortal Youth, Roberts’ last book, deals in
London studio life, with all the arts struggling for
bread, tobacco, and etceteras. The plot leaves no
decided impression on the mind ; but the charac-
ters do—they are the flesh made words, and they
play their parts absorbingly. The invest-
ment of half-a-crown is recommended if the book
be otherwise unobtainable—and the roving eye
lingers upon—
Ail women bite in their own way, though only some
are venomous.
Whatever game is played, unless it be one of brute
strength, woman seems to hold the ace.
Jock was a fine-seeing soul of a man, for all his wildness
at midnight. It is said he has returned to Scotland. It
is a final proof of success in a Scotchman.
Monogamy, properly understood, means carrying on
but one intrigue at a time. It is mostly confined to
bachelors.
There are women who wish their lovers to lead noble
lives of self-abnegation, mitigated by furtive interviews
in bun-shops.
Woman does n’t like blank cartridges, and though they
may never pull the trigger, they wish to know that there
is danger there.
There is no such infallible sign of a man’s native good-
ness as the follies he commits about women.
It is a poor man who cannot find two women capable of
making themselves unhappy about him.
As the man is, so the woman seems.
It is an odd thing that when a man is a universal
favorite with women he has the fewest real characteristics
of his sex, and is parasitic.
After ail, it is not a mother, but a boy’s first mistress
who is brought to bed of a man.
Eleven citations, and only one about a Scotch-
man- so little oatmeal to so much sealskin sack !
But Morley Roberts does not write pregnant
phrases about the weather and the studio crops.
Cecilia, by F. Marion Crawford (Macmillan).
“ A. story of modern Rome,” depending on the
perpetuation of identity in a man and woman
who loved centuries ago, and love again to-day.
Henley has versified a similar idea— ,
When I was a king in Babylon,
And you were a Christian slave—
and Crawford condenses passages from Nietzsche’s
Zarathustra into the following (it recalls J. F.
Millington’s “The Procession of Egos” in The
Bulletin Story Book) :
Time is infinite. In space there is matter consisting of
elements which, however numerous, are limited in
number, and can therefore only combine in a finite
number of ways. When those possible combinations are
exhausted, they must repeat themselves. And because
time is infinite, they must repeat themselves an infinite
number of times. Therefore, precisely the same combina-
tions have returned always, and will return again and again
forever. Therefore, in the past, every one of us has lived
precisely the same life, in a precisely similar world
an infinite number of times, and will live the same life
over again, to the minutest detail, an infinite number of
times in the future. In the fewest words, this is
Nietzsche’s argument to prove what he calls “The
Eternal Return."
Wo would like Sydney Telegraphs contributor
W.G.P. to tell us if there’s anything in vortex
theories or ether theories that sets a scientific bar
to that idea. Crawford could have found a better
basis for his particular imagination in Weismann’s
germ-plasm hypothesis, providing for the trans-
ference from any ancestor of elements sufficient to
govern a new organism. “Roman society”
writes C., who knows—
is good to look at . . . for mere looks, there is perhaps
nothing better in all Europe, except in England. The
French are more brilliant, no doubt; for their women,
and alas ! their men also, affect a greater variety of dress
and ornament than any other people. German society is
magnificent with military uniforms, Austrians gencrall}
have verv perfect taste; and so on, to each its own
advantage. But the Romani have something of their
own, a beauty most distinctly theirs, a sort of dis-
tinction that is genuine and. unaffected, but which
nevertheless seems to belong to more splendid times
than ours. When the women are beautiful-ana
they often are—they are like the pictures in their
own galleries ; among the men there are heads and faces
that remind one of Leonardo da Vinci, of Csesar
of Lorenzo de Medici; of Guidarello Guidarelli, even or
Michaelangelo. Romans, at their best, have about them
a grave suavity, or a suave gravity, that is a charm m
itself, with a perfect self-possession which is the r 3 r
opposite of arrogance) when they laugh, their mirth is
real, though a little subdued ; when they are grave, they
do not look dull; when they are in deep earnest, they are
not theatrical.
Tlie book is worth scanning.
The Lady of the Barge, by W. W. Jacobs, in-
cludes a dozen short stories, most not nautical,
many clever. Some of the admirable illustrations
by Greiffenhagen are signed “ Greffenhagen ” and
“Grieffenhagen,”—which gives to speculate. A
book to borrow.
Flower-o’-the Com, by S. R. Crockett; By
Dulvercombe Water, by Harold Vallings (Mac-
millan). Two tedious quasi- “historical romances. ”
Publishers nowadays think rapier and cavalier
cloak a sufficient disguise for essential and circum-
stantial dullness ; and apparently ladies “at all
the libraries ” agree with them.
Jim Twelves, by W. F. Shannon (Methuen).
Uninteresting pipeclayed yarns of men-cf-wars-
men, for men-o’-warsmen.
The Twickenham Peerage, by Richard Marsh
(Methuen). Marsh is an uninspired Wilkie
Collins, with a gift of plodding recital and a taste
for mystery. Occasionally, as in the first half of
The Joss, his solid mechanism develops real
power. The Twickenham Peerage turns on the
resemblance of two Dromios, one the real peer.
The story is readable, but not enthralling.

A writer in The Magazine of Commerce, noting
somebody’s suggestion that Italian should be
the universal business language, wonders how
mai’ket reports would run in the language of La
Vita Nuova, — e.g., this passage from Lond. Stan-
dard's commercial column—
Wheat opened steady and unchanged, and
developed a buoyant tone on firm cables, a bullish
“Price Current Report,” covering of shorts, and
liberal export shipments. The market later declined
on realising, sympathy in corn, and a slack export
demand, and closed ea&y at -\c. to §c. fall. Spot
easy.
But why drag in Italian ? Are not the resources
of “our glorious English tongue ” equal to casting
a romantic glow upon the arid business track ?
It will be a pleasant exercise for bookfellows to
take the passage cited and coax it into correct
sonnet form. Conditions: The octave rhymes
a, b, b, a, a, b, b, a ; the sestet with three rhymes,
line disposition optional. Every statement to be
reproduced accurately ; not necessarily textually.
Permission : To exult with the bull; or to wail
with the bear ; or to write with the equal mind
of the recording angel. Suggestions : Mind the
commas. Observe that the quotation naturally
takes the shape of Watts’s rising and withdrawing
wave; while “Spot easy” supplies a clinch as
good as Blanco White’s
If Light can thus deceive, wherefore not Life ?
Ten shillings will be paid for the best sonnet
(having regard both to adequacy and to poetry)
received by 7th February.

Capt. A. J. Onslow Thompson courteously sets
us right in regard to the spelling- of the historic
name Macarthur. He writes:
The Macarthur family always have spelt, and do still
spell their name “ Macarthur,” not “McArthur”; and
of course, Mrs. Macarthur Onslow spells it in the’ same
way. I notice that the newspapers constantly print it
“McArthur,” although the Macarthur Onslows have
several times corrected it. . . . The original Capt.
John Macarthur did occasionally spell his name
“McArthur.” Mrs. Macarthur Onslow, his grand-
daughter, tells me she has one or two of his hooks with
it spelt so, hut she does not know why it was done.
This settles “Ortho’s” question. If the founder
of the family occasionally spelt McArthur (though
never, as N.S.W. official Historian affirms, in
original documents he has seen), it was doubtless
because the spelling of tlve prefix Me or Mac has
always varied, and varies* still; and because in the
eighteenth and early nineteenth century precision
of spelling gave in any case, and particularly to
men of action, less concern than it gives to-day.
The point is fixed by John Macarthur’s pre-
ponderant usage, and by the usage of his descend-
ants.
Journalists, printers’ readers, compositors
especially linotypers—are asked to note this in the
interest of historical accuracy. The linotype
machine is playing the deuce with orthography,
for the reason that the whole line has to be.cast
afresh in order to make a correction —and in the
pressure of newspaper work there is always an
inclination to let small blunders go, and “save
trouble. But it should be a point of honour with
editors and sub-editors to preserve the integrity
of typography; seeing that many of their readers
naturally assimilate and continue “errors of the
press,” sharpening the accusation that the news-
paper is a disseminator of ignorance.
«. .
Replying to a suggestion (3/1/3) that Australian
business firms should employ Australian printers
and spend their money where it is earned the
representative of Sir Joseph Causton and Sons
an English printing firm, forwards a letter which’
freed from extrinsic matter, reads thus
Why insinuate that duty has not been paid on our
goods? . . . Atprese.it the trend of Australian printing
“ cheapness. Cheap work, cheap inks, and cheap paper
In short, Australian printing generally is not high 1.-rade'
As to the particular pamphlet in question, a very un-
doubted high Australian printing authority tells me that
it is of a very much higher grade of work than anything
of the kind done in Australia asyet. . . . The facts remain
1. That Iloldsworth <& .liacpherson and other large firms
are daily giving Causton’s orders because, after seeing
samples of work done by us for leading English manufac-
turers, as business men they realise it is of a higher grade
than is yet profitably possible in Australia from local
printers, otherwise they would not give us the order and
pay duty. 2. That if fact lis as misleading as vour para-
graph, the local printers must be mad or lazy not to con-
trovert our work by local samples of their own of an
equally high grade.
Rejoinder : There was no insinuation that fluty
had not been paid on the pamphlet referred to.
Yet even now Causton’s representative gives
no specific assurance that "duty was paid.
Further : three Sydney firms, Messrs. Wm.
Brooks & Co., W. C. Penfold & Co.,
John S-ands & Co., have courteously sup-
plied samples of their work for comparison
with the Causton pamphlet. In every technical
point—three-colour block, half-tone blocks, letter-
press, etc. —there are Sydney samples as good as
or better than the London sample. The “ very
undoubted high Australian printing authority ”
(nameless), if his capacity be as represented and
his statement be as represented, can convince
himself of his error by opening his eyes. The
original reference is therefofe justified. For the
rest: Wm. Brooks and Co. write to the point—
. . . Causton’s representative says—“ At present the
trend of Australian printing is cheapness.” I would
rather say—“ At present the trend of Australian require-
ments is cheapness.” Many large firms in Sydney, when
placing orders for printing, make it absolutely a question
of price. The consequence is, they work one printer
against another, and get the price cut down to a limit
wtiich cannot possibly allow for high-class work. They
then say that nothing good can be produced in Sydney,
and when the}’ want anything really high-class they give
the order to an Eng. firm, and pay their price without
question.
There is no doubt that large printing orders, for colour
work particularly, are at present being given to repre-
sentatives of Eng. firms that could just as well be
executed on the spot, provided that Sydney houses were
prepared to treat the printer here as generously as they
treat the Eng. people. As a matter of fact, they place
an order ; pay the price that is asked without cavil; take
what is sent them (which, it must be said, is usually good
work), but do not dream of protesting or finding fault,
even where their own pre-conceived ideas or requirements
have been departed from : whereas if the same work was
done by a local firm, and there was a departure of one
iota (owing, possibly, to the exigencies of the work itself)
from the hard and fast lines laid down by some junior in
their establishment, then the wyrk is ruthlessly thrown:
back on the printer, withou a thought as to the loss to
which he is subjected. This has been our actual ex-
perience. . . .
If Sydney business houses, when requiring anything
really good in the way of printed matter, would treat any
reputable printing firm in Sydney with confidence, and
trust to them to make the charge reasonable in propor-
tion to the character of the work required, then I am
confident that there would be no need to send outside
the State for any printed matter.
Memoranda.
Peat son’s Xmas Xtra, 1902, prints, apparently as new,
an illustrated page of verses—“ Biggs and the Alligator “
—with no signature, no acknowledgment, nothing to say
that they are (except for blunders and ten omitted
verses) J. B. Stephens’ lines “ Universally Respected,”
first published in Australia nearly twenty years ago,
many times reprinted, and included in both editions of
his poetical works.
The World's News (Sydney) advertises “ tinted illustra-
tions which are unequalled in the weekly journalism of
Australia." They are : in fact, we have never seen their
equals anywhere.
Sydney Telegraph recently printed that the sale of
Paterson’s The Man from Snowy River was the largest
yet reached by a book of verges published in Australia.
But A. L. Gordon is still at the head of the list : his
publishers, A. H. Massina and Co., write that over 40,000
copies of his verses have been sold in various forms and
editions.
Quote : The Nile Dam is n’t finished yet. Surely this
is the longest imprecation on record.

An enterprising American firm hag founded a school
for teaching advertising by correspondence. This school
was not formed for merely teaching the rudiments ofi
advertising, but every student is taken through a six
months’ course, and is taught advertisement writing
from its very inception to the finished copy. He is
taught to discriminate in regard to language, the value
of disp'ay and effect, the sending of “copy" to the
printers with the necessary trade instructions, and in
fact everything that an advertiser ought to know.— The
Magazine of Commerce.
Correspondence.

journal for printing stuff too good to be etc.

men’s watches. Cartridges out of the same box won’t
shoot alike. Some go high, some go low and some don’t
go at all. This is not true of
WINCHESTER
.22 Short and .22 Long Cartridges.

Special powder made according to carefully determined and
tested formulas is used in loading Winchester Cartridges

other brand on the market. A trial will convince you.

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Broke!”
U> “ eo ,0 ,he *° W Und-the land .itho.it
fence or gate,
Where the stringent laws of mine and thine are
rubbed clean off the slate ;
Where the small and the long,
The tall and the strong,
Must each carve out his fate.
Let us get from the iron town, with its dust and
glare and heat,
Where the cynical rays of the sun down crowded
pavements beat
On the great and the low,
The rags and the show,
When the poor the purse-proud meet.
What’s in the merit of Birth ?-where the disgrace
of the slum ?
Why is one great upon Earth-the other just
nameless scum—
, bcum on the top that gloats j
j Scum—that rises and floats ?, , ,
(’Ere, give me my bloomin’ drum !) '
Little on earth can we claim—then why wear our
flesh to the bone ?
When the swag’s rolled up, and we step out, we’ve
everything on we own.
This 1 work-for-food-for-yer-work ”
(Where germs and microbes lurk)
Makes the bread we eat—a stone !
We can see the finger of God in the Bushland’s
green and brown,
In the wind and the rain and the storm, in the
Thunder-king’s fierce frown :
We worship when alone
The twig—the leaf—the stona
(There ain’t no God in the town).
Then hey ! for the High Hill Land where the soul
of man is free,
Where the bold, keen hawk goes circling up as far
as eye can see
High o’er the sun-tipped crag . , ,
(Give me my bloomin’ swag !
And ho ! for Liberty !)
M.U
J. McLAUCHLAN.
The Timber-getters.
Gray dawn, and the birds are stringing
To the East and the West a-wheel j
Sun-rise, and the scrub’s a-ringing
To the clashing of wood and steel;
And hark ! to the cross-cut ripping,
And the chips, as they bum and curl ;
And see, from the white teeth, gripping,
The pine-dust scatter and whirl !
‘ Stand clear! ” —and aloft she’s heeling,
And volleys of splinters flash ;
While over, the skies are reeling,
And below her the branches crash.
She strikes, and the far hills thunder,
And fiercely her back-winds blow ;
The quiver of earth-shock under—
And the “ King of the Scrub ” lies low 1
Spell-oh ! ” for the sweat’s a-streaming,
And the glare of the hot sun lies
Where the stifling vapors, teeming,
With the reek of the dead leaves rise.
Then toil, till the dusk is falling
And the dark shades lengthen and loom,
And the sound of the wonga’s railing
Rings faint from the forest gloom.
Bright stars in the skies a-shining,
The whisper of leaves in the trees,
Tired limbs on the sward reclining,
And the kiss of the cool night breeze ;
The chant of the weird scrub dirges
That peal from the darkness deep—
The drone, as the soft wind surges—
Night reigns, and the bush-lands sleep !
A. CAR. B.
The Bashland’s Songs.
Dear to me the wild commotion of my Mother
Bushland’s breeze
As the droning of the ocean is to singers by the
seas;
And now rising, voice a-thunder, and now sobbing
soft and low,
It wreathes my way with wonder and the dearest
songs I know.
Ah ! I sadden for its dirges droned in unavailing
sighs,
But I gladden as it surges through my native
Paradise ;
And enraptured here I listen as its raving tem-
pests roar
In the skies where cloudlets glisten, and where
wide-winged reamers soar.
When the star-loved Queen of Shadows shuts the
daylight from her throne,
From her Muse’s Eldorados come her songs for
me alone;
And although I may discover that her moods are
strange and wild,
She is kind to me, her lover—yet her critic, and
her child.
And the river sings her greeting (where the moon-
beams kiss the bars),
With her whisp’iings of a meeting with a roadway
’neath the stars;
And, where silent oaks are slender,' sob the dream-
less waters deep—
Oh ! the Bushland’s songs are tender as she cradles
me to sleep.
And the listening seas applaud her as the twinkling
stars, ashine,
With their million lights reward her in this music-
hall of mine . . .
Hark! now rising, voice a-thunder, and now
singing, soft and low,
She breaks my cares asunder, and I gladden as I
go.
N.S.W.
GILROONEY.

Eight Years' Dyspepsia.
A LONG PERIOD OF SUFFERING.
A WELCOME FRIEND IN TIME OF NEED.
The Case of W|rs. S. BRO HH
(by our reporter.)
If you are imbued with any desire to gein
an insight into the methods adopted by
people whose efforts ftre attended with suc-
cess, you cannot do better than read the
following narrative* which was related by
Mrs. Selina Brown, of No. 5 Little Ed ward-
street, Pyrmont.
“ One day,” said Mrs. Brown, “ as I was
talking to a young girl at the front door, a
Strange and overpowering sensation came
Over me, which caused me to have a fit, and
I was taken indoors and put to bed, where
I was lying unconscious for three days. A
doctor was called in, but he said that he
could not account for the sudden turn I
had taken. He prescribed some medicine,
which did not have any beneficial effect,
as three weeks later I took another fit in my
sleep, after which I was so feeble that I had
to keep to my bed for several days, and
again the doctor was brought to my bed-
side.”
► “ Previous to taking the fits were you in
£ood health ? ” asked the reporter.
“ I was not at all well, as you may gather
" from the fact that for eight years I suffered
from indigestion,” answered Mrs. Brown.
“ For a long time I seemed as if I had no
use in my body to work at all. I felt
languid an 1 depressed in spirits, and did
not care whether I lived or died ; neither
could I sleep well. It was a perfect misery
for me to go to bed, a? my mind was so un-
settled that I felt as if I wanted to walk
about all night. Just as I thought I was
falling asleep 1 commenced to think about
some troublesome subject which generally
occupied iny mind till morning, and when I
* got up I was too exhausted to do anything,
and with a taste in my mouth that made
[ everytl 'ug seem bitter. The only sleep I
got Vi,, a ways accompanied by dreadful
dreams, so that I never got any proper rest.
'The sense of hunger was a stranger to me
entirely, but in spite of that I took a little
food occasionally, or else I should have
starved.”
“ Did the food do you any good ? ”
“I am sure it did not do me much good,
ayy, for I was continually getting weaker
and thinner, and the worst of it was that
eating always gave me such awful pains in
the stomach, where windy spasms made-me/
feel as if I wanted to tear my clothes off, no
matter how loose they were. A heaviness
in my chest also troubled me greatly, for as
each breath was drawn a smothering sensa-
tion came over me, and at the same time I
svifibre 1 acutely from pains under my
shoulder-blades and about my back. Every-
thing possible seemed to go against me at
once, as I was often incapacitated from
going about the hou«e on ing to my feet
swelling and my limbs aching, across the
loins especially. Unless I was very careful
and slow in my movements I became so
giddy that I could not stand, while extreme
nervousness was another torture. 1 was
afraid to move in the dark lest something
would happen to me, and if anything gave
me the least excitement my nerves shook to
such an extent that the Vibration of my
limbs was quite visible. Little did I think
.tint something even worse than these ail-
ments was in store for me, but my ex peri
ence goes to show how a person’s constitu-
tion is gradually wrecked, and what fright-
ful results aro likely to follow. After my
sudden seizures I was in a more hopeless
state than ever. My head pains were so
bad that I could not bear anybody to speak
to me, and any food that I took was always
vomited. The medicines and powders which
were given to me were of no avail, and at
length I crawled out of bed and went to
another doctor, who told me I ought to go
to a hospital. Having three young children
I did not like doing so, and instead of going
tc a hospital I catne home ”
“ Were you wise in going against the
doctor’s advice ? ”
“It turned out to be the best thing I
could have possibly done, as a neighbour
shortly afterwards came in to see me for the
purpose of reading a cure performed by
Clements Tonic. When she had finished
the story she exelaimed : ‘ That is just how
you are. Why don’t you try it?’ I said,
‘I believe I will.’ When my husband
came home he thought I was going
to the hospital, but I said, ‘ I’m not
going. Bring me home a bottle of
Clements Tonic, and I will try it.’ On
my word of honor, I felt a slight im-
provement when I had drank only half a
bottle. Just imagine my delight after the
way I had been disappointed with all other
medicines, especially as I had long since
thought that I was doomed to die, because
I could not get relief. But Clements Tonic
soon eased the terrific pains in my head
(which extended right down my neck), and
when, in a few days, they were gone alto-
gether I could get about a little, for I also
felt more vigorous than I had been for a long
time. The first good night’s rest I had had
for many a month was obtained through the
comforting influences of Clenjents Tonic, and
when this grand experience had been re-
peated several nights in succession I felt
quite new, for I was happy in the belief
that I m'ght yet recover m ' health.C
tissue in my body seemed to be strengthened
by the wonderful medicine I was taking,
and for sharpening my appetite it was just
the thing. Before long I sat down to my
meals like everybody else in the house, and
I could always take a fair share of whatever
was placed on the table. The neighbour*
were intensely surprised to see me up and
about once more, as they had entertained
little hope of ever see ng me well aga n. I
had no trouble in keeping mj T food down,
and every day I found greater relief from
the symptoms of indigest on. The pa ns
finally left my stomach and shoulders, and
I could breathe without gasping as I used
to, for the oppression had disappeared from
my chest, and w th returning strength my
limbs acqured freedom from all aches.
When I was taking my doctor’s medicines I
had no desire for anything, hut Clements
Tonic gave me a 1 ght-hearted inclination to
see everything. By its contnued use I
forgot what nervousness was, as I knew no
fear, and my nerves were as steady as a
rock. I vya,& never a stout woman, jet my
diseases had dragged me down considerably,
but C emeut>* Tonic was my sole medicine
till my lost weight was regained, and, re-
markable as it may seem, that is all I
required, for I was then quite cured of
everything. None of my former ailments
were left behind by Clements Tonic, and I
am only speaking the truth when I say that
iny life was saved by that medicine. If you
like you can publish my story in any way
you deem fit, as itmay alleviate the sufferings
of many more.” “

SATURDAY, JANUARY 17, 1903.
The Explanation of Mr. Owe’Sullivan.
E. W. Owe’Sullivan, the tireless Minister
for Works of the N.S.W. Cabinet, a hard
borrower, an indefatigable letter-writer, and
a frenzied constructor of buildings, parks,
drains and stations, lately expounded his
public works theory in the Sydney dailies.
The following extract is the gist of his mes-
sage to the people, and is practically the
whole condensed gospel of Owe'Sullivan :
Let me, then, ask any fair-minded person what would
have happened to New South Wales if there had not been
a public works policy to employ the thousands of men
thrown out of work ? It is no flight of imagination to say
that, but for our large expenditure upon labor and mate-
rial, New South Wales to-day would be in a serious con-
dition commercially, industrially and financially.
The sum of £7,211,089 has been spent by our Public
Works Department since the 14th of December, 1899 (the
day we took office), till the 31st of December, 1902 ; and
fully five millions of that amount was for wages. These
wages were spent amongst shopkeepers, who, in their
turn, spent the money among merchants. The
lan fiords got their rents paid; the clergy, the
professional classes, the bankers, newspaper proprietors,
hotelkeepers, etc., all touched some of these seven
millions at a time when there was very little employment
for the people, and therefore very little other money in
circulation. Every fair-minded member of anyone of the
classes just mentioned will now, I believe, be inclined to
state that it was the policy of the Lyne and See Adminis-
trations which pulled New South Wales out of her
difficulties and kept her upon an even keel during the
severity of the drought. It is morally certain also that if
the same policy is not pursued for the next two years the
serious storm which we have weathered may again con-
front us. Therefore the policy of the Lyne and See
Administrations, instead of being condemned, should be
approved of by every person whose mind has not been
warped by the unfair criticism of opponents.
Let it lie clearly understood also that the expenditure
upon public works by the Lyne and See Administrations
has been chiefly upon reproductive works, many of which
are already bringing in 3.) and 4 per cent., and even more.
Here is the list of works carried out in little more than
three years : Seventeen railways, costing £1,789,602 ; 29
tramways, costing £457,606 ; 107 harbor and river works,
costing £1,137,658 ; 2061 buildings erected or repaired, at
a cost of £890,972 ; 121 sewerage works, costing £517,247;
342 water conservation works, artesian bores, and public
watering places, at a cost of £307,741 ; 91 roads and
bridges, chargeable to loans, at a cost of £412,032 ; and
11,817 ditto, chargeable to revenue, at a cost of
£1,411,530 ; 12 works for clearing Crown lands, at a cost
of £23,371 ; one pneumatic tube, £4340; 11 telephone
tunnels, £45,453; two flood channels, £BO3 ; 49 country
towns and water supplies, £141 ( 992 ; and Sydney water
supply and improvements, five m number, £40,742.
In addition to the foregoing, we are building the hand-
somest railway-station in the world, with splendid
approaches 100 ft. wide, planted on each side with trees,
and with a park in front of the main building.
All these works are irrespective of the works done by
the Water and Sewerage Board, the Sydney Harbor
Trust, and the Railway Commissioners.
It will be Been from what I have said that the works
carried nut hv the aid of this expenditure of seven
millions are ■> i ther of a reproductive or essential char-
acter (such as roads and bridges, the public buildings,
&c.). Railways, tramways, sewerage works, artesian
wells, water supplies for towns and water conservation
works for the country districts, tanks and wells, give a
return from the date of completion—often more than 4
per cent, on the cost of construction. How, then, can
this be called reckless expenditure ? On the contrary, it
gives N.S.W. a number of going concerns, which add
considerably to her revenue, and thus enable her to
avoid the financial difficulties which have beset other
States.
• » * *
Tliere is a quaint, drunken element of
self-deception about the gospel of loan ex-
penditure as preached by the great
Owe’Sullivan. He cheerfully assures the
public, to begin with, that many of his new
works are already bringing in or 4 per
cent., or even more. And he probably
believes it. The old railways of N.S.W.
have been worked at a loss ever since they
were built—the accumulated shortage is now
£8,000,000 or so—and last year they didn’t
yield 3£ per cent., let alone 4. The new
railways are [laying so much worse than the
old ones that the railway revenue for the
half-year just closed is £75,000 less than for
the corresponding half of 1901 —£75,000'
less coming in, and about £75,000 more in-
terest going out. The new railway station—
•the finest one in the world —yields no
revenue at all, and is never likely to ; it
merely replaces out of loans an old work also’
built out of loans. The repairs to buildings
yield nothing. The water conservation
works yield little as yet, and they are
hardly expected to yield much in the way of
direct revenue. Sewerage works earn noth-
ing except in the sense that they are a.
necessary precaution, and that people are
taxed to pay for the use of them. Roads, gaols,
courthouses, asylums, and the great bulk of
the other works yield nothing, however
necessary they are. Telephone tunnels
are necessary, but they don’t add to
the telephone revenue. Dredging works,
river-snagging, the making of parks, etc.,
yield nothing. It may be that the Owe’
Sullivan can point to one or two of his
publ,ic works that yield over 4 per cent. [
this paper will swear that the whole lot
don’t, so far, yield 2 per cent, all round —
probably not 1 per cent. And in addition,
to the fact that the other or 2 or 1\ per cent,
interest on loans lias to be made good out of
taxation, all these new works involve a huge
extra and perpetual expense for repair and
maintenance. Every new road, building,
park, etc., is an additional charge on the
revenue unless it is to be allowed to fall to
pieces again.
* * * *
It is a silly and demoralising theory
to suppose -spthat even railways are
“going concerns” in themselves. They are
only intended to carry the produce of manu-
facturing, agricultural, pastoral and mining
industries, or to carry the people engaged
in them, and all the other varieties of
public works are the same, only more so.
Unless there is an increase of self-supporting
industries to employ the new public works
they are no more going concerns than is a
mountain or a tombstone. They won’t
“go” unless there is something behind to
make them go, and they won’t go to any
profitable extent unless there is sufficient
behind to make them go. They aren’t
eatable, drinkable, wearable or saleable, and
they have no volition or earning capacity
whatsoever of their own. Making railways
is good when there are people and goods to
travel on them, and building houses is good
when there are people enough to live in
them, and making kilts is profitable when
there are Highlanders enough to wear them,
but making any of those for the mere supply
of employment is a very different thing.
* • * *
Mr. Owe’Sullivan claims proudly that he
constructed many million pounds worth of
public works to furnish employment in a
time of scarcity—at least that seems to be
the purport of his remarks. He did it to
keep the country on an even keel. It is a
noble thing to keep the country on an even
keel, but when the thing is carried too far,
and the country is insanely cocked up at one
end it becomes ridiculous. The theory of
the “even keel” is about this: In good
times N.S.W. spends all its ordinary
revenue, all it can get by selling and
leasing the public estate, and two or
three millions of loan money, much of it on
hopelessly unproductive undertakings, as
well. That drunken state of boom is
described as being on an even keel. And
when the earnings of ordinary, self-support-
ing industries fall off, N.S.W. endeavors,
under the Owe’Sullivan’s guidance, to bor-
row so much more that it will be as drunk in
bad times as it was in good ones. If
N.S.W., in reasonably good years, paid for
all its public works out of revenue and regu-
larly }>aid half-a-million off its debts, it
might fairly borrow two or three millions in
very bad years so as to distribute the disas-
ter, just as the person who is sober 364 days
in the year may reasonably get drunk to
drown some specific misfortune—say the
death of his Presbyterian uncle—on the
365th. But to be drunk 200 days in the
year, and drunker on the other 165 is
another thing.
♦ • • •
Every community has bad years. There
are years of drought, flood, war, business
crisis, low prices, pestilence, and other
troubles. There is nothing for it but to pay
for these disasters and repair them in the
best way possible—but the Owe’SuLLIVAN
regards the people of N.S.W. as an especially
blessed or especially pampered race who
shouldn’t pay at any time. It was certainly
the duty of the Government to make a special
effort to find work for that section of the
community which was ruined by the last
especially virulent years of the drought.
'The opportunity was a splendid one.
New South Wales has no cotton fac-
tories, no silk factories, very few woollen
factories, not nearly enough boot factories to
supply its own requirements, and not one
distillery ; it imports over £1,000,000 worth
Annually of clothes ; it buys practically all
its steel rails from abroad ; and in other
ways it imports enormously. Some of these
industries represent work which requires
very little skill, and all of them furnish a
certain amount of unskilled, or compara-
tively unskilled, work. Even bags and
sacks, which represent a miserably common
-variety of industry, are imported by N.S.W.
to the extent of nearly £300,000 a year. A
■Government which hadn’t the borrowing
•corruption in its bones would, when other
industries fell through in a time of extra
•disaster, start State factories for the manu-
facture of anything or everything that
the country requires and which it doesn’t
already supply, or give aids to private
•employers to induce them to do so.
Its workers might not produce at first to
Anything like the value which more skilled
labor could produce, but at least they would
produce something. The State wouldn't re-
quire to find them 6s. or 7s. a day for their
support; they would at least earn a good
part of the money themselves. And some,
at least, of the industries would survive and
become permanent and self-supporting.
• * • •
At such a time a sane Government would
levy extra taxation on those classes which
escaped the ruin of the drought in order to
find means to found new industries, and to
relieve special distress ; and it would practise
•every form of economy in order to find the
money for purely relief works where such
were absolutely necessary. It would seek to
distribute the burden of the disaster, which is
quite different from trying to live even more
luxuriously than when there is no disaster.
N.S.W. has to pay its drought losses some
•day, unless it proposes to swindle its bond-
holders, and it may as well pay them now as
any other time. A large section of the
N.S.W. community suffered severely during
these last dry and bitter years, but the com-
munity, as a whole, if the trade returns
count for anything, bought more foreign
goods, ate more, drank more, and had a more
riotous time than in many previous years
when rain was abundant and a reasonable
state of prosperity was supposed to prevail.
The riot was very unevenly distributed, but
it was there. And the theory that a State
which is drunk all the time should be a little
drunker than usual when it is least able to
pay for the liquor, is unwholesome.
• • • •
New South Wales has already far more
public works than its population justifies,
and far more population than is justified by
the number and extent of its self-supporting
industries. And in the silly and primitive
lexicon of Owe’Sullivan the remedy for
everything is more public works—more rail-
ways and roads when there is less to carry
on them, more telegraphs when there is less
business to telegraph about, more debt
when there is less means of paying it, and
more interest to pay out of smaller resources.
The specially bad times which call for an
extra large addition to the debt are always
coming ; the specially good times which
justify a partial repayment of the debt, or
the moderately good times which justify the
State in leaving its debt stationary, never
come. And it requires very little to create
the situation which calls for a special bor-
rowing effort. In 1901 the produce of all
N.S.W. industries was £375,000 more than
in 1899, and the loan expenditure, which
was high enough in ’99, was promptly
increased by £716,475 to keep the State on
an “even keel.” In 1902 the production
fell off instead of increasing, though the
extent of the falling off isn’t known yet, so
the loan expenditure was increased again by
another t‘2,157,264 to keep the State on the
same old keel. When things are improving
loan expenditure increases to make them still
better ; when they are deteriorating it in-
creases some more to prevent them getting
worse. That is OwE’SuLLiVAN-ism put a
sentence.
The Campaign against Trades-Unionism.
Trades-unionism, when closely examined, can
hardly be called a principle—certainly not an
ethical principle. It is rather an expedient, in some
respects a clumsy expedient, for securing relief
from certain very well-defined evils, by methods
generally, vaguely defined and not always admir-
able-considered as means. But, like some other
expedients of civilisation, its preservation, for the
present, is vitally necessary ; it may be only des-
troyed (whilst industrial conditions are as they
are) at the price of grave suffering to that class of
the community which already carries more than a
fair share of the burden of society, or of an out-
break of revolutionary violence—probably the
latter. Yet, in their blindness, the employing
classes in Australia, < as in England, are
just now engaged in a legal campaign
against the principle of trades-unionism, and
are jubilant at the success of certain initial
skirmishes which seem to promise them a full
victory. In England what is known as the Taff
\ ale decision, the practical effect of which is to
make a trades-union liable for the individual acts
of its members and to throw upon it the responsi-
bility ,of any damage caused by their actions, is being
pushed home; and, acting on that precedent a
decision has been already sought (and gained)
from a E.S.W. court on the same lines. In alarm,
the workers are seeking an amendment of existing
laws to render null the Taff Vale decision and to
secure defined immunities and privileges for
trades-unions. Action of that sort is suggested
in England and by the N.S.W. Labor party
The situation, in truth, is one full of danger to
the Labor movement in its broadest meaning—
the movement which claims that the Man with the
Hoe has a smaller than just share and the Man
with Capital a larger than just share of the
world’s products. And it is not less dangerous
because the issues at stake being complex, and
the scope for plausible sophistry great, the daily
press, which is, in Australia at any rate, always
on the side of the Man with Capital, has set itself
to gratify its hatred of the Labor movement by
ingeniously cunning misrepresentations. Sydney
Daily Telegraph, for instance, on Dec. 31:
Concerning the platform outlined for the (Political
Labour League) conference, there is in it little that is
new, and this is of a character so nebulous as to almost
preclude practical discussion. There is, for 'instance, a
“ Trades Union Act Amendment Bill to relieve unions of
the disabilities imposed upon them by the Privy Council
decision in the Taff Vale case." What “ disabilities ” ?
All the Taff Vale decision does is to declare that trades
unions have the legal responsibility naturally adhering to
all legally recognised rights. The divorcement of this
right from its concomitant responsibility, if justifiable in
the case of one incorporated body, shou.d be equally so
in all. It will be for the league, therefore, to define the
reach of this new Labor demand, and also to explain
why, if corporations should have liberty to break laws,
individuals should not have the same privilege. A
Trades Union Amendment Act would not repeal the
common law that was broken in the TafT Vale case ; the
most it could do is to license unions to violate it. The
justice and the policy of such a license will require a lot
of explanation before the conscience or the common-sense
of the people consents to its being granted.
* * • *
The effect of this is all falsehood, though some
of the individual statements are true enough. It
is necessary to have some clear idea as to the
growth and purpose of trades-unionism to recog-
rise the ingenious sophistries. Modern trades-
unionism began with modern industrialism—that
vast unsettlement of conditions of employment
which entirely eliminated the personal equa-
tion from the labor problem, cleared away the old
semi-paternal relations between employer and
employed, and set up instead the capitalist and
the “hand.” The new system practically abolished
conscience and personal responsibility, which had
been the two great bulwarks against the oppres-
sion of the man with labor to sell by
the man with money —especially when the
joint stock company system began to have
its vogue. Child and woman labor under re-
volting conditions of hardship, infamous sweating
of the male workers, were natural, almost neces-
sary, results. In those European countries where
conditions most nearly approximated to the Asiatic
level, the workers sank into the seifdom of these
conditions almost without protest. In other
lands, a struggle was made for Liberty. In
France by the great Revolution, in England by a
series of smaller efforts in the sime direction, a
betterment was secured either by slaying the
capitalists or by regulating their greed by Acts of
Parliament. The basis of all these movements
was a union of the workers. The individual wage-
slave was powerless to effectively protest against
the tyranny of a big employer or of a bad system,
but in numbers he could become terrible by build-
ing a barricade or by smashing a factory. In
England—to confine the argument to that coun-
try—the workers formed rough-and-ready trades-
unions, the primary object of which was to break
up machinery, to riot through the streets, or to
overawe Parliament by turbulent gatherings.
• • * •
Thus the beginning of trades-unionism was
illegal ; and, necessarily, as will be shown, its
development since has always been on lines which
were technically outside the law. A strike is, to
all intents and purposes, a conspiracy to damage
an employer or a number of employers. One man
may leave his employment legally ; if a number
of them band together, and, with the avowed
object of injuring an employer who seeks to
reduce their wages, go out on strike to try and
force him to withdraw the reduction, the legal
trespass is obvious. (If a number of employers
band together to lock-out their employees that
should, on the face of it, be criminal con-
spiracy, too, but the law’s net is not for
such big fish, and whilst a strike-conspiracy is
easily provable, a lock-out conspiracy is not.)
What has to be kept in mind is that trades-
unionism was from the first illegal; that the
landlord and capitalistic classes endeavored to
suppress it by the aid of the law ; that in France
they succeeded to the great glory of the guillotine ;
that in England they didn’t succeed, for the ruling
classes had wiser heads on their shoulders, and,
prefering to keep them there, made concessions
to the industrial discontent in the shape of
franchise reform and trades-union legislation.
The latter set at defiance the common law by
making certain sorts of “conspiracy” legal, and
by creating corporations which were and were
not corporations—able to sue on their own
account in some contingencies, but not liable
to be sued. _ The illegal trades-union thus
gained a kind of shield in the shape of
statute law, and of a body of judicial decisions
which were, in a measure, forced from the Judges
by the anxious nature of the times, with the
spirit of Revolution abroad and no landlord’s neck
safe from fracture. They were concessions not
from Justice, but from fear, and, that being so,
were clumsily given. The net result was that
workmen were able to form themselves into legal-
ised combinations and, as such, to set to the
illegal work—when necessity arose—of striking
and boycotting. This was the alternative to
allowing machinery riots and Chartist demonstra-
tions to develope into a movement which would
have brought a whole horde of German prince-
lings and English landlords to the scaff< Id, or to
the almost equally distasteful fate of honest toil.
It was unpleasant to the privileged classes to
make concessions to the workers, but it had to be
done.
* • * •
The position now is that it is sought by judicial
decision to withdraw those concessions, to put
trades-unions in a position where they will be
absolutely powerless to organise strikes and where
-t.bgX.will, therefore, be at the mercy of the em-
ployers. Apart from all sophistry as to legality
and illegality, that is the net result of the Taff
|Vale decision pushed to its logical conclusion.
The judiciary may be expected, if no other
circumstances intervene, to push it to that logical
conclusion. The trades-union, especially the
striking trades-union, can very rarely meet with a
judge whose ideas are sympathetic. The judge is
a professional man, accustomed to find that the
law of supply and demand is a very good law
indeed, because under it his special skill finds
generous monetary recognition; he, as a rule,
simply can’t understand that the great mass
of the workers with no special skill, or with
but a poor atom of it which is shared bv
ma, U thousands of others, must under present
conditions, if not allowed a little more or less
illegal interference with the law of supply and
demand, inevitably sink to serfdom. A honor of
strike tactics can be reasonably expected from
ul° S j '^ uc^Bes > and certain employers, in their
blindness, seem to wish to take full advantage of
the fact to enforce the curious Taff Vale decision.
* • • •
In their blindness ! for trades-unionism is the

The Hopelessness of State Politics.
Sydney Daily Telegraph of Jan. 7th, in a
moment of clear sight, saw of a sudden the hope-
S s q n w P f T he °PP° si t ion party (its party) in
.N.S.W. Parliament, and spoke in bitterness—
These are facts that go to utterly stultify the protests
against Ministerial extravagance now raised by the
Opposition leader and his supporters. They endorse by
their unanimous acquiescence Mr. O’Sullivan’s boom
policy when put before them in concrete, while con-
,rL the * abstract - What is the use of that ?
Juti? 11 1i 18 K? r aS fref l uentl y twitted them with getting
money they could for their own electorates,
and when the duty of discussing the items of works ex-
penditure came before them they justified that taunt by
letting the whole estimate go through with their tongues
eYhlhif!nn ch f 6 f k^f n , Mr - O’Sullivan claims that as an
exhibition of faith in his policy. Whatever it was, it
unfortunately discounts the force of Mr. Carruthers’
present protests against that policy.
Mr. Carruthers has. since displayed the stream-
ing wound which this condemnation inflicted on
him, with the air of a patriotic Caesar stabbed by
a most ignoble Brutus. And it must have been a
sad shock to him when the knife of the
King-street daily entered between his ribs. But,
apart from all question as to the “ingrati-
tude of the D.T. in thus casting down the
leader whom it had just created, and whose policy
it had, up to January 6, emphatically endorsed,
it was a clear, cold truth which that paper uttered
on Jan. 7. The Carruthers party in N.S.W.
politics is hopeless, and does share the worst vices of
the See-Owe Sullivan party (without sharing its
virtues). The Lyne party, which held power so
long by grace of the D.T., was hopelessly incap-
able and extravagant. The See party which fol-
lowed, in spite of thi£-efforts of the same paper
(just then under a serious cloud, politically), was
even more hopelessly inept and reckless. The Lee
party, which was once more or less seriously sug-
gested as a substitute, soon became so palpably
ridiculous that it disappeared, and its solemn
leader is now no more heard of. The
Carruthers party, which is the latest suggested
alternative to the Owe-See-Sullivan gang, is, if
that could be possible, a shade more ridiculous
than the Lee party, because its leader is known
to have snapped with hungry chops at the offer of
a place in the existing Cabinet ; and has shown
repeatedly (more particularly at the conference of
parties in regard to drought relief) that he is in
full sympathy with the dishonest Borrow-or-Bust
policy. The See party is a reckless profligate
which compels, in spite of its sins, a certain reluc-
tant atom .of affection, because of the genial
diablerie with which it goes to the deuce. The
Carruthers party is just as much of a profligate,
but of a sour and secret-drinker type, just
as much resolved on taking the State along
the road to ruin, 'but wishing to do it in
a grim and ugly way. Where then is the hope in
State politics? The Bulletin would like to pin
its faith to the Labor Party—the party which is
above the average in ability and sincerity and has
not committed any grave sins itself, even if it has
acquiesced in many wrong actions. But that is
impossible until the Labor Tarty turns its thoughts
seriously to Financial Reform and becomes an
anti-borrowing party. In N.S.W. Parliament, at
the present time, there is not much to see that is
hopeful. And, outside, the position is hardly
better. The D.T., in its virtuous and clear-sighted
mood of Jan. 7, would have us believe that there is
hope of a reform movement from the outside, en-
gineered from its King-street office. Certainly an
honest, capable, patriotic daily paper could, in six
months, organise a party that would capture
Parliament and reform the State. But the D.T.
is not that paper The last time Mr. J. Randal
Carey’s organ controlled a Cabinet, was in the
days of rogue-elephant Lyne, when everybody in
N.S.W. who was really anybody was swilling free
champagne and going about with the promise of a
fat billet in his memory. On the whole, The
Bulletin does not see much hope in N.S.W.
State politics, or in Victorian, or in South Aus-
tralian, or in Queensland State politics. From
the present point of view, the best hope for the
continent seems to be that the people will one
day get tired of all of them, and ask the Federal
Parliament to do everything that is of any im-
portance in the way of governing. It is a cheer-
ful thought that, however awful the muddle may
become in the States, there is always that way
out.

The Adelaide Tramway Gr~b.
The fact that Adelaide’s private tramway grab
syndicate seems to be in considerable trouble—
that it hasn’t started work, that apparently it has
failed so far to form a company to build the new
electric trams, and that it now wants “more
time ” in which to make arrangements, recalls the

the public when they applied for their concession.

reprints some of the almost forgotten misrepresen-

The promoters—the Westinghouse and Callender Com-

wide reputation, and of first rank in electrical manu-
facture. They had the necessary financial support, and
would commence the work of conversion without delay.
And this—also stated in the Assembly two years
ago :
No practical proposition had been made to the tramway
companies or had been brought before the public for the
conversion of the Adelaide tramways to electric traction

and a valuable concession was gained with the
support and aid of assurances which weren’t true,
and it is time for the parties concerned to explain
for their sake, if they have any
characters.
The Blood of Tommy Tanna.
For the edification of alien-coddlers generally the
following figures are given :—The Kanaka mor-
tality for Bundaberg district runs thus : For the
month of September, 1902, out of 26 male deaths
17 were Kanakas and one a Chinaman. For
the quarter ending Dec., 1902, 71 males died,
of whom 38 were Kanakas and three Chinese.
For the whole year 1902, out of 228 deaths of
males no less than 125 were deaths of Kanakas.
That is to say, over 50 per cent, of the deaths
(of males) in the district were deaths of Poly-
nesians, although the white male population is
between three and four times as great as the male
Kanaka population. Hitherto the decimation of
the Kanaka population has been the mainspring
of prosperity to the Q. sugar industry, and the sky-
pilots who have preached in favor of the continu-
ance of the Blackbird traffic on account of the
facilities it afforded for Christianising the “ boys,”
could usually calculate with certainty that the
Kanaka’s religious conversion would soon be fol-
lowed by a “ translation ” to that sphere for
which the conversion was supposed to fit him.
Few people (especially in the southern States)
realise at what a gruesome cost in flesh and blood
the Q. sugar planter built up his glorious industry.

Labor and Federalism.
The complaint of the Lithgow (N.S.W.) iron-
workers that their employer, after receiving some
benefit from the new Federal tariff, attempted to
cut down the wages of the hands, and, failing
that, shut down his sheet-iron mills, is as yet ex
parte ; and there may be another side to the story.
But, in any case, it gives a useful hint to Australian
legislators with progressive views. To the
Federal Parliament the Commonwealth has en-
trusted the protection of Australian industries,
and a tariff which—though in many respects
faulty, and in most respects falling short of
what is needed serves to guard the in-
terests of the Australian manufacturer to some
extent, has been passed. The object of that
tariff is not, however, so much the protection of
capital, as of labor. Without a tariff very many
important manufactures could be carried on,
provided that the employees would accept con-
siderably less than the European wage-rate to
make up for lack of skill and the other preliminary
difficulties. Protection is given so that a reason-
ably high Australian wage-rate may be paid ; and
the object of Protection is defeated if that high
rate does not rule. And since human nature is
human nature, and the incidence of Protection
in his calling will not change a greedy capitalist
into a reasonable one, it is the duty of the
Australian Parliament, coincidently with the
granting of Protection, to insist on a fair
wage-rate. That is a duty which the Aus-
tralian Parliament is willing enough, probably,
to undertake, but for which at present it lacks the
authority. The Constitution only gives it a per-
missive right to undertake the regulation of labor
conditions, if the States are willing. It should be,
therefore, one of the first “ planks ” in the plat-
form of every reform party in a State Parliament
that full control of industrial legislation for the
Commonwealth should be vested in the Australian
Legislature. Then the power that holds the
shield over an industry can insist at the same
time that there shall be no “sweating” beneath
that segis. The bargain suggested to the manu-
facturer will be an equitable one: Protec-
tion against foreign cheap labor, provided he
does not seek to make local labor cheap.
The reproach is often cast upon the United
States tariff system that its benefits are all
for the capitalist. The reproach is mostly a
lie, for the American workman, as a result of the
M'Kinley tariff, is the highest-paid and the most
prosperous in the world. But it has a flavoring
of truth inasmuch as the American worker,
whilst getting high wages, does not always get as
nigh as he should. In Australia, with its uniquely
i? m ? Cra tio system of Federal Government, there
should be no reason for any injustice in that
ar . < f~P rov ided that the Federal Parliament has
the right to pass wage-regulation and industrial
arbitration laws, the workman can be guaranteed
the full benefit of the Protection which is designed
for him. The essential first step to that end is
the transfer of industrial legislation to the
Commonwealth from the States.

The South-East corner of Cornstalk
Land.
If the proposal to make a Federal capital at
Jtsombala is carried out, N.S.W. should suddenly
discover that it has a much-neglected but most
valuable asset down that way. Even supposing
that 5000 square miles of the district are transferred
to the Australian Government for a Federal
capital and a Federal territory, there would be
enough valuable and suddenly-discovered property
left to make N.S.W. sit up in surprise. The
south-east corner may be said to consist of nine
counties—Auckland, Dampier, Beresford, Welles-
ley, Wallace, Selwyn, Buccleuch, Cowley and
Murray. They are mostly table-land, with a cool
climate, a respectable rainfall, a considerable pro-
portion of reasonably good soil, and most of
very f ew permanently-running rivers of
N.S.W. Their small wheat area averaged 12
bushels to the acre in 1902. And this south-east
corner of over 15,000 square miles, averages less
than three persons per square mile. (One county
of 1158 square miles has one square mile under
cultivation, and 71 people engaged in agriculture
—the whole population is 792). This is the region
through which theGoulburn-Cooma railway runs—
tra r n] W,l f which, for want of population, loses
N.S.W. £42,000 a year. It is a region of big
estates and sheep—not nearly so many sheep as it
might carry if the land were cut up into small
holdings, but sheep none the less. It is a region
where it is possible to follow a well and ex-
pensively-made road for 55 miles and pass only
one hamlet on the way—a hamlet not large enough
even to support an hotel. It is dotted all over with
the abandoned houses of the dummy selector who
is gone—to the Benevolent Asylum in his old age.
A graduated land-tax (not like George Reid’s)
that would smite the biggest land-holders to the
extent of 6d. in the £ on rental value, and make
the holding of very large areas impossible, might
break up this favored district into comparatively
small holdings, and settle enough population on
it to make the Goulburn-Cooma railway pay—a
population that could afford, among other things,
to keep its own roads in repair. The existence of
this south-east corner in its present state, and the
existence of an almost similar, and almost equally
lost north-east corner, is only another evidence
that N.S.W., having discovered the West and
found it Sheol, requires to come back on its tracks
and re-discover the East again. The drawback
under which both the north-east and the south-
east suffer is that their trade doesn’t tend to come
to Sydney. They have ports of their own. No
place whose trade doesn’t tend to come to Sydney
is of any real account in N.S.W. If railways are
constructed westward to all manner of placeson the
rim of the Bottomless Pit, the trade may not pay
for axle grease, but what there is of it comes to
Sydney, and that is the first essential. There
is room on Monaro, and some kind of a living even
in the worst seasons, for all the perished agricul-
turists of the west, but the big estates, and
George Reid’s mechanical ld.-in-the-£ land-tax,
which doesn’t break them up, and the desire to
bring everything to Sydney, are in the way. Even
the Federal capital, if it can be contrived, is to be
on the western line, where all its trade will come
to Sydney. But if, by any chance, that capital
locates itself at Bombala, N.S.W. will suddenly
find a most valuable and almost forgotten asset
thereabouts—one that will be worth a vast amount
more than even a new £4,000,000 loan. It doesn’t
W'ant to be rushed at open-mouthed by any wild
Owe’Sullivan and streaked over with new rail-
ways—not yet, at all events. The first essential
is, by a vigorous cutting up of the big estates, to
make room for enough population to make the
present railway pay, and then start a process of
extension.
Be Clause 114.
A State shall not . . . impose any tax on property of
any kind belonging to the Commonwealth ; nor shall the
Commonwealth impose any tax on property of any kind
belonging to a State.— Australian Constitution, Clause
UU.
The above clause is being used as one more
weapon in the miserable, vicious and grudging
campaign of pin-pricks which State politicians
and the parochial-minded State press is carrying
on against the Australian Government. The in-
tention of the clause is reasonably obvious. The
Australian Parliament may not levy taxation on
the public lands or public buildings of a State, or
on furniture in its public buildings, or on its “pro-
perty” generally. “Property,” in the ordinary,
everyday sense, is something quite different from
“goods ; ” a man alludes to his house as his pro-
perty, but he doesn’t habitually call the bacon he
eats at breakfast property. Nevertheless, two
State Governments—those of N.S.W. and Queens-
land—have devised the idea that all goods im-
ported by a State Government should come in free
of customs taxation under this clause, and the matter
is to be tried in the law courts. If the State
Governments carry their point, they gain very
little but the satisfaction of having been sour,
mean, cranky and disagreeable to the only Legis-
lature in Australia which, by reason of its
splendidly liberal franchise, absolutely represents
the people ; and they set up a position by which
they may lose enormously. Supposing they do
succeed in having it laid down that all goods which,
at the date of importation, are State property, must
come in free, then a time may arrive when a Free-
trade State Government will set up business as a
general importer, and almost everything, including
beer and whisky, will thus come in without
paying duty, the State re-selling to local
merchants. This would be none too good
for John See’s produce business, and would
bring about disastrous results to the revenue.
It would give the Freetrade party a chance to
raise the fiscal issue again in State politics, and
to kick out the See crowd on that issue. It would
naturally knock inter-State Freetrade endways,
for the goods which, being imported by the
Government, paid no duty in the State where
they were imported, couldn’t be allowed to
pass into the other States. Obviously there
would have to be an amendment of the
Constitution —an amendment in which the
Australian Government appealed to the people
against two unpatriotic and meanly quarrelsome
State Premiers. Or, if there was no amendment
of the Constitution, the new system, by opening
the way for a great loss of customs and
excise revenue, would only hasten the time
the Australian Government would have to levy a
land-tax and income-tax of its own. The fact that
such a silly and dangerous question should be
raised at all only emphasises the fact that most of
the best men have gone into the Australian Par-
liament, and left most of the political scum behind
in the State Legislatures.
♦
A Drapery Prohibition League.
Thk Bulletin hears from Maoriland of the forma-
tion there of a Women’s Dress Prohibition League,
with in one sense allied, in another sense rival,
organisations aiming at Local Option in regard to
women’s dress, and to Temperance in women’s
dress. The Prohibition League, which finds it
difficult at times to conceal its scorn of the half-
hearted and make-shift methods of the two other
organisations, aims at the absolute prohibition of
all clothing by females : the Local Option League
would leave the question as to whether a woman
should be clothed or not to her husband, her
father, or, failing them, to the nearest J.P. or
other competent local authority ; the Women’s
Temperance Dress .League aimi only at
curbing excessive indulgence in drapery, millinery,
and other articles of adornment by which women’s
minds are intoxicated, happy homes ruined, vice
encouraged and social misery fostered. The abso-
lute prohibition league has so far secured the bulk
of public support. Its existence is due to a
recognition, the more vivid because somewhat
tardy, of the awful evils arising from the drapery
traffic. Mr. John Snorks was its founder.
Married to a lady who had borne to him three
children, whom he was bringing up under circum-
stances of the strictest piety and the most rigorous
godliness, he was obliged to combat in his wife,
almost from the inception of his married life, a
craving for drapery. She would spend whole
days in the pursuit of dress materials, and her
nights were often lost to the charm of the
domestic circle whilst she trimmed, altered,
or fashioned a bonnet. Finally, one day, intoxi-
cated with drapery, mad in her craving for an
expensive and unattainable boa, she left her home
to become the shameful partner of Henry Joseph,
who satisfied her diseased appetite for drapery.
Mr. Snorks, thereupon, filled with wrath at the
havoc wrought in happy homes by over-indulgence
in fashionable drapery started a campaign as an
“emporium-smasher.’' The windows of several
establishments flaunting their alluring temptations
to vice in the face of women-folk, he broke with an
axe and sent to the gutter the deadly frills, furbe-
lows, laces, gauzes, trimmings, feathers, silks,
satins, shoes and other devices of the Devil. He
was subsequently elected first President of the
Dress Prohibition League, the membership of
which now numbers many thousands, which has
appointed several paid lecturers, and which will
run its own candidates for Parliament at next
election. In its declaration of principles, the
League points out that it is of no use to tinker
with an evil which can be proved by statistics to
have caused three-fourths of the divorce-cases in
the State, which was mainly responsible for 72
per cent, of the embezzlements committed by
men, and of 49 per cent, of the bankruptcies.
Regulation of the dress traffic was of no use :
female dress would have to be absolutely pro-
hibited, and its manufacture or sale, except for
medical purposes, made a penal offence. This
was the only way of putting an end to a trade
which was filling the gaols and covering the land
with misery and shame. The drapery trade is
organising a strong defence movement, and in a
manifesto to the voters urges the terrible loss that
the national revenue would suffer if all drapery
importations were to cease, and the unreasonable-
ness of prohibiting all women from dressing
because a small section abused the use of that
drapery which to the majority was a source of
reasonable comfort and innocent enjoyment. The
“Drapery Issue ” is likely to have a very material
effect on the results of the next general elections.
♦
The recent remark of a U.S. statesmen that it is
necessary to build 50 new warships so that Uncle
Sam will be ready for the inevitable war with
Germany, suggests a multitude of things. The
U.S. is getting tangled in so many foreign com
plications that it must needs build or buy a big
navy very soon, and when the present ill-feeling
against Germany is past the navy will still be
there and all ready to whip somebody. And the
possession of a navy brings with it the desire to
whip somebody. The Nineteenth Century was a
busy time ; the Twentieth is likely to be quite as
busy. The Nineteenth Century saw France rise
to be the greatest military power in the world and
fall again. It saw Britain rise to be by
far the greatest manufacturing power in the
world, and fall back again. It saw the rise of
three empires—Russia, Germany and Japan—
which Western Europe hardly regarded as a row
of pins in the year 1800. It saw the making of
Italy, and the formation of 16 American republics,
and the beginning and end (if it is really the end)
of two African republics. That was a good deal
for one century. Now, if the United States starts
to build a really dangerous fleet it means that
there is another bull in the ring—and the bulls
that were there before were enough to make a
very considerable commotion without further
assistance.
