One recent day both the Sydney morning papers
broke out bitterly in their leading columns (on the
occasion of the appointment of a board to select
and recommend to Parliament a Federal Capital
site) about the fact that one whole session of the
Australian Parliament had passed without a
capital being chosen. George Reid denounces
the Australian Government on the same ground
in every speech he makes; so do all his following
of hangers on, dry dogs and political pimps. The
Australian Star is sore about it, and the Evun’
Noose is full of bitterness. Yet, by the same
process of reasoning, what an unspeakable gang of
imbeciles must be these same N.S.W. papers
and politicians, seeing that they tried,
jointly and severally, for thirty years to
decide where to build the new Sydney railway
station, and ended by leaving it in the old
out-of-the-way corner ! Reid himself pondered
over the question during the five years of his
Premiership, and didn’t even decide to leave the
station where it was—he decided nothing. The
Americans are supposed to be a tolerably swift
race, but it took them ten years from the close of
the War of Independence to decide where to put
their capital. It took them seven years even to
constitute a Federal Supreme Court, another
matter which the Australian Government i'r
greatly blamed for not fixing up in the first 18
months of its existence. The Australian Govern-
ment has been, on the whole, ten times as prompt
and business-like as any N.S.W. State Govern-
ment ever was ; the complaint levelled against it
by the incapable and sluggish N.S.W. State
politicians is that it should have been 50 times as
rapid as they ever were themselves.

♦
“ Paul Jones,” as revived at Sydney
Palace by the Royal Comic Opera Co., is the best
performance of that attractive musical trifle
within the memory of most theatre-goers. Miss
Florence Young, in the name part, is something
more than successful—her fresh, vigorous but
somewhat hard voice fitting the masculine char-
acter to perfection, while her stature —not calling
for anything more in height or anything less in
breadth —is equally well suited to the idea of
Paul. George Lauri, as Bouillabaisse, is quietly
humorous when that is called for, broadly far-
cical when that is legitimate, and very much
more effective always than in some of those
Jv'.rts where, the author not having provided the
nuX'or, the comedian has been compelled to try to
be funny on his own account. Miss Maud
Chetwynd, in the soubrette part of Chopinette, is
dainty and charming. In those three respects the
production is excellent, and there is no failure in
other points. Miss Carrie Moore, Miss Ghiloni
and Messrs. Lear (a most humorous “ Insect ”),
Bathurst and Ward come up to all reasonable
expectations. The ballets, too, are more than
ordinarily attractive.
* » * *
“ Paul Jones ” is of the old-fashioned type
of comic opera, and relies to a great extent on its
melody. There is some genuine comedy-—a good
deal of it, in fact —but in that respect it is not to
be compared with the Gilbert and Sullivan series,
or with such operas as “Mascotte,” “Nitouche,”
or “ Tiie Rose of Persia.” An ideal representa-
tion of it would not take liberties with the music
or seek by too much “gagging” to add to the
humor. And on these points the Palace repre-
sentation is open to criticism. Full scope is not
always given to the charm of the music, and there
are some silly and anachronistic interpellations
about “the hatters,” and so on, which might very
well be dispensed with ; they are foolish without
being funny. George Lauri has ample chance of
showing originality and capacity for broad
comedy as the King of the Mosquito Coast in the
third act; and he takes full advantage thereof.
It is a pity that he should seek to “pad out” his
lines in other acts with what is at once unneces-
sary and absurd. But, apart from all drawbacks,
this revival of “Paul Jones” is strikingly suc-
cessful.

Next Saturday, when the dozen Thieves
have ceased from thieving at Sydney Royal, the
“Fortune Teller” will be staged at "that popular
establishment. The advertisement describes it as
a comic opera, not a musical comedy, which is a
glad thing to hear. In these bald and autumnal
days of the musical comedy, when it has lost any
sense it ever possessed, and its hairless front
gibbers at the public across the footlights, and its
shoulders are weighed down by the airy joke that
runstoabouthalf-a-ton, thesaid musical comedy has
become a very sprry thing. Its jokes, when it has
any, suggest Old Dad Kruger trying to cast him-
self lightly into the air and let loose his
animal spirits, and its airy witticism ought
to be lifted with a steam crane. A genuine
comic opera, if the “ Fortune Teller ” is one, will
be a pleasant relief, and theadvt. takes its immor-
tal oath that the “F.T.” is the real article.
Madame Slapoffski and Lempriere Pringle are to
be in the east, and present writer loves Madame
Slap in her stage capacity. A “brilliant, cohesive
story' 1 is promised, likewise “beautiful, melo-
dious music,” and Hungarian gipsy airs, and
when all these things are added up, and Madame
Slap is included, the outlook seems promising.

“The Drought” breaks nightly at the
Lyceum, and Sydney, viewed from a high-
class immoral standpoint, continues to make
a favorable impression. Adventuress Harrie
Ireland’s . retje.aio.is on the fact that she
will one day be old, and that her life
of sin will not prevent her from becoming
weary and rheumatic and undesirable, might be
more logically left out, because virtuous people
are equally liable to all these drawbacks ; and very
often become a great deal more rheumatic
than the bad people, because gaol is a dry
place, whatever its disadvantages. Even the
best aged ladijs often get neglected, and
don’t receive invitations to champagne suppers
and similar sprees. They can’t even remember
the sprees they used to have, which is a rather
serious argument against steady conduct. If a
good heroine cursed her virtue because it wouldn’t
prevent her growing old and requiring hair-dye
the clergy would probably rise in revolt ; but it
would be ju-st as sensible as H; irrie Ireland’s
remarks.

Sheridan’s pantomime shines in its usual
brightly imbecile manner at Sydney Criterion,
and, 'by strange mesmeric means, lias captured
the affections of numerous sane citizens. There
are no great spots of brightness in “Cinderella,”
but, on the other hand, there is nothing especially
sad. It is an affable piece of foolishness, but
would ba better if the glittering show slipper that
is carried round in Act I. were not quite so unlike
the veiy ordinary white one that Cinderella dons
later on. It is probibly too early in the year to
urge S leridan to find some newer story on which
to build next year’s panto., yet it might as well
be done now as any other time. A very good
panto., by the way, might be built round George
Washington, especially if the cherry-tree that he
cut down with his little axe in boyhood fell on
George 111.

In a few days the Fisk Singers will cease
their murky melody at Sydney Centenary Hall
and take th3ir quaint, lilting hymn tunes to other
parts. When they are gone The Bulletin intends
to catch itself, and bail itself up in a corner, and
ask itself firmly to formulate a theory as to why a
hymn sung by a “cullud pusson ” often has a
charm about it that no hymn sung by a white
person ever possesses. Possibly it is that
the Fisk Singer and his kind sing the hymns
as if they really believed the sentiments con-
veyed therein, while the white person sings
them as if he were trying to get some value for
the 3d. he put in the dish. Possibly it is because
our religion came from a brown man’s land, and is
a brown man’s creed which only a brown citizen
really understands. Anyhow, a citizen who is
strenuous for a White Australia can go and hear
the despised nigger sing a very crude hymn that the
hearer doesn’t care 2d. about, and yet not regret
the shilling he paid for the privilege—which is
curious.

A bright and varied programme at Sydney
Tivoli is now provided by the Schenk Bros.,
Miss Gracie Emmet’s co., Mr. Fred Poplar
(character comedian), Miss Kitty Wager (seu-
brette)—the last two reappearing after several
years’ absence, the Le Monts with their perform-
ing dogs, and Miss Jennie Opie, whose popu-
larity as a ballad singer has lasted through a very
long season at this theatre. There are some
absent items, on the retiring of which the manage-
ment is to be congratulated —that of the man with
the sodden soda-water joke in particular.
** * *
Three of the Melbourne theatres continue
to present their Xmas bills, but one of them—the
Royal—will make a change on Saturday. “The
Power and the Glory ” must now pass away, in
the blessed hope of a joyful resurrection next year,
or later. Melbourne could have better spared a
less gibberingly lunatic melodrama. “ The P. and
G.” goes in for “situations” and gets ’em as it
pleases, leaving nothing to be desired at the finish
except the news that a huge mass of transformation
scene has fallen on the pair of funny men and
crushed them into sausages for the clown.
Nothing else suggests itself in the way of de-
cided improvement. The question as to whether
the angel kid in the Eternal Sky ought to sing a
sentimental song and kill the villain with its
little topnote is debateable. Perhaps the
silent effect is more impressive. If the glorified
infant were to warble the latest two-shilling ditty
from Allan’s or Glen’s, the Kingdom of Heaven
would seem like unto an advertisement, and
although it actually is a business ad. for the
church, a lot of people don’t want the fact made
too apparent. “When London Sleeps” is the
next thing that’s going to happen at the Royal.
It has happened before, and is technically known
as the telephone-wire drama.
** * *
At the Princess’s the candid sinfulness of
an elegantly-furnished comedy is doing very well,
all things considered. Miss Nellie Stewart’s
treatment of the name-part in “ A Country
Mouse ” is a little less over-colored than it was.
She has toned it down almost to naturalism in
some places, and probably the picture wouldn't
be more acceptable to the local world at
large if it were modified any further. Other
members of the Musgrove co.—notably Sydney
Stirling, Harcourt Beatty, and Tapping—
have balanced the account by broadening the
humor. It is not a highly polished performance,
but it gets there all the same. The Brough and
Boucicault co. at its best, failed to catch the
popular vote for any guilt-edged comedy of a
highly immoral character, which introduced dukes
and lords, and ignored the existence of a world
outside the uppah circle. The B. and B. touch
was too light—besides, they hadn’t a Nellie
Stewart to draw the multitude. The multitude
comes with a predisposition to catch on to the
humor of “A Country Mouse,” and the humor
sticks out so that the multitude can catch on
easily.
#* * *
On behalf of the panto at Her Majesty’s,
it is only fair and just, likewise generous, to re-
mark that the fitful narrative of “Whittington”
is free from the intrusions of a gnome king, or
other evil spirit. The dark cavern scene, where
gloomy supers take instructions from a depraved
and husky sovereign, has been kindly omitted.
In fact, “Dick Whittington ” is rather
more commendable on account of the things
which don’t occur than for anything else
that can be remembered at time of writing.
All the same, the attendance maintains a good
payable average each week, and the house last
Saturday night seemed to be expressing gratitude
for the gnomes’ absence in a heartfelt manner.
There are matinees on Wedneslays and Saturdays,
when children, at half-price, bring their parents
and guardians, at whole price, to hear them laugh.
The spectacle of two parents and an aunt urging
a cynical Australian boy to commit boisterous
merriment is pathetic in the extreme.

Spry and Monti, this lady Spry being a sister of

tween Ins verses. The new “turn” is another
Amurkan bar-room sketch, beginning with talk
and ending in dance, and the dancing offers
ample apology for any injuries that the rest of
the sketch may inflict. Little Eileen Boyd and
her comparatively huge voice came into the
proceedings on Saturday, likewise Elsie M’Lach-
lan, the Fiower of Chilblane. She isn’t

braw Scawttish name suggests that she must be the

a friendly footing with his audience, whilst the
Harbecks continue to improve on acquaintance
I heir trifling with the hoops is pretty, the lady’s
mild juggling is neat, and she herself 'is both neat
an l pretty. She smiles like a woman whose
Uluuons are not yet all shattered. A beautiful
smile, worth sixpence a time to the soul that
needs a tonic.

At Adelaide Royal, Alfred Dampier has
put up “His Natural Life,” himself gathering in
most of the credit as the parson -tippler-hero.
After which “The Power of Wealth. this is
Marie Corelli adapted to the stage, and shows the
moving adventures of a count, a countess, and an
earthquake which makes the recent local quake
seem very feeble. Maggie Moore, back from
Broken Hill, put in a short season at Port
Adelaide. She is now described as “ Australia s
greatest favourite, and the World’s most accom-
plished actress.” Cinquevalli drew crowded
houses at the Tivoli right up to the end, business
being somewhat helped by a local doctor, who
denounced the cannon-ball feat as dangerous.
The juggler replied that he could throw up an egg
and catch it on a plate without damage ; and he
did so. This was generally accepted as a
satisfactory answer! He has invented a new
trick in Adelaide, turning a man’s waistcoat m-
side-out while being worn, the coat not being
taken off. The Tivoli is now temporarily closed.

The Bank of North Queensland concluded
a merely respectable half-year on 31st December, 1902,
though times are not good just now 7 with Queensland]and,
consequently, with Queensland institutions :

The profits (apart from the £6BO recovered from bad
debts formerly written off) are the smallest since June,
1899, and represent £3 10s. 7d. percent, per annum on the
shareholders’ funds (capital and reserves) with which the
half-year commenced. This isn’t any too good ; in fact
nothing in Queensland is too good just now. Still the
Bank of North Queensland has now the largest reserve
fund in its history (the reserves, just before the suspension
in 1893, were only £4454); and, as the present reserve is
all represented by State securities, there is no doubt of
its reality. Also the addition made to the reserve this
half-year is the largest since December, 1900.
In most respects the Bank of North Queensland
looks solid and prosperous. Deposits (£261,541) show an
increase of over £25,000 as compared with the figures of
six months ago. Cash and liquid securities (including
£25,750 of Government stock) are £108,559, equal
to 34 per cent, of all liabilities to the public. Some
years ago the Bank of North Queensland conceived the
idea of issuing long-dated 44 per cent, debentures,
but the fall in the money rendered the experi-
ment less profitable than wets anticipated, and during the
half-year just closed the last of the debentures have been
repurchased and extinguished. The chief drawback of the
B.N.Q. is that it is a very small institution, and a small
institution can never be managed quite so economically
as a large one. And this isn’t a good time for raising
the money to turn a small institution into a large one.
One consolation for the shareholders is that small bank-
ing institutions have often proved, in Australia, very
much safer than large ones. ’ J

During 1902 it is reckoned that the borrowings
in Australia of the seven Australasian States, the cities of
Sydney and Melbourne, and the Vic. State Land Bank
totalled £5,449,000. When Australia can put up that
amount in a bad year there is surely no real need of
foreign borrowing. If this country would only recover
its sanity, £5,449,000 is a great deal more than it would
want to borrow in any one year. In the last three years
about £16,030,000 of loans have been floated locally so
Australia can lend its own Governments
£0, 000,000 a year as a pretty regular thing.

Concerning the 15 surviving Fire Insurance
Companies of Australasia (there are nominally 16, but
the 16th reallv belongs to an English company, though
outwardly preserving its separate local existence), some
more or less interesting particulars are appended. The
profits in each case represent the amount earned for the
12 months covered bv last published balance-sheet, after
deduction of all bonuses paid or payable to policy-holders.
In many eases the dividends on the capital originally paid
up are enormouslv larger than the amounts here shown,

Some curious facts stick out in this table. The
co. with the largest reserve is the New Zealand Insurance
Co. (Maoriland); second largest reserve, South British
Co. (Maoriland) ; third largest, National Co. (Maoriland).
Largest profits are those of the New Zealand Co. ; with
the South British second ; and the National third- same
three. Largest sum paid in dividend is that paid by the
New Zealand Co., with the South British again second ;
but this time the Victoria (Melbourne) comes third.
Highest rate of dividend is the 27 percent, of the Victoria
(Melbourne), with the South British second, and
the Victoria General (Melbourne) third. (For wast ng
splendid business opportunities in the craze for
big dividends the Melbourne crowd is generally
well up on the front seats). For the biggest addition
made to the reserves out of the year’s earnings the South
British Co. (Maoriland) is first, Colonial Mutual (Mel-
bourne) second, National (Maoriland) third. The two
smallest additions to reserves are those of the Victoria
General (Melbourne) and Australian Alliance (Melbourne).
Maoriland, though it has less than 800,000 population, as
against Victoria’s 1,200,000 and N.S.W.’s 1,360,000, sup-
ports five fire insurance offices, while the two laige
States only own four each. Queensland, S.A., and YY’es-
tralia, with over 1,000,000 people among them, don’t
possess one fire insurance office ; yet little Tasmania, with
far less than 200,000 people, possesses two prosperous in-
stitutions. But the Commonwealth generally is very
much behind in the way' of running its own fire insurance
business. Protection, and a very strong measure of Pro-
tection, too, for the local fire insurance industry, is
urgently wanted as soon as the Australian Parliament can
attend to the matter.

It costs Victoria about £1 ss. per cent, on the
amount of the Old Age Pensions to cover the cost of dis-

£3 6s. Bd. per cent., but the figure is being reduced. In

could do the distribution for 5 per cent., and that the
cost to the State was 20 per cent., the above facts are
worth repeating. Private enterprise is always making
wild statements and benevolent offers like that.

* * • *
Tho Westport N.Z. Harbor Trust lately
arranged with the Austral Otis Company of South
Melbourne to convert one of their Steam Hopper Barges
into a Suction Dredge. This has now been satisfactorily
completed. The Otis Company fitted one of their
Centrifugal Sand Pumps with driving engine on the deck
of the barge with suitable winches to work the suction
pipe and drag. The plant has now been at work for
some weeks and at a late trial by the Board’s Engineers
waq foundto be raising over 180 tons of sand and gravel
per hour and worked equally well in the heavy swell on
the bar or in the river. The result has given the Harbor
Boqrd the utmost satisfaction. The Sand Pump used is
of similar type to that employed in alluvial Gold and Tin
mi Ring, with which the Austral Otis Company has been
long identified.*

Broken Hill: Stirred up by the B.H. Prop’s
activity in developing a chemical process of zinc ex-
traction, the Aust. Metal Co., which works in conjunction
with German zinc and lead producers, will at once start
its new magnetic plant. Magnetic plants have been
running on ’Murkan sulphide fields for some time ; lately
in addition, a zinc smelting plant has been put up at
1 i e 1) 1 o to handle the zinc concentrates, so apparently
these magnetic plants are doing all -
Tenders are being called for Block 10 new mill
The work should be pushed on ; surface cracks
show that the underground movements which threaten
the old mill are still slowly proceeding. Of
broken Hill stocks, nothing slumped last vear like Block
11, which opened at Bs. 2d. and closed at 3s. Id. ; though
Sulphides, which went from 9s. lOd. to 4s. ran
them close. Some others : B.H. Prop., from 365. to 27s
6,1. ; Block 10, 335. to 255. ; British (old), Bs. to ss. 9d. •
Junction, Is. 7d. to 2s. 2d.; North, 9s. 3d. to 7s. 8d •
South (paid), 19s. 6d. to 20s. ; South (con.), 13s. 3d. to’
11s. ; Junction North, lOd. to Is. The improved position
of Junction shares is well justified. Though probably
still unpayable, a very fine body of ore has been
well opened prospects of the Car-
michael-Bradford desulphurising process, which is now
owned by a co., are not dazzling. The success of the
Huntington-Heberlein rival process, adopted by the B.H.
Prop., is a disappointment; and the mission of F. 11.
Snow in Europe and America was not wholly successful.
Still, there is said to be an opening for the process in
Germany, where negotiations are still proceeding.
Apropos the Ashcroft invention for recovering zinc by
electrioity, which the Sulphide Corporation was formed
to work : Ashcroft is still experimenting on a large scale
in England ; John Swinburne, one of the best-known of
English electricians, is now his partner—of course, he
went out of the Sulphide Corporation long ago. The
B.H. mines ate up Hi in llion feet of timber in 1902. as
against 13J million feet in 1901.

« • • •
The Tucker’s Hill field, near Hargraves
(N.S.W.), is being boomed for more than it is worth by
the Sydney press. There are the usual exaggerations
and stories about powerful Sydney syndicates—alway s
1 oiverful in gas —which have acquired claims, and are
going to “ develop them systematically.” Hogan's claim
is said to be the best on the field at present, and a recent
report respecting it illustrates the extent to which things
are magnified. “The reef is from 18in. to 2ft. wide at
the 200 ft. level, and worth from 4 to soz. per ton, accord-
ing to the hulk crushing.” Same report says that at
200 ft, “the reef has been driven on for 300 ft., and the
stone taken out in driving yielded £lOOO worth of gold.”
A section of lode, 300 ft. x 6ft. x 18in., would produce
180 tons of quartz. This section has produced the £lOOO
worth of gold, or less than £6 per ton, yet the report
states, “ according to the bulk crushing, the value is 4 to
50z.” Of such is the average of “our own correspondent.”

The huge grain-shed erected at Darling Harbor
(Sydney) to shelter the State’s surplus wheat while
awaiting the arrival of Vessels, is now coming in handy as
a store for importations of bread-stuffs.

The Queensland amount for 1902 includes the 2s. 6d. div.
declared by Mt. Morgan concurrently with the call, 2s.

up ; allowing for this, there is a difference of £lOOO only
in the divs. for the two years. N.S.W. suffers through
the lessened divs. from Broken Hill mines; included in
its figures are £124,000 from coal mines and £117,000
from silver; gold, and other mines being repre-
sented by the paltry sum of £14,000. It has to be
borne in mind, however, that many of the large profit-
earning mines do not appear in the div. list Great
Cohar, Nymagee, Mitchell’s Creek, and Neeld’s (Wyalong),
for example. Tasmania’s figures for 1902 are affected by
the reduced divs. of Mt. Lyell, resulting from the
big fall in the value of copper. For divs. from
gold mines alone W. A. easily beats all the other States
combined, and is likely to repeat the performance in
1893, notwithstanding that Queensland may reach the
seven figures. The statement generally is eminently
satisfactory. It indicates that the mining industry is in
a sound condition. The total output of minerals in 1902
was nearly equal in value to the year’s production of both
wheat and wool. But the importance of mining as a
factor in the circulation of money is not to be gauged by
comparative sets of figures. In a good season the value
minerals t wou d far exceed-that of metals and

Queen Margaret (W.A.), new holding, the S.
BQnff S i and ! i? OkS u very well - Drives at the 280 ft. and
380 ft. levels have been extended fully 150 ft., and are still
being pushed ahead. A rise is going up from the lower
level to junction with the winze from the upper, so that a
large block of stone will shortly be read}- for sloping. As

obtained. On present prospects a substantial rise in co.’s
scrip may be looked for.
• * * »
Oroya-Brown Hill (W.A.) is strong financially,
a ! pap addltlon to a substantial Cash balance, co. holds
45,000 reserve shares, worth at leasto£loo,ooo. (By
the way, it. would be interesting to know what became
of the 16,000 reserve shares of Oroya, arid who got the
profit on their issue). Many blamed Captain Parkes for
not being able to locate the lode at No. 8 level, but
his successor has proved no more successful.
Although the chute has been found at No. 9 level
quite as rich as No. 7, there is no trace of it at
No. 8. The lease of Hannan’s Star mill has expired
but no diminution in output is anticipated l , as Brown Hill
battery has been extended to meet this emergency. As
co. is earning, say, £12,000 a month profits, even with
costs at the extravagant figure of £4, it will be able to
disburse 7s. per share annually. Costs are likely to be
materially reduced, and output more likely to increase
than decrease, as a large amount of ore is blocked out on
fche Oroya s south block, so shares look like good holding.
No reliable information is obtainable as to the state of the
Brown Hill property, but the Oroya’s two blocks are
good enough for anything.
* • • •
Bellevue Consols, Mt. Sir Samuel (W.A.)-
which has worried two managers into their graves owing
to its refractory ore, is losing the copper at depth, and
the ore is becoming much more easy to treat. Develop-
ments are being pushed in order to have plenty ore ready
for the additional 40-head mill now on order. Some
months must elapse ere this mill gets to work, but, on its
completion, the Bellevue will rank as one of the
big producers of the State. At its reconstruction
a year ago the co. was placed on a strong financial
basis with a working-capital of £70,000.
* * • *
Paddington Consols (W. A.), long under a cloud,
will shortly reappear amonggold-producers. By vigorous
development everything is being done to get the battery
going again. Previous manager rooted out everything,
and left no stone in sight, and, as the mine was closed
down for nearly two years, his successor has had a tough
contract. Main shaft now being sunk to 500 ft., and
driving is being pushed at 400 ft. level. Lode at this
depth very wide, and carries a good deal of visible gold.
Co. helplessly weigited down, being amalgamated
with Whittaker Wright’s litter of cats', known as the
Standard Exploration Co.
* » • *
The alleged forgery of 15,000 Great Fingall
(W.A.) scrip by a London official of the co. will have a far-
reaching result on the mining market. The official,
in addition to his connection with the Great Fingall,
acted in a similar capacity for Hannan’s Brown Hill, E.
Murchison United and several other W.A. co s. As fully
20 W.A. mines are controlled by the firm of which he
was a representative, his act will have a very wide
effect. It is exactly years since an Associated official,
together with a stockbroker’s clerk, got three years’ hard
for forging 2510 Associated shares.
* • * •
Out’ook for year 1903, so far as mining in W.A.
is concerned, is extremely promising, and unless some
unforeseen debacle takes place, a continued advance in
output may be expected. A large number of co’s.,
including Hainault, G. Boulder, Bellevue, Oroya-Brown
Hill, Golden Pole, etc., have been extending their plants,
or intend doing so ; and others, such as N. Kalgurli, are
talking of following suit shortly. As to divs. there
should also he a decided advance—such mines as Ivanhoe,
G. Fingall, Oroya-Brown Hill, Ass. Northern, Kalgurli,
are likely to considerably beat their past records. The
bumper year 1899, when L. V. Consols alone distributed
£625,000, is not likely to be reached again in a hurry, if
ever. A good start has already been made with the kal-
gurli’s £30,000 and the Perseverance’s £87,500.
* ♦ • *
“Glenara”: Mt. William (Vic.) goldfield has
gradually dwindled to a very tired township of from 200
to 300 residents. Mines officer Goodenough reckons
that there are yet some 50,0000 z. to be won from the
field. Certainly he does not explain where, when and
how it is to be got ; 25,0000 z. is the estimated yield to
date. But ’tis not to Mt. William that the old miners pin
their faith. The Hopkins Valley, some 20 miles east-
ward, and stretching away from the old Ararat goldfield
—that is their tip for the future Vic. gold-producer.
“ Deep-sinking, bad ground, but gold when you get
there,” say they. ’Twas here the unlucky Lange-Logan
spent many thousands in a futile attempt to reach
bottom after having got splendid bore prospects. Water
and drift (mostly drift) blocked the way. But some day
(say the ancient under-grounders) someone will get below,
and then a great goldfield will be opened up.
The “public spiritedness” of King Lansell of
Bendigo is acclaimed by the local press. Here is a
sample of his patriotic liberality ! He offers a bonus of
i5OO to any man who will treat 100,000 tons of Bendigo
tailings, or £lOO for 10,000 tons and £2OO for 50,000 tons,
taking the larger amount the wonderful inducement
offered to cyamders and others to tackle the mountains
of worthless sand is Is. for 10 tons ! The same offer has
been made twice before, but has elicited no response.
Bendigo tailings are too poor to be dealt with bv any
knownprocess; their value would hardly cover the cost
of handling. Even if they did contain enough gold to
warrant the erection of extensive cyanide works the
difficulty which would then present itself would be as to
the disposal of the refuse.
* * • .
A Bendigo fossicker has been fined £1 for
sweeping a public road. In olden times it was quite
common to see hundreds of men, women and children
wielding rush-brooms on the roads of the district, but
this was the first instance in which anyone was proceeded
against. The town clerk stated that the corporation had
lately been put to considerable expense to repair roads,
the surface of which had been damaged by fossickers
sweeping for gold. This case recalls a noted event in the
early 60s. The Council called for tenders for cutting
down a, certain road. The estimated value of the job
was about £5OO, and a large number of tenders
were sent in at about that amount, but there was
one tenderer who offered a premium to be allowed to do
the work. He was a well known mining man. There
was no question as to his sanity. His offer was accepted,
and the secret was out when it was discovered that all
the material which formed the old road was being carted
to an adjacent battery. The contractor knew that the
metal had been taken from a mine-heap, and that it was
highly payable quartz ; further, he knew that the cutting
would go through the cap of a rich lode which had
been worked underneath the road. The result of the
bargain was that the Corporation had the work done for
nothing, and the contractor netted £2OOO.

Long Tunnel Extended, Walhalla (Vie.) last
half-year produced £48,000 worth of gold and paid
±■25,000 in divs. Co. has a cash reserve of £5OOO, and
±/000 invested in debentures. Speculators who have
recently invested in the stock on the assumption that
there was to be a repetition of this creditable record
in the current half-year have been rudely shocked by the
sudden intimation that the late average cannot be main-
tained until considerable further development work has
been done. It was never even hinted in any official report
that the rich chute which made the returns so high was
almost exhausted in existing levels. Who is responsible
for the concealment of the information it is impossible to.
say. The incident supplies one answer, out of many, to
the question, now long discussed in Melbourne papers’ as
to why mining speculation is so unpopular. ’
• • • *
A Maoriland syndicate operating a dredge on
Buckland River (Vic.) is reported to be getting splendid
returns—quite equal to anything from M.L. dredges.
The syndicate has an extensive area of land and is now
erecting another dredge of very large capacity. Marks
Bros , who were lucky enough to peg out the rich section
of the Eucumbene River embracing the principal portion
of Kiandra diggings, from which they have won a lot of
gold, hold the adjoining blocks, on which they are now
erecting a dredge.
* • • *
One curious feature of Australian mining is the
neglect of the law to insist upon the covering-up or
fencing in of disused shafts. Scattered right through
Victoria are old holes without safeguards of any kind, and
sometimes partially hidden by overgrowing bushes, down
which holes the most sober of men might plump 100 feet
or so and perish miserably in the water below. At Ring-
wood, a populous Melb. suburb and picnic resort, there
are three deep shafts, only one of which is boarded up,
while one has absolutely no indication in its vicinity
except a small heap of mullock that any digging ever took
place there. There are several houses close by, to the
gambolling juvenile inmates of which these abandoned
holes must be a constant danger. No wonder it is difficult
to pick up a daily paper without meeting with the head-
ing, “ Fall down an abandoned shaft.”
* • * •
The new rush near Tarnagulla (Vie.), which has
been mildly boomed by the press, doesn’t seem to pan
out well, though 5, 10, 20 and 30oz. nuggets appear to be
not uncommon. A man trying his luck at the place
writes : “This rush is, as usual, much over-estimated by
the papers. For ozs. read dwts., divide the weights and
numbers of nuggets by about four, and you may have
something near actual yields. ... I never put in’such a
miserable Xmas Day as the one just past. Breakfast-
bread and fat; dinner—a piece of pudding from a
neighboring camp, and nothing else ; tea—raw corned
beef. A man gave me a piece of beef, and being ravenous,
I ate it just as it came. . . . One man died yesterday
and more will follow, as the water here is awful stuff.”
• * * •
“ Working Miner ” writes from the Etheridge,
N.Q. : Efforts are now being made by cat-artists to float
and boom worthless mines on Etheridge field. Much
Australian and English capital has already been wasted
in this home of felines, and as a result, the district has
for many years been left without outside capital. A
notable feature of the Etheridge is that at, say, 200 ft.,
the reefs change to white buckstone and all trace of gold
is lost. Several reefs have been sunk in for comparatively
long distances without meeting payable stone. At the
Golden Crown about 130 ft. of sinking was done ere the
fatal buck was hit. Nil Desperandum reached 300 ft.
level before striking buck, and 100 ft. was sunk in it with-
out reaching pay ore. Queenslander’s last sink of 130 ft.
was all in buck ; and in the No. 2 level for 600 ft. no pay
ore has been got ; mine has been on tribute for
the last three years. The Big Reef struck buck at
180 ft., and there is no trace of gold left. This mine has
already sickened a strong English co. The International,
owned by Schafford Bros., is just now the only payable
mine on the field, and the only one, so far as I can see,
which can be looked upon as a good property. Writer
was on this mine at time of first crushing, which yielded
9oz. per ton ; value of gold, £36 a ton. Lode well defined,
and varying in size from Ito 5 ft. At Goldsmith’s Creek
several old reefs are now being taken up, chiefly by
corpse-revivers, and will, no doubt, shortly be offered on
the Australian market. But the only hope of salvation
for the Etheridge is a few strong syndicates with plenty
working capital ; and practical managers who will go
down and find out what lies below the buck.
« * • *
The “ plums ” of Gym pie share-market last year
were No. 2 S. Gt. Eastern and South Glanmire and
Monkland. The former was quoted at 455. per share at
the beginning of the year ; it paid 13s. 9d. in divs., and
wound up the year valued at 80s. per share, so that the
man who bought in Jan. and sold in Dec. would have
cleared 48s. per share. South Glanmire was quoted at
235. per share in Jan. ; it paid 265. 6d. in divs. during the
year on the original number of shares, and wound up the
year with shares saleable at 335. (there being two shares
for one, the capital having been doubled), so that a deal
made in Jan. and cleared up in Dec. would have shown a
profit of 695. 6d. per share, equal to 300 per cent.

The De Beers people last year sold £4,687,194
worth of diamonds, and the profit for the year was
£2,162,709.
* * * *
The negotiations for the purchase of the Tas-
mania gold mine have fallen through. The English
syndicate reduced its proposal to £510,001 in cash and
scrip. Co. promptly refused this absurd offer, and
decid'd to raise £100,009 by debentures to provide a new
pumping plant. The debentures are to be redeemable
in shares, an extra issue of 20,000 having been agreed
upon to provide for this. The mine has had a great
record and will continue for years to be a large
profit-earner. Had it been sold to the Englishmen for
£500,000 the chances are that they would have formed a
company of £1,500,000, that they would have deposed the
practical men who now run the show to such advantage,
and have substituted for them haw-haw youths from
London, with the probable result that the mine would
never again appear in the div. list.

One of the chief questions which Chamberlain
will be called on to decide in S’Africa is that of the con-
tnbution of the Rand gold-mines to the cost of the l.Ve
war. The Rand people are in a cleft stick, Hays Ham-
mond, President of the Rand Chamber of Mines, having
reported in ISB9 : “ The amelioration of conditions conse-
quent upon good government will most materially
enhance the value of the properties of the Witwatersrand
district. . . . I would regard the sum of 6s. per ton as a
conservative estimate of the direct and indirect benefits
of good government . . . [this] would result in an increas e
of annual dividends by £4,826,535 based on last year’s
tonnage of ore crushed.” The gold-bugs have their
good government ” now, but object violently to pay a
f° r it > so the existence of that estimate of the
direct gain ” to be won from a change is loudly cursed.
How grimly humorous were Chamberlain to appropriate
all the £4,826,535 a year and allow the polyglot gang who
engineered the war “the blessings of British rule" as
their sole reward.
“ Alceste ” writes: “A futile discussion has
been going on in the Melb. Aroius on “ how to improve
mining.” Shepherding of leases, insecurity of tenure
and the scarcity of new capital from Bullsland are all
put forward to account for the decay of Victorian
mining, in face of the fact that the gold yield
of 1902 was about the same as that for 1901,
and that the dividend list is the longest for some
time past! The cry about the shepherding of leases has
been grilled till it’s black. Very seldom is a lease in a
good district with what we mining men term ‘ fair pros-
pects ’ kept idle. Plenty of duffer leases are shepherded,
some, I admit near good mines, but, as they pay Crown
rent! it does not seem it would help to improve mining
much if these leases wereall suddenly forfeited and thrown
open for the use of Fleming’s followers, or the Opposition
in Vic. Leg. Assembly, or the Victorian Club, or, indeed,
anybody else with nothing to do. And as to security of
tenure ! Not one of the writers gave an example of
where the terms of a Crown lease had been abrogated in
an arbitrary-way by any Vic. Minister of Mines. Nor did
any of them instance the forfeiture of a lease held by a
British co., the British shareholders in which, for the
most part, do not own the freehold of their shops or homes
and simply pay ground rent to a noble landlord on build-
ing leases. The reluctance of Bull to send capital here
for mining is not astonishing. At the King’s Head
Tavern, in Old Jewry, E.Ct, next Reuter’s office, you 11
find mOre men interested in Victorian mining than
in Pall Mall on a Saturday n'ght, and they all have leases
to sell. The pioneers of this mode of ‘introducing
British capital to the Victorian mining industry ’ some
years back did well. But poor o’d Bull is aweary of the
tribe, and will not listen, charm thi y never so wisely.
That s why. The true causes of the present passing
dullness on the Stock Exchange, and consequently in the
faraway habitats of the wild-cat, are two. one' is that
while metals keep low many small progress ve mines, and
more developed mines in the producing, but not in the
payable stage, are shut down, because holders won’t pay
calls when the market won’t work the shares up and down.
The other reason is that the public in thelast three years
has been forced to lose faith in its'mining leaders of
former days. All investors who followed well-known
directors have lost their money in one big mine after
another. There is no need to be rude to the leaders of
the mining’World, but let any dabbler in mining who has
lost niOney during the last three years lie back and think
over the list of big men directing big mines in which
.his money was lost. There is no specific remedy
for folly, and the investor who has suffered by
buying shares because So-and-So was a director
should make up his mind not to do so again. No altera-
tion of or addition to the laws will imp: ove mining.
We want more honesty and fewer swindles. If it were
made criminal for a director or legal or mine mai ager to
sell or buy shares in his own mine before publishing his
mine's news, we might improve mining—off the face of
the earth ! No, you can no more improve mining than
you can better heaven, or hell, or human nature.

The Governor-General (at whose solemn
swearing-in, t’other day, half-a-dozen people
attended) has been given the, no doubt, unwelcome
task of holding (in Sydney) an “investiture” of
recently-granted Imperial dis-honors, this year.
State Governors were never authorised to hold
“investitures,” and a man of Lord Hopetoun’s
mental size would have surrounded this Durbar-
like proceeding with what the Americans call
much “ dog and hauteur ” —but that is not after
Lord Tennyson’s heart.
* * * *
A.delaideans who have been reading of
Delhi Durbar have been impotently trying to
picture to themselves the magnificence with
which they are sure Senator Richard Chaffey
Baker, K.C., clothed himself on that superlatively
gorgeous occasion. He always was a man to
make the very most of any dignities he managed
to grasp, and, in his own opinion at least, he is the
most important personage at any show he honors
with his presence.
* * * *
The King’s Coronation medal, about which
so much has been made by some of its recipients,
is a trumpery little thing of silver, and by no
means a good sample of the die-sinker’s art. Also,
it bears the wrong date (June 26, instead of Aug.
9), and its intrinsic value is certainly not more
than a pound with the case and ribbon thrown in.
The King “requests” that it shall be worn on
all suitable occasions, so that its utter tawdriness
will soon be widely recognised. Its semblance is as
that of the pewter medals which children delight
in.

£ Australia’s latest murder-victim was a
peppery old fellow, past 80, who found comfort in
damning his relatives to every stranger who came
along. And the old chap, who lived alone in one
of the few delightful spots in S. Aus., would wind
up by telling the “swaggie,” or whoever else it
hxppened to be, that, damnem, the scum
shouldn’t have any money of his—and he had
whips of it. As a matter of fact, though he was

more than six or eight pounds in the house ; so it
was probably a very disgusted stranger that made
tracks from the place an hour or so after breaking
the old man’s skull.
This is what it has come to in M.L :
Vagrancy * John M'Kough, a young man who said he
had been in S’Africa, and had seen service, pleaded not
guiltv to a charge of vagrancy. Constables Wade and
Moriarty said accused had done no work since his return
a few months ago. Accused said he had been operated
on in the legs as the result of the war, he having served
in Brabant’s Horse. He alleged that he had been hay-
making’ and working on a fishing boat. His Worship said
Queen-st. was too full of such men as accused. Returned
troopers had a tendency to go on the downward grade.
The case was one to be met with three months’ imprison-
ment, but he would only award a sentence for a time
that would enable accused to recover physically. A sen-
tence of three months’ hard labor was imposed.
So the gaol is now the sanatorium for wounded
returned troopers, and hard labor the medical
treatment.

One big Australian firm of great military
ardor made a point of giving the returned
swaddy preference of employment. It now
has good reason to be sorry for its “loyalty.”
Certain cohentingenters, who had learned to pick
np sundry trifles at Boer farm-houses and on
board the transports, could not help displaying
their foraging ability when behind the counter.
There have been already some appearances at the
police courts of returned patriots—the military
enthusiasm of their employer not going so far as
to make him condone little martial irregularities
of that sort.

As The Bulletin remarked on a previous
occasion there is a great deal of hard flogging in
the Bible, and when a demented or ill-balanced
person gets religion very badly he generally flogs
somebody. That Melbourne youth, who is accused
of flogging a small boy almost to death, is described
as a religious maniac, and a Ballarat man has just
been diagnosed as a religious lunatic, partly
through his conviction that he is the prophet
Elijah, but mainly through the appalling manner
in which he flogged ids son. The combination of
Elijah and the flogging was held to be sufficient
evidence, and the Ballarat man went to the
asylum. , * *
As the result of years of growling on
parents’ part, most of the S.A. private schools
have agreed to adopt the English system of three
school “ quarters ” a year.
Part of Westralia’s
‘ ‘ charity ” vote goes to
“fresh-air” funds, and is
really a subsidy for carrying
the run-down children of the
poor from the hot, grimy,
barren goldfields to the
seaside. The House passes
the item every year, but
never feels quite sure whe-
ther it ought to, and gener-
ally growls a little. But,
anyhow, it is better and
more honest to have a
straight-out vote like this
than to charge the railways
with the (State’s charity ;
and that is what it amounts
to when the railways are
practically forced to carry
passengers at less than pay-
ing rates.

The early retirement
of the veteran Inspector-
General Fosbery from the
control of the N.S.W. police
should afford Govt, a chance
of placing a strong man of
practical and wide experi-
ence in his place. The
number of subsidiary duties
imposed upon the present
head materially interfere
with the effective working of
the department. The detec-
tive branch of the service
badly needs remodelling ; it
contains a few smart men
and many unspeakable duf-
fers. Salaries for good
“ d’s. ” should be consider-
ably raised, as brains and
pluck cannot be lured for
a laborer’s wage. Were this
done, it would tend towards
making the ’tecs more inde-
pendent and less addicted
to taking presents from
private individuals; it would
be hard for a. detective, as
things stand, to fully dis-
charge his duty and yet live
within his pay and allow-
ance3. The police system also is sadly in need of
a thorough scrubbing; a transference of some
officials would be the best means of breaking up
sundry undesirable cliques.

N.S.W. Govt, has announced that in
future the daily-wage staff will be paid for only
seven non-working days per year—New Years
Day, Anniversary Day, Easter Monday, Eight
Hour Day, King s Birthday, Xmas Day and
Boxing Day. Eight Hour day is a gift of the
O’Sullivan’s, but it is mistaken kindness, as it is
secured at the expense of Easter Saturday. As
Good Friday is not to be paid for, this will mean
that the mechanical and artisan staffs will lose
two days’ pay running. Easter week is too valu-
able a holiday for the professional and clerical
divisions to give up, and the workmen can hardly
go on “on their own.” If the Saturday is not
put in, the men won’t have much of an Easter
Monday, and if the Easter week is to be inter-
fered with the other fellow will make a row.
• • *
N.S.W. public service is to lose its half-
holiday for race-meetings, cricket-matches, open-
ings of Parliament, and other excuses for taking
its best girl down to Manly, or doing-up the front
garden. The practice began with letting half the
staff off on Metropolitan day and the others on
Epsom day, and gradually spread until (even the
service itself admitted that this was “pretty
strong”) the recent Queensland cricket-match was
seized on as a pretext. The Ministerial foot came
down when some of the daily-wage men started to
demand the same concession. Besides the time-
markers in the service, those most affected will be
the race-meeting promoters and the Cricket Ground
people, who used to reckon on the civil servants
to supply a good portion of the ‘ ‘ off-day ” gate.
* * * *
N.S.W. Railway Commissioners are to
save vast sums by knocking off Saturday’s work
from some 2500 employees in the various work-
shops. The majority bow to the ukase, as with a
falling-off in the railway revenue some retrench-
ment was expected, and it might have been worse.
Voices here and there are heard inquiring how
much the Commissioners themselves, the princi
pal officers of the service, and the professional
staff, whose salaries are four figures high, are to
be reduced. Nothing, of course. They shelter
themselves behind the good old plea that their
salaries have been “voted” by Parliament.
Surely when the Assembly voted the railway
estimates they were drawn up on the understand-
ing that the small men were to consider their
screws as inviolate as those of the big folk. A
reduction of even 2h per cent, on salaries over
<£6oo would represent an amount quite as con-
siderable as tire contemplated saving, and the
reduction would, or should, not come so hard on
those affected.

The cable-liar grows monotonous. But
now that all the dailies get the same service, and
expaitd their cables extraordinarily, the result to
the public, though bewildering, is often also
amusing. The Age last week published this from
its “own correspondent”:—
The prevalence and severity of ophthalmia in Egypt
make the disease a kind of national plague to the natives.
With the view of discovering effective remedial means of
dealing with the evil, the “ Oaisse de la Dette,” which
deals with the finances of India in respect to foreign debt
payments, has offered to devote £40,000 for the promotion
of the medical study of Egyptian ophthalmia.
The Argus version of the very same wire was :
Sir Ernest Cassel, the well-known London merchant,
who has made large contributions to philanthropic objects
within the past few years, has offered £40,000, to be
devoted to furthering the study of ophthalmia in Egypt,
with a view of obtaining a cure for the disease.
The joke of the whole thing is that the great
Egyptian organization for reducing interest should
be made an Indian affair, and be confounded with
Cassel, the London Hebrew financier.

In three days during the holiday season,
Melb. and Sydney papers reported over 200
fatalities in the two States alone, mostly from
heat-apoplexy, drowning, and fooling with a gun.
A holiday season seems to be as deadly and almost
as costly as a big war.
Primate Smith cir-
cularises for 20,000
gifts of Is- or 2s. (id.
from the 20,000 persons
he has confirmed since
he arrived in Sydney.
He wants the gifts for
the Cowper Dean-
ery Endowment
Fund. It must
often seriously
tax the sacerdo-
tal mind to
discover a new
way of getting
a finger into the
public pocket.
Whatever it ought to do, election of
mayors by the ratepayers does not, in S. Australia,
give results worth enthusing over. The billet is
in most places reckoned the rightful property of
“the man of means,” and in more than one place
a little coterie of more-or-less gilded ones cut up
the chair among them by mutual arrangement.
Last year there was but one solitary fight over a
mayoralty in city and suburbs, and in the country
there were less than half a dozen. No doubt the
people could do better if they liked; but in
practice they don’t, with the result that actually
they have less say in the business than when the
mayor is elected by the aldermen or councillors.
Also, instead of being chosen for his brains, he is
chosen for his “ beans.”
* * * *
The decease of the Tasmanian Deputy-
Postinaster-General has directed attention to this
point, which has always exercised the minds of
the small army of public servants who have been
transferred to the Federal service :
Is the whole Postal (say) Department one ? Can Jones,
who has been 35 years in the N.S. Wales P. 0., claim
seniority over Brown, with 34 years’ service in Victoria?
Because, if Brown finds out that he is behind a man
whose existence he had never suspected, Brown will make
a row. If Smith, up in Queensland, who has been watch-
ing his immediate senior die slowly by inches for years
past, finds that Robinson from S.A. gets the dead man’s
billet, Smith will write indignantly to the Courier as a
mistaken individual who voted for the Bill. The point
that Brown, with his 34 years in Victoria, is before the man
with 33 years in Tasmania, and that Smith has his chance
of a dead man’s billet in Westralia, won’t occur to them.
For, properly speaking, one Federal Department is one
Federal Department. It isn’t six ; and the man immedi-
ately under another in one State has no claim to the
vacancy if it can be more properly filled to the credit of
the Commonwealth by the transfer of an officer from
another State. Not that you will be able to convince
Brown and Smith of that.
* * * •
Pastor Tremayne Dunstan has told his
story to a gatheflfcig of Sydney friends. It is to
be presumed that he made out the best case that
lie could for himself ; and it does not look too
good a case :
The rev. gentleman, it seems, has been juggling with
money, and losing it in the process, for almost a decade.
During the greater portion of that time he was earning a
salary which, for a clergyman, was munificent, and he
might at almost any time have settled his affairs on a
square basis by an assignment. But he went on,
hoping against hope, plunging into fresh diffi-
culties, borrowing from one to pay another, and,
finally, before his hurried departure,' arranging little
loans by means which are indefensible in the
light of any sophistry. Mr. Dunstan gives us his
assurance (which, all things considered, may be believed)
that there wasn’t a woman in the case, that he only took
a very small amount of money with him, and that he
didn’t lose money by playing cards. On this point—“ I
was invited to a house once and found that it was a
progressive euchre party. My wife and myself proved a
nuisance all the evening, because we did not even under-
stand the game. Even yet I do not know how to play
euchre, yet presumably on the strength of that visit my
downfall has been ascribed to card-playing.” Of course
one can lose money at cards without knowing “ how to
play ” and playing ; in fact, that is the easiest method of
losing. But Mr. Dunstan, on his own account, had as his
only luxury for the large amount he got through, the pay-
ment of money-lenders’ interest. The investigation of
Mr. Dunstan’s conduct is to be undertaken by a com-
mittee appointed by the chairman of the meeting of his
friends. But the verdict from such a body can hardly
carry any weight. An impartial investigation would be
alone of any value, and possibly that may be held. Mr.
Dunstan states his whole indebtedness at under £3OOO.
There are onlv two “ widow ladies ” among the creditors,
which shows that the luck of widow-ladies is 11 in ” just
now. When a popular clergyman gets into difficulties
the widow’s mite generally figures very largely in his
debts.
* * . ,
Brisbane inspectors found an Asiatic state
of things sanitary in the northern towns during a
recent tour. Townsville, where three cases of
plague had occurred, had no rubbish service, not
even a dust-cart; and the back lanes, yards, and
vacant blocks were covered with household gar-
bage and all manner of filth—half hidden, in many
cases, by the growth of prickly-pear and castor-oil
plants. Ihe accumulated mucks and smells of
yeais had become part of home, sweet home, with
some of the residents, and at first the municipal
authorities opposed the inspectors’ cleaning so
vigorously that an Order in Council had to be
obtained to cleanse the town. Then 30 men and 17
large drays were specially employed, and these
removed and burnt 2000 loads from one-half Jof
lownsville. I hey had started to dig up the other
bait when the inspectors (who probably couldn’t
stand any more stirred-up Townsville) departed
hurriedly for the next place.

Hutton and Bigjohn and the Backwash
among them have just about flattened out the
army in South Australia. On a recent Ade-
laide parade there were 34 officers and 88 men,
and several of the officers admitted that they had
turned qualify for their quarter’s pay.
A Sydney woman petitioning for divorce
hasn’t heard of her husband since he escaped from
the Central Lock-up, Sydney, three years ago.
ihe Court has ordered that, as he can’t be per-
sonally served, he must, by way of substituted
service, be advertised for in the Sydney papers.
a ice chance of a response.
WHAT A HORRID WOMAN!
DADA : “She says our kid gave her kid the
measles."
MAMMA : " What a horrid woman! Why she
knows perfectly well that her kid came over to
our Cecil and took them.”
Woman hasn’t lost all her privileges
though she has found her rights. She can still
get the best of the fight and then proceed against
the man. At a police-court, t’other day, a man,
charged with assaulting his wife, appeared with
his arm in splints and swore that in the little
domestic row between himself, wife and children
he had his arm broken, his ear slit, and was stabbed
in the groin. Still he was fined 40s.

Koftnd about Adelaide it is complained
that suburban roads are going to wreck for want
. of repair, while the citizens are
putting up their money—not much
of it, but enough to do a good deal
of road repairing—for some kind of
a brazen image “in memory of
the S.A. soldiers who fell in
Africa.” Surely the people
who fall just outside Adelaide,
owing to the big holes in the
highway, should be the flrafl
consideration.
There is incentive to reflection
on the intricacies of the social sys-
tem of N.S.W. in Minister for
Education Perry’s announcement
that he favors the exclusion
from the Service of female
teachers on their marriage,
but is willing to re-admit
them as widows.
There are three “Ragged” schools in
Hobart —wretched importations from the slums of
London dumped down in a healthy Australian
island which should have no stratum of paupers.
The Lewis Govt, has just taken the first step
towards wiping them out by reducing the State
subsidy. Parsondom and old maidendom fought
and kicked and bit, but the Govt, was unmoved.
One lady who runs a night ragged-school boasted,
with beautiful unconsciousness of the exquisite
humor of the thing, that she had now a second
generation of boys in the school, the sons of “ old
scholars.”

NATURAL HISTORY.
"Pa!" “ Well”
" Can I ask you a question, Pa?” “ Ye-es.”

with his jumping, Pa, or would it help him

Echoes of the Yentnor disaster and the
loss of a huge consignment of Chinese corpses (or
rather skeletons) still float round. Latest news is
that the s.s. Energy has been employed for some
three weeks past in an effort to locate the wreck
of the Ventnor off Hokianga Heads, with a view
to the recovery of the large cargo of Celestial
bones. There are on the Energy two Chinese re-
gresentatives and a rep. of the underwriters. The
hinese are sparing no expense, especially for the
recovery of the coffin containing the remains of
magnate Sew Hoy, whose relatives are willing to
pay £2OOO to £3OOO to secure the burial of his
corpse in Chinese soil. There is thus a chance for
any enterprising expert at corpse-finding, although
as a steady business it is as out of date as astrology,
and experts are naturally scarce.
PROBABLY NO ONE KNEW THE
DIFFERENCE.
FRIEND: “By Jove, they've put it in an awfully
good position."
IMPRESSIONIST : “ Yes, hut what's the use of that
when they've hung it upside-down?"
“It is remarkable,” writes a correspon-
dent, “in connection with Sydney Hospital that
one never sees (even in winter, when the sunshine
is new life to the convalescent) any patients out
on the balconies in the front of the building. I
am assured that this is because the people in the
houses opposite (many of them subscribers) object
to the sight of convalescents sitting in the sun.
And, as the hospital cannot afford to lose its
subscriptions, the patients are allo ./ed to use only
the cold back balconies. Even those in invalid-
chairs have to lie out in the shade, and the others
have to walk about wrapped in blankets if they
go out of the ward. If the explanation of this
restriction be as stated, I can only say that when
those subscribers come in their turn to die, they
won’t want any sunshine in the next world.”
r^ e m edical superintendent of Sunbury
(Vie.) lunatic asylum is an enthusiatio advocate
of female “warders” for male patients. The
new system works admirably at Sunbury, He
says : “ I’ve seen a nurse sit for hours after duty
was over with a dying man, soothing and com-
forting his last moments, and he died in her arms
with his head resting on her shoulder. The men
woujd have simply let the poor beggar die. Not
that the men mean to be unkind, but they are not
built that way.’” The case quoted by the
super, is not necessarily a fair argument. A
photograph of that death-scene might have
accounted for the situation. If the patient was
a classic-featured lunatic with long, poetic hair,
and a sweet, consumptive smile the humane nurse
was doing no more than any man would have
done for an insane damsel of personal attractions.
Which reminds The Bulletin that the girl
patients are not permitted the soothing influence
of a male warder’s arms. The new system is a
one-sided arrangement at present. And it seems
to have started on the wrong side of the asylum.

After the match was over at Sebastopol, the cricketers

On their arrival home, the Cup was taken to Mr. Brett’s,

* * * •
Railway men visiting Victoria are aston-
ished (writes a correspondent) to see the “ make-
up ” of suburban trains, and predict a big accident
one of these days. The trains are built up on a
system of heavy engine first, then a couple of
heavy first-class carriages, a middle composed of
short and. very light second-class cars, and a tail
of additional heavy first-classers. Instead of
being strongest in themiddle they are weaker there
than anywhere else, so look out, says the visiting
expert, for a disastrous smash.
• » * *
Drivers and firemen on N.S.W. western
line are now provided with respirators for the
Glenbrook tunnel. In spite of an officially-
vouched-for statement, the recent close shave of
two railway men, who were nearly suffocated
whilst trying to get their engine through this
terrible hole, is. not by any means the only inci-
dent of its kind. Why do not the Railway
Commissioners take some steps to ventilate the
tunnel ?
Considering the amazing capacity of Car-
dinal Moran for putting his feet in it when he
has only two feet, the Church ought to be right
glad that his Eminence isn’t a centipede. That
appallingly wild statement of his, to the effect
that at Samoa some Protestant missionaries in-
duced a British warship to bombard a building
where a number of R.C. non-combatants were in
refuge, was most glaringly improbable all along,
though the Cardinal doubtless believed it to be
true when he said it. But, then, a man with a
capacity for believing such things off-hand, and a
tendency to state his bel'efs off-hand, shouldn’t be
a Cardinal —he ought to be a bull in a country where
there are plenty of fences to rush at, and if he
had been born 800 years ago he would have made
a very good battering-ram. This isn’t an age
when any church can afford to put its head down
and rush at things. The church’s head isn’t so
solid as it was 1000 years ago. And now that the
Cardinal has refused to apologise, and has decided
to keep rushing at things, the best thing he can
do is to leave. The spectacle of a high clerical
dignitary being sued for libel wouldn’t be edify-
ing, and it may come to that. And, if it does, Dill
Macky will stand on his head in George-street,
Sydney, from sheer joy. Cardinal Moran is Dill’s
very best asset just now.

TO-NIGHT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I ever scorned the lover light,
Who loved and rode away,
Who clouded all the summer bright.
I ever scorned the lover light,
Who made a night of day.
But, God be with you, dear, to-night,s
And make my night your day.
I cry for him passed out of sight—
Ah, God be with you, dear, to-night
(Who loved and rode away !)
THE YOUNG 'UN.
SONGS UNSUNG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Songs unsung will the longest last,
Soft sweet tones are at rest so soon ;
Clear and shrill is the lark at noon,
But still when the even hour is past.
All the songs that in Spring fall fast
Cease when the Winter snows are strewn,
Songs unsung will the longest last,
Soft sweet tones are at rest so soon.
Ardent vows to the winds are cast,
Faint as the winds that brood and croon
Deep in the firs ’neath rising moon ;
But, while others are dead and past,
Songs unsung will the longest last.
