A WOMAN’S LETTER.
My Dear Arini—
Sydney, January 13, 1903.
Our State Govt. House people are now 80 miles
or so away from the metropolitan dust and nor’-
easters in their Sutton Forest home—riding,
golfing and photographing, and looking forward
to nearly three mouths’ rest from openings, first-
nights, the concert of the phenomenal student,
the cake-and-apron fair, the speeching and the
charity ball. Sydney citizens, all who may, are
making for the Meccas of their fancy—Moss Vale,
the Blue Mountains, Tasmania, Maoriland.
A fortune-teller who has just retired from busi-
ness tells me that the majority of her clients were
married women from 35 to 50 years of age. Very
few unmarried women, excepting really young
ones, consulted her, she said. It was the discon-
tented, unhappy ones that came for consolation
and hope. “They wanted to be told that they
would be widows, and I told them, and they used
to go away radiant; but they didn’t want to be
told that they’d remain widows; not they ! Not
the oldest or plainest of them, and to make them
entirely hopeful I had to indicate another hus-
band —rather vaguely, of course. He was already
married, with this or that number of children,
and it was evident that my client had him
already in her eye, for she nearly always said,
‘Yes, that’s him!’ and gave me details about
him, and said she was sure ‘ things would right
themselves ’ —which, of course, referred to his wife
being got rid of somehow when her husband had
gone to glory.”
A distinguished visitor to Sydney was going to
Melbourne by express, and was asked by a friend
of his to show some attention on the road to a
lady fellow-traveller who was journeying alone.
The D.Y. promised enthusiastically, and fulfilled
his promise by bringing the lady a cup of tea next
morning at Albury, saying, “3d., please!” She
missed a point by not giving hint 6d. and telling
him to keep the change. Perhaps last time he
looked after a lady on a trip she was an actress.
A man friend of mine tells me how he looked after
an actress down to Melbourne once. During the
evening’s run she got through his flask of whisky,
all of it, and he had to renew it at a refreshment
room with very poor stuff, which he couldn’t
drink—but she could. At Albury she had break-
fast at his expense and hinted champagne. Later
on she had luncheon at his expense, and demanded
champagne. And she was such a sweet, fresh,
innocent young thing, too !
The Forbes man who is reported to have
entered, late in life, into his kingdom a
million in Chancery money —has a grown-up
family, with whom he used not to be on the best
of terms. He lived apart from them, and went
on with his wood-carting in solitary sordidness.
Two of his daughters work at warehouses in
Sydney.
What an extraordinary vogue appendicitis is
having! Yet a few years ago the very word
was unknown to the average person. An old-
age pensioner who died of it recently was
quite cheered in his last hours (as was also
his wife) by the fact that “ he had the same thing
as the King.” An American doctor attributes
the increase in the number of app. cases to the
changes in the methods of milling flour, which
take from the grain all the phosphates and leaves
only starch and gluten. He asserts that wherever
a community has abandoned the old-fashioned,
coarse flours, appendicitis has very soon made its
appearance, the latest illustration being among
the negroes of the South, who, while their bread
diet was solely cornflour, did not know what the
disease was. As the use of white flour has
increased, appendicitis has made its appearance
among them with disastrous results. The reason-
ing is, of course, quit j empirical. At one time
it was believed by many that tomatoes caused
cancer, simply because cancer began to be under-
stood when tomatoes began to be eaten.
A child, six years old, has been killed by the
cow which he was milking. And an infant left in
charge of an older infant went to sleep, and the
younger infant was burnt to death. These two
accidents occurred recently in N.S.W. Someone
will have to write a new “ Cry of the (Australian)
Children.” A child of six milking a cow ! One
of the proofs that we are living in a gentler time
is the growing recognition of the rights of child-
hood, but the growth is slow ; there is so much
ignorance and brute-beast to be yet eliminated.
At Newington Refuge, lately, I came across a
nice, mild young woman of about 25, who is par-
tially paralysed and wholly blind from the effects
of hard labor and ill-treatment when a child. She
was a bush girl, and her mother died when she
was six years old. “Friends” took charge Of
her, and over-work ill-treatment wrecked her
physical health.
Lady Beaumont’s departure from Sydney will
leave the Women’s Club in tears. She was a
most enthusiastic clubite, never missed a
committee - meeting, and had her fellow
members to Admiralty House lunches. Mrs.
Hodges, wife of the master of the C. of E. Gram-
mar School, was nominated by Lady B. her
successor as President of the club.
Melba’s favorite foliage is clearly not that of the
olive tree. She had friction with pianistScherek.
She quarrelled with Miss Gill, her secretary, the
other day. Now her ad vance agent, Mr. Tait, is
suing her for breach of agreement.
Alice Hollander is living and studying at
Lexham Gardens, London, with Miss Hope-
Wallace and teacher Bethune. Miss H.-W. is a
cousin of our late Gov.-General, and a niece of
Mr. Bethune’s, who is also, of course, a cousin of
Hopey’s, and she chaperones Alice when they go
visiting into the —er—peerage.
“Willie” Beattie, who took his sweet tenor
voice and a romantic play in MS. to London a
year or so ago, is now on his way back to Sydney.
Too much fog, mist and cold for the Australian
boy. r
Myrtle Meggy, the 14-year-old pianiste wonder
who went to London to study, is domiciled in the
home of her teacher, Miss Verne. She has a
lesson every day, and a weekly lesson in harmony
and orchestral work ; and is also taken to the best
concerts and given the chance of meeting the best
musical people—all for £lOO a year.
Mr. James Milson’s death last Monday, at
Milson’s Point, put the ferry-steamers’ flags at
half-mast. He once owned the ferry and the
Point which bears his name. Mr. Milson, famed
as a pioneer yachtsman of Sydney Harbor, was 88
when he died. He was a picturesque old gentle-
man, with snowy hair and beard, and almost up
to the last seemed good for another 20
years. He was a great chess-player, and
a 70-year-old niece of his, Miss Shairp, once
a noted beauty, used to regularly play with him
a foewoman worthy of his skill. Mr. Milson’s
long life seems to have been one of unbroken
health, domestic happiness and financial success.
His first wife was a daughter of the then Surveyor-
General of N.S.W. ; his second—Miss Stewart,
of Parramatta. His six children, three sons and
three daughters, survive him, and are all well
known in pastoral, business and society circles.
“Paul Jones” is back in town in her saucy
shipping-clerk’s uniform. A crowded audience
greeted Miss Young in the part at the
Palace Theatre on Saturday night, when she
came out in brown velvet coat and knee-
breeches, and lace-trimmed shirt; and posed her
fair head against the blue waters of the harbor
of St. Malo—Herve Riel’s St. Malo, I sup-
pose. “Rocks to starboard, rocks to port; all
the passage scarred and scored.” It is a fine bit
of scenic painting, and you catch yourself imagin-
ing the French warships, twenty-two in all, and
the English victors in full chase, almost treading
on their tails.
Miss Florence Young’s curvilinear attractions
are undeniable. There is nothing massive in her
revealed contours, and she is still under the age
limit at which artistes should be pole-axed for
wearing revealsome male attire under the lime-
light. I like Miss Young’s interpretation of
Planquette’s Paul Jones better than anything else
in which I have seen her. There is much work in
the part, but Florence’s version is artistic and
piquant right through the comic opera’s storm and
stress, and her fine, strong, bright voice shows no
sign of fraying after the hard season she has had.
Carrie Moore, as usual full of vitality and
pretty, cheeky frivolity, was overweighted in
appearance by her wild red wig. The thin little
soprano, with her bird’s neck and eyes, always
has “a way with her” in the matter of hats—
their fashion, the angle at which they are worn,
or the manner in which she dangles them by their
strings on their arm. She makes them point her
“ points ” in her gospel according to Paul Jones.
One hat, all wire ribs and forget-me-nots, has no
crown, and leaves the top of the wild, freckle-
colored wig exposed to the limelight.
Lauri’s soul-girdling smile and the ballet’s
sweep and swirl and spread of lace-frills were
thankfully, if languidly, accepted by the crowd of
wilting first-nighters. Mid-January first-nighters !
How I longed for a water-melon, a big fifteen-
pounder, with a big hole in it (made by myself),
which I might hold on my knee and in which I
might bury my face (and blushes) whilst the
frisky pink and white ballet was lifting up its
torchon lace tail.
“ The Christian King,” which Wilson Barrett
tried on the kangaroo in Australia on his last visit,
had its first British production (at Bristol) last
month, a correspondent tells me. Our tall,
saintly-looking friend, Lillah M'Carthy, with the
wonderful bend in her back, looked very fine,
“ but was melodramatic as Zebuda ” —she who
tries to lure Alfred on to the path that is supposed
to be paved with rapture and bordered with roses
and primroses. As if the part, or any part
of the play, was aught but melodrama !
Miss Edyth Olive took the fancy of my corres-
pondent as Elswitha (who marries Alfred, and
rocks her babe in the wooden cradle), and the
critics praise her looks and acting. Can Mis 3
Olive be Edyth Latimer, of Sydney, who played
with the co. here ?
A well-to-do widow, who went to a fashionable
Summer resort for the season, took a furnished
cottage and engaged the services of a local damsel
as maid. This girl was shortly to be married to
the village blacksmith, and the swain soon got
into the habit of passing the evenings in the
kitchen with the maid, while the mistress spent a
lonely time in the front parlor. “What,” she
asked one day, “do you and William find to do
out there every night?” “Oh, ma’am,” sim-
pered the maid, ‘ ‘ we—er-er —oh, we mostly play
cards.” “Well,” rejoined the widow, “bring
your cards in here to-nght ; I’m too lonely by
myself night after night. I’ll join in your game.”
Soon the cards became a nightly institution.
William was a fine-looking blacksmith, and soon
the luckless little servant-girl found—well, one
day the faithless William and the mistress dis-
appeared, leaving her the cards to play Patience
with.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ There is gnashing of teeth in certain quarters
regarding the alleged ingratitude of a personage
entertained by the ‘friends’ of her youth. Not
only has the honored guest neglected to pay back
in kind the hospitality of her hostesses (p’raps she
took it for a free-will offering, not a loan), but she
is reported to wax ‘ killingly funny ’ re the
‘frumps,’ otherwise her sometime entertainers,
while the sight of their little gift, ’tis understood,
invariably ‘ tickles nearly to death ’ the quizzical
recipient and her chorus of facetious fashionables.
If the fair Frivol associates such infinite jest with
said meek little souvenir, what jokes she must
crack concerning that expensively funereal urn,
which coffins the greetings of another band of
admirers ! It really was rather funny in any
capacity unconnected with tombs.
“ One of Senator Sargood’s daughters married a
young Adelaide medico, Dr. Rupert Hornabrook,
who, as befitted the son-in-law of a Defence
Minister, has done much fighting. He began as
a student in the Adelaide Hospital dispute, into
which he entered with great enthusiasm. Then
he battled with the plague at Bombay, going
thence to S’Africa, where he was at the first con-
test of the Boer war, and was afterwards shut up
in Ladysmith. It was also cabled once that he
captured 500 Boers single-handed. Dr. Horna-
brook’s father owns the York Hotel, Adelaide,
which he once kept, and the late Premier and
Speaker, John Bray, was his uncle.
“A Melbourne trading magnate, who lately
bought a—haw—estate of about 10 acres in a
village near the city, promptly had a flagpole
erected, and aped vice-royalty’s plan of denoting
his absence and presence. Even when he drops
across to the township barber for a shave, the bit
of calico is hauled down. The rag-man hasn tas
yet started a sentry, but the —haw—lodge-keeper s
cottage has a most suitable verandah, so that that
may yet come. Melbourne rag-trade is rich in
humorous features.
“ A private letter brings word of great Lyne
junkets in Tasmania—of how Oom Bill sported on
his native heath, down on the East Coast, and
danced at hops given by his neighbors and him-
self. In fact, the blithe rogue-elephant was so
constrained by the bonds of festivity, woven by
the brethern and sistern of his birthplace, that he
had to resist—with great sorrow to resist —an
invitation to a Commonwealth feast on January
the first, where Gen. Hutton sat conspicuously
above the salt. It was to have been a merry little
birthday dinner, calculated to obliterate the
tracks of strife; stomachs with but a single
thought, two mouths that eat as one. But the
elephant was too busy.
“A marriage listed for this week (14th) is the
sequel to a brief courtship in Japan. The lady
took three months’ leave-of-absence from a
Government department (she had saved up her
bawbees from many a year’s screw), and set off
last winter for the East, with some friends. They
had mapped out arrangements for much sight-
seeing, leaving nothing like a fair margin for
random pleasurings. However, that margin
availed, and the lady returned to Melbourne with
an engagement ring. Last week her Croesus from
the East came for his treasure, who resigns from
the Government service, and sails away to Japan.
Now all the girls in the Public Service dream of a
trip to Japan.
“ A wealthy Melbourne widow who has survived
two husbands relinquishes a charming suburban
home and enters the nursing lists. She goes to
Sydney immediately, having arranged to enter a
hospital there. It is that there may
be a desire to give a possible No. 3 all possible
advantages of skilled nursing, should he threaten
to die.
“At polo at Moonee Valley, the other day,
after relays of tea-cups had gone the rounds, the
latest drink fads—mineral waters —were on tap.
You mustn’t endanger your prestige for smartness
by refusing to drink of these waters. To be in
touch with the mighty, you must take a publio
dose of the waters. Alas! woman, having now
the vote, is also going to make public confession
of a liver. No more will man think it fit to offer
gentle sympathy when he sees his beloved with a
far-away, eating-her-heart-out-expression ; no, he
will slip away to the druggist and come back with
a tender of Carlsbad !
“ The polo match, on Friday, served to introduce
the lately-returned voyager—Miss Landale— to
public sight again. The strikingly chic loorakite
has come back from her long globe-trot mi re
French -than the French in her dressing, and m< re
Yank than the Yankees in her manner, bhe
struck on Friday a distinctly new note, with an
independent laugh and some ideas, which she
served hot and savorily. She wore a sketchy
French muslin, with black and cream trimmings.
Her saucy floral hat was a bolder edition of a
Dolly Varden.
“ Melbourne seems threatened with a confusion
of prominent Clarkes. Already there is difficulty
in differentiating clearly the many ‘ Miss Clarkes,’
members either of the State-Governor’s family,
the Cliveden clan, or the new Bishop’s household ;
and now another of the name is expected on a
lengthy visit to State Government House —Miss
Clarke, only daughter of the late Agent-General
Andrew Clarke.
“ Nellie Stewart’s ‘Country Mouse’is a cunning
rural coquette, far better able to * play ’ her
victims than the ‘town cats,’ upon whose pre-
serves she poaches. Woodland nymphs, as a rule,
are less artless than they look. A certain child
of the bush lately wrote home in her first letter
from boarding-school that ‘she knew the English
master was in love with her by the way he looked
at her.’ It was a ‘high-class’ school, and the
English master was an exemplary member of a
circumspect, not to say austere teaching staff, so the
complaint, lodged against him by the new pupil’s
father, fell upon the Lady Principal like a bolt
from the blue. ‘My daughter,’ wrote papa, ‘has
known how to talk her own language since she
was a two-year-old. lam now paying for her at
college, to be learned French and finishings, not
to be made up to by English teachers ! ’
“Nellie Stewart, nowadays, lives and has her
being in the theatre. She has filed down her
dress-distractions, eschews her old reso.ts, and
shuns the casual gapers. Even New Year’s
Day—a nice sunny day to have off—Sweet
Nell spent in a box at ‘Dick Whittington.’
In this stern devotion to work, how different to
the other Nell, who walks from the Opera dress-
ing-room to the circle, and eliminates the artiste
entirely in the Social frivoller ! They tell a yarn
illustrative of Melba’s grand spirits and love of
fooling in the Big Smoke. Some kiddies’ party
was on in a Royal quarter. One Hon. Something
or other, aged eight, declined to play ‘Kiss in
the Ring,’as being‘such shocking form.’ ‘Oh
is it?’ laughed Melba, leaving the lordly adults
and joining the ring. ‘Come, I’ll take your
place—and the kisses.’ She did, and entered
into the fun past all the understanding of the
seniors ; for all the kisses, you must remember
were juvenile ones.
“Armes Beaumont—the next on the list to
benefit keeps up-to-date in books. He can no
longer see to read, but has all the best fiction of
the day sorted out by a diligent taster, and those
that pass muster are read to him.
Mr. Bent, the Railway Minister, is mourning
his wife, who died last week at Brighton. Mr
and Mrs. Bent and their only child, a daughter
have always been a happy family—the head of
which testified that, in bad times, ‘ our little place
was like a new pin, for then my two turned up
their sleeves and made things extra spruce.’ P
“A. good test of Melbourne’s quietness at Xmas-
tide is that of a recent honeymoon pair who
finding every nook in the mountains and every
niche at the seaside monopolised, remained in the
city and arc putting in a three weeks term of
idleness and bliss to the soft music of the familiar
tram bells. No one is calling, though they are
staying at a city hotel not five miles distant from
their former homes.
“ The she-golfer is a nicely-discriminating per-
son. Her squatter chum on the links she calls
‘Bob,’ ‘Dick,’ ‘Ted,’ ‘Bill,’ but her caddie is
‘Richard,’ ‘Edward,’ ‘Augustus,’ ‘William.’
There is a world of snobbishness in a strong con-
tralto call of ‘Richard !' and of friendliness in a
‘ Dick.’
“ Every woman, these days, is tempted to be her
own dress-builder. A Collins-st. cutter offers, for
half-a-crown, to allow a client to use her profes-
sional scissors, pattern, time and advice, in the
cutting-out of a dress. The novice can then take
her nicely-scissored materials home, and join them
without trouble, and run them up on the machine
quickly and easily. The result is a smartly designed
frock. Two sisters out Toorak way produce most
artistic blouses by this half-crown method. They
do more ; before stitching up, they use their scis-
sored pieces as patterns, and oblige half-a-dozen
friends.
“The ‘baby’ sun-hat, with its starched frills,
or its miniature muslin flounces, is the antithesis
to the Panama. The fairy Lilian shades her
peach complexion with the former, the smart
Claudia shows her pert face beneath the latter.
The flying gossamer aids the complexion in the
case of the Panama patron—but the wearer of the
guileless ‘baby’ hat has to trust to its casual
shade and the natural roses or lilies of her cheeks.
The latter is, of course, the less common of the
two thatch mounts.
“The M.C.C. moonlight concerts were popular
with canoodlers. The other Monday a fierce
electric light picked out the platform and its near
vicinity from dark space, and showed a few rows
of middle-aged bonnets and respectable, elderly
boxers. That was all the audience the concert
drew, while two thousand couples, more or less
(probably more) wandered to the cloistered paths,
the shrub-shades, the friendly nooks of the course,
where the sound of the tootlers’ top G and the
eye of the multitude could not reach. And so
the evening progressed, the bonnets and boxers
forming a solemn congregation about the platform
and the giddy Panamas secreted far away in the
outer circles of the benevolent darkness. Thus
the ‘ Concerts ’ are very popular.
“Where are the male singers ? When are they
going to enter the ring ? The Marshall Hall
school only boasts one eligible for the platform,
and his debut, the other day, was not to be
regarded seriously. The Peterson classes pro-
duced not one male student, although there were
a few of the sex taking instruction at the Univer-
sity classes. The little singing-girls seem to make
a dozen debuts in long dresses, then short dresses,
then again in long, before they finally confess
themselves satisfactorily launched in the musical
ranks. The male, it seems, only comes out when
he means to stay out.
“The miniature craze increases. A wealthy
matron from St. Kilda side sports the champion
medallion of Melliourne. It is a full-length
painting on opal of her husband in a fancy ball
costume. This precious miniature has a diamond
frame.

“ The old order changeth. The suburban rate-
payers spilt no over proof stuff by way of wel-
coming 1903. ‘Waits’were warned off. Garden
gates were barred and windows locked ; in some
suburbs dogs, were put on patrol. An automatic
bull-dog is the latest freak of the householder.
How would an automatic bite feel ?
“The swarm of barrackers at the wheel-race
made a great sight of the M.C.C. grounds last
Saturday. The pavilion was as bright and ani-
mated as a field of poppies in the wind. It was a
fair square chance to dress to a warmish tempera-
ture, and to sport ‘openworks’ (above and below),
also the ribbon-threaded lingerie. No amateur
dressmaker need bother aliout patterns for under-
wear. The new styles may be studied from the
samples shown through fresh-air yokes by the
smart. On Saturday there were rows of charmers
who made a profound study of each other’s slip-
bodices —and other things.
“Old Mr. Dardell, the Freneh-Swiss vigneron
and gardener, just dead at the age of 91, has long
been a respected identity in the Geelong (Vic.)
district. Departed was the owner of a lovely
garden at Batesford, one of the prettiest, though
dampest, villages in Victoria. Mr. Dardell
always maintained that a garden should be de-
corative, even though planted primarily with a
view to profit. His own was laid out in terraces,
and when the cherries were ripe, the effect was as
if a rain of great rubies had fallen on theleify
arcades and vine-covered trellises of ‘ Dardell’s
Hill.’ The old gardener, who had been married
three times, suffered a great loss some years ago
when his youngest child, the son of his old age,
met his death by an accident.
“A Westerner took the palm among the
Saturday night audience at Dick and his Cat,
Mrs. Archibald Burt (Miss Roberts of yore).
Residence on the goldfields has increased instead
of diminished her style. Her warm Spanish
coloring gave a new glory to ivory satin and reefs
of chiffon, to say nothing of a rich cloak of inky
satin. Diamonds had a good background in the
attractive raven head. Mrs. Agar Wynne also lit
up her diamonds on a black background.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Premier Barton completes his 66th year
on Jan. 18.
Premier Seddon this year surpasses the
Premiership record of ex-Premier Forrest.
Captain Wallington and Cecil Rhodes were
fellow-students at Oriel College, Oxford.
B. J. lias linked again, this time in
London. It probably comes easier with praotice.
Clement Wragge was originally intended
for the law; He was articled in London and
served a term at Clifford’s Inn.
N.S.W. ex-Lands Minister Hassall is now
in business in S’Africa. He is said to have
accidentally dropped on a very good thing there.
Encouraged by the success of “Tommy
Cornstalk,” Mr. “Jack” Abbott intends to
publish a S’African story called “On Plain and
Veldt.”
Died recently at Scone (N.S.W.) Mrs. G.
Beii, widow ef the late member for the Upper
Hunter, and the eldest sister of old “Jock”
Robertson.
Admiral Bedford, the new Governor of
Westralia, is the officer who commanded H.M.S.
Shah in her memorable battle with the Peruvian
man-of-war Huascar.
Among Australians who lorded it at Delhi
Durbar was Sydney’s big beer man, R. L. Tooth
(Mr. “ Lucas Tooth ” he is now), who was among
Lord Curzon’s special guests.
Sydney Town Clerk Nesbitt is said, on
such competent authority as that of John Morley,
to have delivered the most eloquent of the British
addresses on the death of the Duke of Clarence.
Premier Philp, Queensland, was at one
time a much-buffeted boy in the publishing room
of Brisbane Courier. Present Courier sub-editor
E. J. T. Barton and Philp were “ boys together.”
Ex-Governor Norman entertains hopes of
returning to Australia as Governor-General.
Queenslanders are pushing friends, and some of
them remember their old soldier with great affeo
tion.

GODDARD.—On 31st Dec., at Kyabram, the wife of
B. Goddard, sen. (the Kyabram reformer), of a son.
The great reformer is 70 years older than his new
son.
Of the handful of men who reached South
Australia by the pioneer immigrant ship Buffalo,
in 1836, half-a-dozen are still above-board, and
were hale enough to attend the last annual “com-
memoration ” at Glenelg, the landing-place, on
Dec. 28.
THE LATE SENATOR SARGOOD.
Some solemn humorists persuaded General
Hutton to lay a church foundation-stone at
Hobart t’other day. What a trial it must have
been to the good man to refrain from exhorting
the heavenly contingent to “Stand still, you
blankers, stand still ! ”
Vabien Solomon, once Premier of S.A.
for a week, will not stand again for the House of
Reps., as the splitting-up of the State into dis-
tricts will not suit him. He will run for the
Senate when next elections take place, the retiring
S.A. members of that chamber being, Downer,
K.C., Charleston and M‘Gregor.
Lieut. F. C. C. Pasco (son of that well-
known old Victorian identity, the late Captain
Crawford Pasco, R.N.), commanding H.M.S.
Dart, now at Hobart, lias been promoted to the
rank of commander. If heredity counts for any-
thing, Commander Pasco should get on in the navy.
His grandfather was Nelson’s flag-lieutenant.
Dr. H. A. Langley, C.E. Bishop of Ben-
digo, now visiting N.S.W., is brother of Arch-
deacon Langley, of Sydney. One was originally a
schoolmaster and the other a bank-clerk. They
are natives of Dungarvan (Ireland) but Austra-
lians from boyhood. Dr. Langley was invited to
Melbourne-side by Bishop Moorhouse and the
way to the Bishop’s apron was thus opened to
him.
Archdeacon Tyrrell resigns from East
Maitland after 40 years’ service* He is now 76.
The Tvrrells hold church seats long. His uncle,
the Bishop of Newcastle (N.S.W.), held that see
for about 30 years, and left a reputed quarter of a
million behind him for the use of the diocese. It
lias panned out much less, however; flood and
drought played havoo with the sheep and cattle i
en the estate.
Talking of the “ Festive Season ”
and its awful bearings on the human
stummick, a Melbournite is fond of tell-
mg how he dined one Xmas time with
the late Dean Macartney, of Melbourne.
. e old Dean was then well into the
nineties, and his guest took it for
granted that he wouldn’t want anything
more solid than bread and milk. But
the nonagenarian ate his dyspeptic
young friend to a standstill. Boast beef,
duck, pudding, mince-tart, cheese, des-
sert—everything that the Dean had pre-
viously asked the Lord to make him
truly thankful for he was truly thank-
ful for, because he sampled it. The
man who tells this inspiriting Xmas
story is not quite clear on one point.
Uwing to his dazed condition at the
time, he can’t now remember whether
the grand old man finished his repast
with filberts or dried figs.
For the holidays both Argus and
Age packed Kingston off to “ Merino,
in the Vic. western district, where he
spent his youth.” But he didn’t. It
was at Marino, down where the amphi-
theatre of hills that shut in Adelaide
comes down to the ocean on the south.
Here “Sir Paddy,” C. C. Kingston’s
father—for years S.A. Speaker, and a
fine old democrat for his day—planted
acres and acres of vines, and made such
wine that the fashion was set to plant
vines on all those rich black southern
slopes ; and now there are millions of
them in bearing. But, unfortunately,
the old Speaker (he was from Ireland,
and his wife from Spain) died in the
son’s warm-blooded days ; and
while the other and newer vine-
yards became mints, this pio-
neer among them became a
wilderness, and the cellars a
wreck. Only in recent years,
when he has wanted time to
think, has C.C. gone back to
the old ruined home and to the solitude
which he can command there.
The Bulletin’s old contributor,
Von Kotze, who has lately returned from a
trip round Africa, among other interesting
institutions met, at Lagos, William Mac-
gregor, one time boss of B.N. Guinea:—
He is quite as keen, energetic and Scotch as of yore, in
fact, as regards the latter point, even more so. For he
sallies out personally on market days to barter for the
luscious yam and the emaciated fowl—and he has taken
to wearing kilts. Fancy walking about with bare legs in
a more than tropical, mosquito-infested country'! Lagos
regards him accordingly with a mixture of awe and
ridicule. There exists a snapshot taken by some
irreverent subject of the mighty doctor, which depicts
him in the act of bending down over a heap of yams in
the public market, while a curious, naked little nigger-girl
of ten or thereabouts lifts up his kilt behind to see what
the strange garment is. Unfortunately, 1 couldn’t get hold
of a copy. Anyhow, Mac is doing good work in Lagos,
draining the place and cleaning it of tins and empty
bottles and suchlike breeding-places of the malaria-dis-
tributing anophiles, and he swears he’ll make the colony,
formerly a hotbed of fever, as healthy yet as the Blue
Mountains or total abstinence or an Australian pensioner.
A glance at Henry Parkes’s Ministry of 1G
years ago shows how evanescent political reputa-
tions are in N.S. W. Parkes, Garrett, Sutherland
dead ; Bums, Inglis, Clarke, Abigail, politically
dead ; Roberts and Salomons flickering like
political rushlights ; the only man of them alive
and forcefully living in the Parliamentary world
being B. R. Wise.
Victorian Legislative Council unknowingly
showed its gratitude to Frederick Sargood, who
led it along its Tory way so long, by postponing
the new Duties on Probates Bill over the
Christmas adjournment. Had the Bill passed at
the date originally intended the deceased Senator’s
estate would have paid an extra two per cent,
duty, a matter, certainly, of five figures.
Justice Moorhead, of W.A., who died
lately, was one of the few Roman Catholics who
have become Supreme Court Judges in Australia.
In Victoria, there hasn’t been one. N. S. Wales
has had Milford (?) and Faucett; Queensland has
Power and Real; South Australia and Tasmania
have had none. On Vic. County Court bench,
however, there have been several R.C.’s.
The Bulletin’s old friend, artist F. P.
Mahon* writes cheerfully from London, where
he has waoome a contributor to several papers
including The Graphic and Wide World
ZINE. He nas joined the Langham Arts Club
(mainly freoneiHed by high-up professionals), and
there spends several evenings a week at the
costume or nude classes. Mr. Mahony will con-
tinue his Bulletin contributions.
“Tom” Bennison, the new Mayor of
Hobart, is son-in-law of Alderman Pigdon, who,
away in the Seventies, was Mayor of Melbourne
and laid the first stone of the new Eastern
Markets. He can add 2 and 2, has a taste for
tree-planting, and is an educated man. He lives
in the biggest house in Hobart (except Govt.
House), and lets the Admiral of the Squadron
occupy it while the flagship is in Hobart port.
SO AWKWARD.
MRS. SOROY : "Do you know, Mr. Tenter, if the
Divorce Court doesn't stop work Til have to
stop giving dinners. Last night I put Mrs.
Lyon's previous husband next to her at table,
and Mr. Denison was opposite to his last co-
respondent. And it was so awkward.
For the first time since Maoris were
granted special representation in M.L. Parlia-
ment all four members lately returned are Govt,
followers, and all are full-blooded. The mode of
voting at native elections should be amended.
There are no voting papers. The voter gives his
name, tribe, family and abode. These are entered
in a book; also the name of the candidate he
favors, and then the weary returning-officer says
“Next, please,” and starts afresh.
Those petitions for the appointment of
King Dick Seddon as next Governor of Maori-
land will be of no avail. The Colonial Office will
never, if it can help it, appoint any man as vice-
roy of a country in which he formerly retailed
grog, no matter how good that grog may have
been. But nothing would please the Maoris
better than to see Richard of Kumara taking on
Ranfurly’s job. Seddon’s enormous avoirdupois
finds great favor with the natives.
Now that Seddon is casting round for
a fresh advt. (the latest move concerning him is a
petition that he be made Governor of Maoriland
after Lord Ranfurly’s term), he might consider
the chances of being worshipped by a new sect of
Maori fanatics. The star of the Hau-haus has
sunk ; and there is room on the horizon for the
sun of the Dick-Seds. A lot of Maori enthusiasm
(and "isli) is being wasted on Mormon missionaries
whit might well be diverted to a nobler object.
Sedduu has the physique of a prophet, and any
Wellington journalist could write his gospel.
Anything with free love and free drinks in it
might do. And think of the Glory !
'Reported that a new Irish saint is to be
canonised—St. Oliver Plunket. The name doesn’t
go well somehow, and when St. William Smith
comes along he will go still worse. It is curious
how many ecclesiastical things sound feasible
just because they are old. St. George of
Cappadocia goes well enough, just because
Cappadocia is reminiscent of the first century,
but St. George Jones of Boggabri would make the
whole fabrio of churchism totter. . . And there
are thousands and thousands of believing people
whose entire scheme of unquestioning faith would
fall to pieces if St. Paul telephoned to the
Ephesians. That one touch of modernism would
be fatal.
Dr. Emily Constance Stone, who died the
3ther day, was Melbourne’s first lady physician.
When she put up her brass plate, “Constance
Stone,” strangers did not always recognise the
name as feminine. On one occasion a R.C. priest
went into her waiting-room, and, in course of con-
versation with a lady patient, he learned that the
doctor was a woman. “Great Heavens ! ” he ex-
claimed, in wild confusion, cramming his hat on
his head, and rushing out of the house.
A letter received in Sydney indicates that
Bell, of the notorious firm of Bell and Davis, was
on the island of Martinque, and was lucky enough
to clear out before the disastrous eruption. Bell,
the letter conveys, is now on his own, having a
couple of years ago separated from Davis. The
pair, it may be remembered, swindled Sydney
right and left, and succeeded in departing (in a
yacht with much gold aboard) with effusive pro-
sssions of admiration from their victims.
Dr. Coekburti is falling further and further
from grace Last mail tells that he is using all
his influence (he is a graduate and a member of
the council of London University) to secure the
return of Prof. Philip Magnus, a Tory, to repre-
sent that ’Varsity in the Commons. This, too,
although a Liberal is also contesting the vacancy
caused by the withdrawal of Prof. Michael Foster.
It is hard to remember that Cockburn was once
more Radical than even Kingston himself.
Mr. Jabez Wright, whose name now crops
up in the newspapers as the victim of matrimonial
infelicity, was once the uncrowned king of Broken
Hill. A strenuous and masterful, though poorly-
educated undertaker, he made his coffin-shop the
hub of the political labor movement on the
Barrier, and many an acute move was planned
whilst Jabez nailed down the lid. He rose to be
Mayor labor Mayor of Broken Hill; then was
rejected for Parliament, and found his power
waning. Business used often to send Mr. Wright
to Adelaide or Sydney, but (he once assured the
writer) he was never happy out of Broken Hill.
That was Heaven to him—that and a popular
undertaking business where people die frequently.
Though out of regular harness, the hand
of ex-editor Windsor, lately returned from his
trip ’Ome, is again to be detected in Melb. Age.
There is no mistaking Windsor’s style—that of a
man who might easily have achieved literary
celebrity as one of the finest essayists of recent
years in England. He is most at home on scien-
tific subjects, which he treats in a vein of delight-
ful humor, that contrasts strangely with the hard-
hitting leaders with which he used to destroy
politicians when D. Syme was quite done with
them. A search through Age files would bring
to light enough matter for three or four books of
essays, sufficient to light a lamp in front of the
modest Mr. Windsor’s name for many years to
cr
A NON-SMOKER.— (AS OTHERS SEE US.)
WILLIE (a cigarette-fiend): "Don't yer old man
smoke?"
BILLIE (ditto): "No; he just sucks a bloomin'
pipe."
The last three Speakers of Yic. Assembly
have been men who, for the whole or greater part
of their lives, devoted themselves to politics.
Graham Berry was in the 50’s a grocer at South
Yarra, and in the early 60’s a journalist at Col-
lingwood and Geelong; F. C. Mason was a
schoolmaster on board the training-ship in Hob-
son’s Bay, away in the 60’s; Duncan Gillies was
a miner when the 50’s were still running. But in
later life they have all three been “gentlemen,”
and professors of politics.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors.— ln the case ofi ethnological and
natural history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience ;or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific
value.
Among all the rain-recording stations in
N. S.W. the driest last year was Pooncarrie, on
the Darling, with less than 3| inches for the year.
The ironical “ Handbook ” says that Pooncarrie is
‘ pleasantly situated on rising ground ” 346 mile 3
from a railway station, and has a population of
“ about 50 ” —5O dried-up mortals who crackle
when they move and dry-blow each other on the
pleasant rising ground. Then comes Hillston, on
the Lachlan, with less than 4|in. Ivanhoe,
Milparinka, and Wentworth follow, all with less
than sin. Orange, one of the claimants for the
billet of Federal capital, had a little over 20in. ;
Bombala, another claimant, 26£in. For long-
continued dryness—the last year when Bar-
ringun scored lOin. of rain was 1898 ; Gongalgon’s
last ten-inch year was 1899 ; Louth’s 1898,
Menindie’s 1894, Milparinka’s 1894, Pooncarrie’s
1894, Tilpa’s 1898, Wanaaring’s 1896, Wilcannia’s
1895. At 23 stations there was more rain in 1902
than 1901 ; at 105 stations 1902 was drier than
1901. It was a miserable year, but the misery
seems to have had its hub and centre in that town
which is situated on rising ground—a live coal
rising out of Hades—by the Darling.

“Barcoo”: Re beast-identification (8.,
27/12/’O2). It is not unusual for an owner to
positively identify and swear to a “stolen beast”
which carried neither earmarks nor brand. At
Ipswich (Q.) criminal sessions, about five years
ago, a noted cattle-“ duffer” and a boy were
charged with stealing some of “Jim” Tyson’s
unbranded cattle from Meteor Downs station,
beyond Mitchell. When asked if he could swear
to an unbranded beast, Tyson replied, “Yes, cer-
tainly ; I can swear to my own cattle ; and there’s
some of ’em outside in that yard.” Both prisoners
were convicted —the senior afterwards died in
Brisbane gaol. I also remember a case wherein a
“squatteroo” swore to 10 stolen geese out of a
flock of 50. On the 10 bung caught and
handled, he justified his oath by showing some
indigo ink marks on the under-tip of the wing.

Been so ill I couldn’t before tackle “ A. 0.” (B. 29/11/’O2)
who asserted that he’d seen and caught hundreds of hare-

* * * •
A Rockhampton auctioneer has lately been
selling lucerne grown in Chili without rain. And
Rockhampton has been living for months on
vegetables grown out on the Alice River without
rain. There’s a lesson in this somewhere for the
Bananalander, but now that W.Q. has had a
couple of inches of rain all the lessons of the
drought will be forgotten in a month or two.
• » • •
“Weribene”: The bottom has already
fallen out of a part of N.S.W. On the Upper
Shoalhaven is a vacant spot in the earth’s surface,
occupying about half an acre in a roughly circular
form. It is on the E. slope of a solid sandstone
hill, and the sides go down perpendicularly for
over 200 ft. Then occurs an underlay to the east-
ward, and all to be seen is blackness. Whole
trees have been cast into this place—in fact, all
growing handy, yet there is nothing visible from
the top. Some few years back a party of six
descended by rope about 300 ft., and were not
sorry to find themselves safe,on the surface again,
although they discovered nothing uncanny.

“Bunny S.” : That mustering 25-mile-
yarn (B. 10/5/’O2) is either a lie or means nothing
worth recording. Depends on circumstances. If
there was a big spread on the sheep the men could
not have sighted and “ cleaned ” every part of the
paddock—the true definition of mustering—in
time to count 80,000 sheep—six or seven hours’
work for a good counter. On the other hand, if
the men simply rode into the paddock, and
dropped on the sheep pretty handy—within a two
or three mile radius of the water, for instance, put
them together, and ran them through the counting-
gate—then there was “nothing in it.”

Concerning Barton Bros.’ mule, now
starring with Fitzgerald Bros.’ Circus, thus the
Age :
Barton’s turn was brief, but particularly lively. He
introduced a villainous-looking bare-backed mule named
“ Barney ” at the end of a halter,
and informed the audience that
Barney was “ hard to ride.” As
he turned round to make this
observation Barney took advan-
tage of the opportunity to
attempt to batter in the back
of Barton’s head with his fore-
feet. ‘ ‘ He’s a playful animal,
and likes to stroke me some-
times,” explained Barton, as he
moved away a few yards. Barton
didn t attempt- to ride the mule
himself. He considered it less
trouble to offer jEI to any person
who wanted the money. Such
an offer amongst a crowd of Aus-
tralians couldn’t go long un-
accepted. A young fellow
stepped into ths ring, divested
himself of his coat, hat and
neckerchief, spat on his hands,
and advanced. As he took hold
of the surcingle to mount, the
mule revolved upon his own
axis with the velocity of a milk-
separator. the young man stood
oh to cons.der the situation,
and when he tried to get a
fresh hold, th ■ mule reached out
and tapped him on the shin.
As he hopped out of the ring he
yelled back at the shrieking
audience, “ Some o' yous clever
blokes come down and tackle ’im.
P’raps ridin’ inules is dead easy
to them as know the way. I’m a
’orseuian meseif.” A picturesque-
looking young fellow, who had been -
foremost in ottering advice to the de-
feated candidate, then came forward.
He carried a certificate of character from
“out back’’ in his appearance ; he wore
a red shirt with blue pockets; his red
hair had evidently been cut with a pair of
shears at his last shed. He astonished the
mule by mounting with a flying bound, and
the mule astonished him by dismounting him
with a flying buck. “ I wasn’t on him at all,”
i said the rider, as he shook the sawdust out
of his ears. “ I’ll stick to him this time.” But it was
novuse. Nothing buta well-established blister could keep
on- that mu.e’s back. After five plucky tries the ex-
h ms ed candidate gave up, and he exclaimed breath-
lessly, “I came here to ride a ’orse. That’s not a ’orse ;
mat’s an electric disturbance.” Barton passed the mule
out, and save a Ciever exhibition of buck-jumping on a
cream-colored pony, which he rote with a flank rope.
The pony backed cleverly, but Compared with the mule
lie was the merest circumstance.

tralian of some wild man-eating animal to give a

f ation of the coast, besides being too s.u gisli
for ordinary use. Why not try the iguana?

The other day, as I was riding along the Uratm road
from Deniliquin, I noticed as big goanna making frantic
dashe-at a box-tree oil the side of the road, and upon
invest gation found that lie had attempted to swallow a
good-sized-rabbit whole, but had only managed to get
half of it doVn, and was trying to knock the'rest down
by bashing into the tree. Also, I have seen a goanna
chase a man for nearly 20J yards, stopping at last to
worry the man’s hat, which had blown off. A cyclist
tried ta rescue the hat, but was chased in bis turn.
With a little imaginative perseverance, an iguaua
which would stick up a buggy and bellow for
human blood might be evolved.
BORROWING EXPERIENCE.
RIDER: “ Great Scott! that blessed new-chum’s
taken your horse instead of his own. The
beggar doesn't know one horse from another!"
FLAPHAT: “ Anyway, he knows NOW!”
The worst and more permanent effects of
the drought are other than the disappearance of
the grass and water. The vanishing of the
eaten-off and dried-up roots causes the trouble
now. T’other day a brisk breeze swept across N.
Victoria for a few hours, but those few hours
sufficed to pile up the sand on the railway lines at
Birchip, Donald, Woomelang, and Swan Hill
sufficiently to block the trains for many hours:
while at Lake Charm the line was covered 4ft-
deep in shifting sand, and only a train’s length
could be cleared at a time ! A gang of 14 men is
now regularly employed in keeping the Bendigo
to Swan Hill line clear of sand.
• • • »
During the drought a driver in charge of a
big lot of sheep for a Diamantina firm was in-
structed to secure some of the good, unoccupied
northern country as a permanent run. He got it
all right—in his own name; then he demanded
£5 10s. per thousand per month, in advance, for
agistment. The manager, however, secured
another drover, and took up better country ad-
joining, shaving off the one good water-hole,
which the first man thought he had taken in. The
latter has now a lot of cheap country to let. He
also wants a job.
* * * •
A Mount Chalmers (Q.) butcher notifies
that he will soon have to shut up, but will do his
best to procure a supply of hogs, goats and
wallabies, and keep going a while longer. If
some of the wild-west drovers would bring in a
few good mobs of wallabies from the back scrubs,
he could, he thinks, just about pull his customers
through the bad times. He also anticipates that
next big drought will see Queensland marsupials
ear-marked and branded.
* * • •
Writes “ Eiddal ” :
Mulga, saltbush, sucker-scrub, and other makeshift
fodders are better than nothing, but where any moisture
is available, lucerne takes the cake. Seven years ago a
Carag Carag (Vic.) cookie, on the main channel, sowed 40
acres, and, during the past bad seasons, he has been the
only cockie within a radius of 20 miles that has had a
dairy-herd in full profit. He has never had occasion to
hand-feed ; while the majority of his neighbors have lost
half their stock (after stuffing' them with twice their value
in chaff). Another case : a few years back Gibson Bros.,
of Top Creek, sowed several paddocks with lucerne, and
have gone to the expense of irrigating these patches.
Result—while the surrounding land is bare as the bones
of their neighbors’ stock, the Gibsons have been regularly
trucking fat stock to market and re-stocking with N.S. W.
starvers, turning over their cheques every few weeks.

“ A.M.” : Be the aboriginal shell deposits
on the Californian coast, which have yielded many
scientifically valuable relics of ancient Indian
races. It .is said that there are near Ballina,
N.S.W., similar deposits, due to the feasting of
our aborigines there for ages. Possibly these may
be worth the attention of the Anthropological
Society of Australasia.

“Fitz”: Beef is scarce and families are
large on the Riverina, and when a visitor strikes
the cocky’s hospitable home at dinner
hour he may receive a lesson in the art
of stretching the meagre joint. 1 did
once. Said the head of the family:
“ Will you have some meat, Willie ? ”
“Yes, please, father.” In a louder
and severer manner father repeated
the question. Willie replied : “Yes,
please, father.” Then the father
spQke again with a terrible voice,
and a look of warning: “Will you
have some meat, Willie ? ” This
line Willie woke up to the situation
with a start and answered “No,
father, thank you,” and father said—
“ That’s right, my boy, I always
like you to speak out plait 1/
when ye’re asked a simple ques-
tion.” The others took the hint
and spoke out plainly and made a
meal on damper and syrup, cheaper and more
filling than meat !

“ Prospector ” on ants (from Mt. Weld,
W. A.): —
Mostly all the box-treea are hollowed out by white
ants. When black ants get in they go straight for whites
and chew their heads off. White ants, too, are pretty
tough in the jaw. I once stuck a new axe in a white-
anty tree, went to pull it out next day, and it came out
minus an inch strip off the blade. When I chopped the
tree down I found a handful of steel borings.
» » • »
“ Myall ” : Rt peddling tramps. I once
knew an artful bagman who, when things were
bad, would grind two bricks together, bottle the
dust, and offer it from house to house as the
“Great American Mosquito and Fly Eradicator ”
at 2s. 6d. a bottle. He had a good gab and a per-
suasive manner, and the bogus powder found sale.
Once, however, a burly bullock-driver reviled the
vendor as a fraud, but the latter, who had
been imbibing freely, took the bullocky’s wrath as
a good joke. “ Won’t kill ’em ! Why you
couldn’t ’ave used it right, old man. You’ve got
to tickle their ribs till they laugh, and then throw
the powder down their throats and choke ’em.”
The bullocky couldn’t stand that, and a four-
round fight ensued, in which the ferocious cattle-
puncher got all the worst of it.
* • * •
“Mick’' : I was camped out last Dec.
sleeper-getting for Narrabri-Walgett railway, and
one very hot day my tent was taken possession of
by parrots, magpies, and other birds, and my cat,
who at other times would climb trees for a bird,
was so done up with the heat that he took no
notice of them, although they were perched on my
bunk. In fact he lay within a few feet of them
for hours, but when the cool of the afternoon came
and the birds flew away, Tom also pulled himself
together and looked as if he regretted neglected
opportunities.
• * * «
The big crocodile which lately popped
forth from the Endeavor River and gohhled a
piccaninny who was sprawling on the beach near
its mother was, perhaps, the monster—24ft. long
—which has been promised as a captive to J. A.
Miller, of Melbourne. A while ago, “Jimmy”
Miller aforesaid was embarrassed by the receipt
of a 12ft. crocodile, and the donor, in sending
it along to Mellwurne, stated that he hoped to
land the 24ft. specimen shortly. Miller being a
peaceable show-printer, with no use for crocodiles,
transferred hie drowsy consignment to the Mel-
bourne Zoo, but it died of self-starvation in the
usual way. Huge crocs, rarely consent to keep
themselves alive in captivity for the amusement
of visitors.

AUSTRALIAN ESE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Two youths I met outside a pub.,
Long, shambling youths were both ;
I asked if they were natives, and
The longer said “My oath I"
“ O lanky friends,” I said again,
“ What toil are you at now ?
Are you with stock upon the road ! ”
The shorter one said “Yowl"
I told them that I guessed it by
The dust upon their clothes ;
The longer said their flock was but
A thousand blanky “ Yoes.”
We drifted in and drank a while
(’Twas at Taranganba);
I said, “ I guess the roads are bad,”
And both of them said, “Yah!”
And then we had just one more drink,
And parted on the step.
I said, “I trust we’ll meet again.”
They proudly answered, “ Yep ! ”
J. H.

The drought has almost ex-
terminated the bee 3 in the bush. Very rare
nowadays to find a bee’s nest, and then it seldom
contains much honey. Except in the coastal dis-
tricts the wild bee is practically extinct in N S W
S , years ago, throughout the central division
of JN.S. W., as much honey could be gathered in a
week by a couple of men as would last their
.amilies a year. At present it would take a year
to get a week’s supply.

“Merton”: During the burning of the
Union flour mills, at Ararat ("Vic.), a cat, driven
to the top of the building by the flames, leaped
from the window to the ground, a distance of
about 60ft., without injury. It was seen next
lay busily engaged hunting burnt-out mice, quite
bund and happy.

A railroading incident. Writes “ Loco ” :
Driving my engine, t'other day, arrived at a place
where a man had his sht ep feeding on the railway line. As
our Crain neared, the generally quiet enough mob made a
move, as though to “ break ” over the line. The man got
excited and sent his dog to head them off. Dog seemed
to get excited too, but, as the train rapidly approached,
the sheep seemed suddenly to realise their danger and
stood still, leaving the dog standing fair between the
rails. Man waved his arms and seemingly shouted (noise
of engine too great to hear him). Dog looked at man,
then at sheep, then at us—a look that said as plainly as
words : “ 1 must stay or the sheep will rush in front‘and
be killed.” Hiss-s-s-s-s-s went the air from the driver’s
brake-valve, and the mighty “ Westinghouse ” put such a
check upon about 250 tons of dead w r eight, moving
at 30 miles an hour, that the cow-catcher only ius't
bumped the dog gently aside.

Vic. streams are almost devoid of fish
since indiscriminate netting, trapping, and night-
lines have been winked at. Recently the police
were appointed Fishery Inspectors, bub their
unpaid zeal is sadly intermittent.

Major Laylor, whose portrait appears in
this issue, is champion bike sprinter of Europe and
America, and is striving hard for the Australasian
supremacy. He is not technically world’s cham-
pion, for the reason that he would not compete
owing to the race I icing held on Sunday : but a
couple of days later he met and defeated Ellegaard
the winner, twice out of three times. He is a
fun-blooded negro of medium height, heavily but
well-built, who has judicio ;sly trained and
developed a much-envied pair of legs.

i„™ V i <len > Ce seems to have some especial down on Method-
ists Sunday-school picnics. Where’s the friction—kiss-in-
the-nng, or the raspberry vinegar?. .John. J. : Nobody can
sneeze satisfactorily with his pipe in his mouth. Go
t™ a °° ol P lace an< ? Ponder this dark saying
k- V",’ abe lnar ? wbo writes this has seen many executions
and has experience of many memorable murder-trials
and he can lay his hand on his heart and conscien-
tiously say that he knows of more than one Australian
case where the prospect of capital punishment caused
the jury or juries to finally disagree—which is pretty well
the same as acquittal so far as the community is con-
cerned. Not so very many years ago as cold-blooded,
cruel, calculating, treacherous, yet withal clumsy, a
murderer as ever stood behind spikes-a murderer who
had probably been guilty of other similar murders—
escaped scot-free, purely because of jurors' especial repug-
nance to capital punishment in certain cases. Two
of the jurors, questioned separately, told present
writer so, and the public and the Crown Law authori-
ties could see no other reason for the failure to con-
vict. Were the question of death or life (or other) im-
prisonment left to the jury, no man or woman would
ever be hanged who had not deliberately committed
murder for what is regarded as an entirely sane motive-
such as the sake of gain. Let us get that reform-advo-
cated in these columns for many years—and there will be
very little discussion about capital punishment. As
things stand, some people are sent to death by juries act-
lng more or less under the influence of an oath repugnant
to them ; but when the average jury, having the "clear
option of sparing a man’s life, sent him to the scaffold
after a dispassionately-conducted trial, you might depend
on it that little sympathy would be felt for hini by
the general community. This paper thoroughly believes
m the jury - system, but it wants to see un-
Junes, especially in cases of life-or-death.

Meyer Altson, latest winner of the triennial
Travelling Scholarship offered to students at the
Vic. Art Gallery, is younger than any of his five
predecessors. His brother, “ Abey” Altson, who
came second on the roll of prize-winners (after
John Longstaff), was 23 years of age at the time.
Meyer is now only half-past 21, though he has
been a persevering worker for six years. To
speak quite candidly, the picture that captured
the scholarship from seven or eight rival com
MEYER ALTSON, Winner of the Vic. National
Art Gallery Travelling Scholarship.
Photo . by Talma , Sydney & Melbourne .
petitors is plainly the work of a very youthful
artist. It shows much promise, but is crude in
all-round effect as compared with the “Breaking?
the News,” which sent student Longstaff to Europe
15 years ago. Meyer Altson, taking “A Leisure
xiour for his subject,, laid the scene in an artist’s
studio. The nude girl model is leisuring on a
couch, with a rug thrown over the lower half of
her nakedness, and an illustrated paper in her
hand. Iwo men talking “shop” are placed
behind the couch. Another fellow, further away is
presumably leaning lazily against the wall. *A
painter, seated in the foreground, has his back
turned to the outside.world, and doesn’t make it
quite clear that he is the painter of the picture
which stands on the easel near him. Perspective
is weak, drawing very good as a rule, coloring
unsatisfactory. The flesh tint of. the girl’s neck
and body, as far as it goes, seems rather inhuman
and her commonplace face looks as though she
bad bought its bloom from a chemist. One of the
critics at the back —the one who wears an air of
profound artistio thought—is the happiest figure
in the work. Meyer Altson will do something
much better than this ere his three years of
scholarship travel have expired. He is bright and
earnest, and, as his name implies, a Jew not
Christianised, but genuine. The love of art and
the genius for art are tempered with worldlv dis-
cretion in the Hebrew young man.

ami lies work very hard ; but the women here are not inclined to work in that way
course, sometimes, it must be very difficult for farmers to keep their women.”

donkey, the house-dog, and even the family goat. It is picturesque, it is angelic,
we R Australian women and dogs are not educated up to that at present

A SIMILE AND A MORAL.—“What the
dustman is to the dwelling in which we live, that
Hunyadi Janos is to the local habitation of the
human spirit.” Thus, writes a well-known
medical writer.*
Mr. SAMUEL WORMLEY, Florin, Pa.,
writes :
“I have used Jayne’s Expectorant for ASTHMA and
for SEVERE COLDS, and CANNOT FIND WORDS
STRONG ENOUGH to recommend it.”
To rheumatic sufferers, and people in general:
Australian Boards-of-llealth might as well take up the
interesting question of the “ Dr. Deimel Underwear,”
lately introduced to this market by Rochussen Bros., of
Melbourne. Dr. Deimel and his linen-mesh garments
first came into notice some three or four years ago, and it is
claimed that they have already shaken the doctrine of wool
under-clothing to its foundation in America and England.
The Deimelites contend that whereas linen, in itself, was
always recognised as the best of materials forunderwear—
better than wool if it could be woven into a porous fabric
—the manufacturing difficulty prevented it from taking
its proper place next to the skin, until Dr. Deimel, “ after
exhaustive trials, succeeded in obtaining a yarn which,
after being twisted into a composite thread, could be
knit by special machines, into a uniformly porous and
pliable fabric of unrivalled durability.” Deimel, anyhow,
has created a yarn which is very persuasive, on paper.
Wool, he argues, is the right material for outer clothing,
on account of its slow conducting and absorbing power,
and the wrong thing for underwear, because it obstructs
the evaporation of moisture from the skin. Fabrics
intended for absorbing moisture—towels and handker-
chiefs—are made of flax. In the power of evaporating
moisture flax comes first. It will dry much quicker tham
wool, cotton, or silk. But, for underwear purposes, the
garment flax, i.e., linen, must be made porous, so that
the air contained in its meshes, becoming warmed by ths
heat of the body, will prevent the cold feeling that one
gets from a close-woven linen shirt. The Deimel Under-
wear, then, is put forward as being the best for all
seasons. Also, notwithstanding than the price of vest
and pants is 10s. for each garment, the local apostle of
Deimelism—Mr. Rochussen, aforesaid—alleges that these
linen-mesh garments are infinitely cheaper than cotton
and merino because they will last for ten years. They
haven’t been manufactured for ten or even 5 years as yet,
therefore this allegation is mainly prophetic. Neverthe-
less, their durability is undoubted, and their other virtues
have been freely testified to by many people. Judd,
M.D., lecturing in New York, declared that he ceased
to be a martyr to rheumatism and never caught cold any
more after putting himself into “Deimels.” The new
theory is worthy of attention from rheumatical subjects
in particular and reasonable mortals in geneial.*

The Bulletin’s London correspondent: •* If the vocal-
istic tutors of ‘ Down Under’would give up cultivating
mythical Melbas and ‘ cronk ’ Crossleys and go in for
developing lion comiques, character comedians, etc it
would be wiser and more profitable. Anyone is heard’at
the London ‘ halls ’ once a week by the managers, without
the audience. If successful, billed as an ‘ extra turn ’
the aspiiant flutters at night when an engagement
depends on his song and style. The ‘ halls ’ jump at
a good man or woman. Finally, Dan Leno gets £l5O and
George Robey £l3O. This is real money, not mere talk.”
An artist who lately supported a certain famous per-
foimer is famed for the good opinion he has of himself
Some kind friends thought to “ take a rise out of” him’
and went to some trouble in proving that the singer had
evinced an extraordinary liking for him. Their victim
waited for them to finish, and then solemnly drawled
out:—“Well, you quite surprise me. In Sydney she
called me a silly fool and in Melbourne a damned ass.”
Australian managers have to support a dreadful
number of imported “ scraggs.” The clever comedian
generally has a wife who doesn’t even possess the merit
of being beautiful. On the other hand, the favorite
comedienne carries round with her, as often as not, a
husband who is not the least bit funny as a comedian.
Unfortunately the “ encumbrance ” of either sex is not
content to draw a salary for keeping off the board.
S.A. has some sporting clergymen, who are fond of
moderate whisky and a rubber of whist; several who
attend the theatre on slight provocation, and (a few
years back) one who went racing and had his occasional
sov. on the tote. But a new line has been opened up by
the Port Augusta cleric who played Romeo to the lpcal
tragedienne’s Juliet.
“ P.A.L.” : Re Kyrle Bellew as a good advertiser. On
going to a Melbourne theatre to write a paragraph about
the performances as reporter for the local Daily
Telegraph, it was his custom to take a front seat in the
D.C., place a heap of “ copy ’’-paper on his knee, and
scribble furiously every three minutes. When he was
appointed fourth mate on board an Australian liner, he
immediately donned the uniform and paraded Oollins-st.
for the whole afternoon.
Oinquevalli, speaking of Rickards’ show in Melbourne
what time he was appearing there, remarked that Willie
and Charlie Schenk, the “head-balancers,” were abso-
lutely the most wonderful performers in their line whom
he (Cinque.) had ever met or heard of. Uniil he saw the
work of the Schenks he didn’t think it could be done
with such grace and apparent ease. Yet Australian
audiences are disposed to accept the said Willie and
Charlie as a mere “ good turn ’’—very clever, but nothing
extraordinary. Even a European reputation when
known to be genuine, is apt to be underrated in "•
Australia, while local talent is almost invariably belittled
on account of its local training. The young performers
who have grown up with Fitzgeralds’ Circus are probably
unsurpassable as equestrians and all-round athletes.
They can do a “jockey act,” a “ cycle whizz,” or what
not in brilliant style, but the Public hereabouts never
thinks of “starring” those versatile artists in its own
Australian estimation.
Old Alberto Zelman, the exquisitely tailored and
barbered musician fainilar to Melb. hlockites, has just
completed the composition ot a new Mass, which is likely
to be given a show at one of the big city churches shortly.
Zelman is much given to the composition of R.C.
church music, flavored with a characteristic Italian
allowance of florid melody that would do just as well
for opera. His work is generally done at the very
last moment. A year ago, Zelman was commis-
sioned by Glen’s to write an “ Ave Maria” for a
proposed Amy Castles’ souvenir book. The night
before the book was to be sent to the
printer, son Bertie, the violinist, arrived
home, and woke the old man up at
midnight to tell him Ihe composition
was required at once. Zelman had
forgotten all about his bargain, but
got up, arrayed himself in his most
gorgeous garments, combed his
silky beard and hair, and set to
work. Three hours later the new
“Ave Maria’’was ready. But,
alas ! through some hitch, the
Amy Castles souvenir never
came out as such, and Zel-
man’s melody was pub-
lished later on with a
dedication to another
soprano.
Ajax, the contor-
tionist, an occasional
performer with Fitz-
geralds’ Circus during
the last few years, has
sold his Melb. pub., at a
profit, and is going to
America under professional
engagement. Little Ajax
has not visibly aged since
the Fitz’s first imported him
as one of the Eclair Bros.
Contorting seems to agree
with him ; and there’s good
money in it for a first-class
man.
“T.D.”: The audacity of
the deadhead. 1 watched
three of them at a Melb.
theatre t’other night. They
said the manager had in-
structed them to ask for
seats in the front row of the
circle, near the middle. Three
seats were vacant, these being
reserved by a leading hotel, but
the deadheads insisted upon
their “ rights,” and threatened to
appeal to the boss because they
were put in the second row. When
the curtain rose they proceeded to
jump the unoccupied seats, but were
intercepted by three people who had
bought the seats. More expostulation
and general disturbance of the dress-circle
for five minutes followed, at the eml of which time the
manager fortunately arrived and took his deadhead
friends round to a box, where they luxuriated and
preened themselves haughtily for the rest of the evening,
and no doubt considered tnemselves much better than
the casual people who paid for seats.
Vic. Public Works Secretary Martin was a prominent
amateur actor in Melbourne in the good old days when
half the populace was trying to emulate G. V. Brooke and
Charles Kean. As such, he had the run of the stage
entrances, and had one or two funny experiences. Once,
when Willie Edouin, since a big man in London, was
playing the part of a sailor-bo.v in correct marine rig,
Martin found the youthful alleged tar peeping at the
audience t trough the usual hole in the curtain before it
rose. With a ‘Ship ahoy!” Martin playfully smacked
him in a convenient spot. He was flabbergasted, however,
to see Julia Matthews turn round, and, rubbing the
assaulted part, exclaim, “Now, then ! mind what you’re
up to." Mr. Martin had forgotten that there were two
sailor-boys in that play, and that the fair Julia was one
of them.
