“ E.F.” ; The fiction that the obviously-Cockney Maud
Hobson was born at Toorak or Footscray, or some other
Melbourne district, originated in the fact that Melba took
her professional name from her native city When
Melbas European celebrity was in its earliest stage, an
English lady with a beautiful voice came, to Melbourne
" k u er husband > and took a house in the Elsternwick
suburb. She, copying Melba, presently appeared as a
public singer under the name of Marie Elster. The
origin of her professional title was widely paragraphed in
Australian papers, and subsequently in English papers
after she had gone ’Orae and become a prima
donna in a travelling opera co. Just about that
time Williamson and Musgrove imported the “Gaiety
Girl” co., with Maud Hobson, Grace Palotta, Blanche
Massey, Decima Moore, and other pretty women in the
crowd. As soon as the names of the ‘forthcoming co.
were cabled to Australia, a watchful paragraphist seized
upon the notion that Maud Hobson was a native of
Melbourne who had taken her stage name from Hobson’s
Bay. On the arrival of the co. at Sydney an interviewer
questioned fair Maud as to her Hobson’s Bay origin, and
she, being already advised of the fable, supported it.
Perhaps the stage-manager gave the interesting visitor
her cue. Anyway, Maud was ready with a cram. She
said she had been born in Melbourne, but her
parents took her to England when she was a
child ; therefore she didn’t remember anything
about her birthplace. La belle Hobson, as a matter of
fact, is a Cockney of Cockneys. When she first joined the
Gaiety Theatre “chorus” (in which she played silent
leg-parts for years ere she came out as an actress) she was
said to be a distant relative of John Hollingshed, the
manager. Perhaps she was, or, more likely, she wasn’t.
Inasmuch as she entered the Gaiety chorus under the
commonplace name of Hobson (long before “ Melba ”*nd
“ Elster” had been adopted as professional titles) it seems
probable that that was her real parental name.
THE RISE O’? PATRICK
FAT.
JOHN CHINAMAN (chaffed about
having christened his son Plecceman-Patrick): “My boy,
grow up stlong, wantec be pl-Patlick; he
wantee go into seven shillin' a day—Patlick; he
wantee boss public'ouse-Patlick. All'll Patlick."
George Moore's portrait (in “Mike Fletcher ”) of Peggy
Pryde : “She was followed by the joyous Peggy Pryde
(sweet minx), the soul and voice of the small back streets.
Screwing up her winsome, comical face, drawling a word
here, accentuating a word there, she evoked in an illusive
moment the washing day, the quarrel with the mother-
in law (who wanted to sleep in the house), tea-time, and
the trip to the seaside, with all its concomitant adven-
tures amid bugs and landladies. With an accent, with a
gesture, she recalled in a moment a phase of life, creating
pictures vivid as they were transitory, but endowing
each with the charm of the best and most highly-finished
works of the Dutch masters."
The most amusing story of the Nance O’Neil *' fiasco ■
—as the London newspapers called it, for want of a
newer word—came from a member of the co. in a letter
to an Australian friend : “ Rankin engaged 90 supers,
hordes of iron-workers, carpenters, &c., and decided to
do ‘ Elizabeth ’ on the smartest American principles.
-None except the stage hands and the smiling band were
paid. The poor idiots—the actors—were not even
approached by M'Kee at any period of the game. On the
fourth night of ‘ Elizabeth,’ during the first act, a hoarse
voice called out from the lime room : ‘Take off those
blanky limes ! ’ It was the cry of the contractor for the
limes, who hadn t received a bean. Some of the supers
said, ‘ \Y hat’s up with the illuminations?’ and when they
heard there was no stuff coming they began to drop
pieces of heavy armor during quiet scenes, and kick
against braces. We played the second act in st mi-dark-
ness, and the climax came before the curtain rose on the
throne-room scene, where Rankin had contracted to have
over 100 people on the stage. The noble 100 said : “We
want our little bit,” but they didn’t get it. Then they
went up in a body to their dressing-rooms, the men
amusing themselves by playing egg-in-the-hat with tin
helmets and swapping wigs with the girls. You
can imagine how much ginger Nance put into
the line, ‘ God’s wrath ! hut this is too much ! ’
y hen she called out ‘ What ho 1 my guards ! ’
instead of 12 guards coming on in military style, the Lord
Chamberlain bolted on and tried to make himself look 3
or 4, and the house laughed heartily. Fox, the wig-
maker, got news of the smash and sent down to seize the
wigs from off the heads of the actors, and I had to beg to
be allowed to wear mine 2 minutes longer whilst I
declared war against England. After the declaration a
brawny hand stretched out to scalp me before I had done
backing off the stage. Ye gods ! Then came the children
of Israel from the costumiers, Levy, Nathan and Cos.,
who had the honor of supplying the dresses. All our
energy and alertness were required to save our bodies
being despoiled of our trumpery finery before we were
through with the play. One youth, who had made
himself very conspicuous, was named Isaac, and his
entreaties for me to take off my tights were too laughable
for words. He followed me to my dressing-room, and for
the first time in my life I was graced with the services of
a dresser, at no expense—and a Jew, too.”
The true story of an Australian actor who went to
America with his wife. They were both young and
capable, but after filling a few casual dramatic engage-
ments they recognised, in a certain cold February, that
the best chance lay on the varie y stage. So the Aus-
tralian interviewed a gaunt, hurried, dyspeptic, cigar-
stained potentate who runs many variety shows, and
made propositions. The dyspeptic one coughed in the
bleak February air, and rushed at him, and said : “Just
let me see you and your wife do a sketch, an’ I’ll tell you
if we can do business.” “ Where ? ” said the Australian.
“Well, just you hire a small theatre, and I’ll
be there to see you do the sketch—say next
Wednesday, 11 o’clock, tell me where, good-day*,”
and the dyspeptic one fled back to business. The Aus-
tralian stood aghast at the idea of hiring a theatre
to play for half-an-hour to an audience of one ; then he
set his jaw* firmly and walked out into the February
slush. He found a small theatre, and notified the
manager, and did a few sketches in the bleak, raw
February air, with an audience of one, who hadn’t even
spoken to them, dimly visible on a back seat. When it
was over the Australian went round to interview the dim
figure and ask if he was satisfied, and found it wasn’t
the manager, but the negro caretaker of the theatre. The
manager had never turned up at all. The Australian went,
full of rage, into the February gloom and interviewed the
manager. “Look here,” said the cigar-stained one;
“ I’m real sorry, but couldn’t get raound. I’ll be raound
at that theatre next Tuesday cert’n, if you’ll do the
sketch again.” “Isn’t this rather an expensive way of
doing things?” said the Australian, beginning to look like
a man who might wreck the office at any moment.
“And a little precarious? You mightn’t be there and
we might play to the nigger again.” “That’s so,” said
the manager; “look here, just you fetch your wife
raound naow.” The Australian fetched his wife, and the
manager led them through six or eight Streets, and half-
a-mile of February blast to a music shop, at the back of
which was a small room with a sort of stage. There in
the half-light and the cold they did two or three items
and the manager applauded. My boy,” he said when it
was over, “it’s real good. Your wife’s as pretty as a
picture, too, and darned clever. Just you come daown
day after to-morrow, and we’ll settle things.’’ There was
a February snowstorm on the day after "to-morrow, hut
the Australian put on his bigsrest overcoat and his
seven-league boots and went “daown,” and was wel-
comed effusively. “I’m glad to see you,” said the
manager ; “ real glad. I’ve got the agreement all ready.
You can start a five weeks’ season next September.”
“ When ? ” “ Next September,” said the manager briskly.
“Everything’s filled up till then, but I’ll place you in
September for sure.” The Australian had an interval of
unconsciousness, and when he recovered he was at the
Shipping-office booking two passages for Australia. To
this day he can’t remember whether he crowded the
manager up the chimney before leaving or only put him
in the spittoon, but he hopes he put him up the chimney.
An ingrained desire to play showy tricks in his
accompaniments is the fatal failing of one crack
pianist, and has cost him numerous big engagements.
One time the key-thumper was touring, as accom-
panist, with a visiting star, and when she was
singing a difficult cadenza the clever pianist-man pur-
posely struck a top note half a tone too high. The lady
was ruffled quite out of her usual placidity, and demanded
an explanation. “ Well,” replied the accompanist, “it’s
a most difficult passage, and I wanted to see if you
could do it when I played wrong. It was a test only.”
But the artist slapped his face, told him he was no
musician—certainly not enough to “test” her- and sent
him about his business. Since then the accompanist has
been content to keep any similar pranks for the question-
able benefit of nervous young singers who are trying to
get a footing in the profession.
The planters’ Polynesian pets are taking to the theatre
in Queensland. two time-expired Tannas,
with their “ Marys," occupied dress-circle seats at
Rickards’ Brisbane theatre. They sat the show through
with absolute stolidity and manifested interest only when
one of the “ White Marys ” on the stage shook a very
spry leg.
James Anderson, the terrible old-school tragedian who
lifted up his voice in Australia years ago, gets an un-
flattering mention in Herman Merivale’s book of remini-
scences. Speaking of a dinner-party at Thackeray’s
house, Merivale says : “ It must have been on the same
evening that I expressed some youthful admiration of
James Anderson’s performance of Orlando. He was a
very handsome man, but a real ‘ roarer.’ Thackeray
beamed at me at once in his dear old way, and quickly
laughed. *At last,’ he said, ‘ I have seen a boy who
admires James Anderson. I felt I must, some day.’"
And yet there is somebody just now employed in writing
a book—a history—of that same roarful Jimmy Anderson.
There is a good deal of George Rignold about Henry
Lee in all respects. Right down to the gout, in fact.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance
is solicited.
The Closed Roads Swindle.
Amid all the retrenchment of Victorian publi*
servants and the hustling after odd half-pennies
in out-of-the-way corners, nobody seems to have
noticed that that impecunious State is at present
being, and has for many years past been, swindled
out of thousands of pounds yearly by the very
wealthiest of its citizens. Private M’s.P. have
often tackled the Closed Roads question, and
have as often been blocked, but no Govern-
ment has ever yet raised the necessary courage
to fight the greedy landowners who rob
the people. _ The State is mapped out in
rectangles, with a road at eveiy mile ; and
omitting altogether the dry North and North-
West, there are, at a very moderate computation,
in N., E. and S. Gippsland, the central province,
and the W. and S.W. country, at least 48,000
miles of as yet unused roads which the owners of
the adjacent property have the use of without fee
or reward. Taking these roads as one chain wide
all round—some of the main arteries of the future
run up to 3 chains, others are 1^ —there is here an
aggregate area of GOOsq. miles, or 384,000 acres !
(A typical instance Rupert Clarke’s Green
Hill run. near Romsey, encloses over 200 acres of
such public land.) These thousands of acres
would have fed many “starvers” from the drought
country away back ;in fact, they are so feeding
them—and the adjacent landowners are getting
up to 255. and 275. 6d. an acre per year for fatten-
ing these starvers on land which doesn’t belong
to them and for which they pay not one farthing
of rent to its owner—the State. Here is a
sum of perhaps £50,000 a year, out of which the
btate, through its paid servants the Treasurer and
the M.’sP., is being diddled by those who can well
afford to refrain from such petty larceny. Talk
about the beam of the landowners and the mote of
the civil servants, it isn’t a beam—it’s a whole
timber-yard!
-
“ The Other Bounder! ”
A Sydney paper’s London correspondent says
the mutual hatred of English and Germans is
inexplicable. The truly inexplicable thing would
be their friendship, if it ever existed. Of course,
the royal houses are allied to save their joint necks
and aid the common cause of monarchy against ris-
ing democracy. “ Reasons of State ” unite the two
nations for such ends as bullying little Venezuela.
But, essentially speaking, England hates Germany,
and Germany loathes England. Naturally enough,
Germany has decided that colonial expansion is
necessary to her greatness—it may be to her
existence ; and when, late in the piratical day,
she endeavors to expand, she finds Britain
sprawling over the map of the world, blocking
every desirable path. The war of commerce
is as keen a contest as any ever fought; and
England’s Manchester - men and Germany’s
Manchester-men are, even now at death-
grips. Germany successfully attacks England’s
commercial supremacy because, as Prof. Dewar
told the Royal Association, German technical
brains are a generation ahead of English technical
brains. The German middle-classes, still retain-
ing their primitive idyllic strain, still under the
domination of conscience, count the smothering
of the little Boer nations cause for a Holy War.
Trade rivals, hostile from patriotic bias, English
and Germans are bitter foes because sprung from
the same racial stalk. They hate one another like
brothers. They have similar zeal, and similar
defects. The typical Englishman and the typical
German, as met on the highway of are
both insufferable bounders ; and one holds the
mirror to the other. The cold insolence of the
English official is surpassed only by the swagger-
ing: insolence of the German official. Each moves
as if he owns the earth—until he meets the other
bounder who claims the universe, and detests him.
Germany is bumptious, aggressive Cain ; England
is sneering, self-satisfied Abel. Perhaps each
will slay the other at Armageddon.

Discussion of the N.S.W. Licensing Act
Amendment Bill re-emphasises that there is only
»ne true liquor reform —a State monopoly of the
retailing of alcoholic liquors.
Thus a N.S.W. country paper:—
The Liberal party, it is stated, will send two speakers
nto every electorate during the recess. We trust they
Will send one treasurer also to pay up accounts incurred
at the last State election.
Victorian Treasurer Shiels hopes to cut
another £60,000 off Old Age Pensions this year,
and save a general land-tax, which would
fall on the valuable and now exempt ground
values of Melbourne, Ballarat, Bendigo, and
Geelong.
The Bulletin hears from a big mining
authority in S’Africa that “the difficulty in
getting labor ” for the mines is a fake. Cohen
does not want to work the mines at anything like
their full capacity until the question of war taxa-
tion is settled ; he wishes to show as poor a mouth
as possible until then.
N.S.W. old-age pension items :—A healthy
man of 65 loses a Corporation billet, and applies
for old-age pension. Refused because he had
earned £52 during the past year. He is now
qualifying by a spell in Liverpool Asylum. A
man (72) and his housekeeper (75) refused “be-
cause they are living in adultery.”
The “ pin-prickery ” of Brisbane Courier
against the Philp Government is really a blessing
in disguise to the Continuous Ministry. Now
that Granny Courier begins to wallop its Joss
the average man commences to surmise that the
Joss must have shown a bit of backbone. So the
.Boodlewraith scores again. What luck it has !
A mild recrudescence of the Single-tax
idea has appeared of late in Sydney. But the
time is ill-chosen.,, It is only necessary to go and
look at the land now to feel badly discouraged
about the prospect of increasing the land-tax ten-
fold—and that is about what the Single-tax idea
amounts to. The land is a sore subject in Aus-
tralia these days.

Nothing has been heard lately of the
contemplated journey of Premier Jenkins to Eng-
land in the interest of the transcontinental railway
to Port Darwin. He is most anxious to get the

The N, S. YV 'Taxdodgers’ Union announces
that its ideas of State Constitutional reform
include an elective and unpaid Upper House.
As a Council elected on the principle of one adult
one vote would be only the Assembly over again,
the Taxdodgers doubtless propose a property-
elected Council. N.S.W. won’t bite the hook
with that worm on it while the horrible example
of Victoria is at its door. The proposal only
lowers its opinion of the Taxdodgers’ intelligence.

The vaunted Cobden Club—the head and
centre of the Freetrade theory in England—
consists of 475 members, though it makes as much
row as if it consisted of thousands. There are 233
British members or members residing in Britain,
who (presumably) really believe in Freetrade, and
242 foreigners who whoop Britain on to Freetrade
because it is a payable foolishness for the
foreigner. Thus, more than half the club now
consists of foreigners, who find that Freetrade
makes John Bull a profitable ass, and who want
to encourage him in being a profitable ass.
Failing to foist on the Commonwealth an
Imperialistic ornamental Governor-General, Great
Britain will, it is almost certain, try next an
imperialistic hard-worker, like Milner or Cromer.
The new man will not go in for show so much as
for driving imperial nails into the coffin of demo-
cracy. When such a man plays his first trick, it
will be necessary for Australia to speak as em-
phatically as in respect to showmen. Indeed,
silly showmanry, tawdry and gawky as it was
Hopetoun’s ambition to make it, was not nearly
so dangerous a menace to the Commonwealth’s
Republican future as will be the strenuous in-
fluence of a man of the Cromer or Milner pattern.
There can now be no doubt that a strong
move is on foot in Victoria to hand the railways
over to “ private enterprise.” The
Tory press of the State has long
been busily inflaming the public
mind against the State manage-
ment of the lines, and from time
to time quiet suggestions have
been dropped that it would be far
better if they were run by a private
firm. Naturally, a Tory Govern-
ment like Irvine’s leans vigorously
in the direction of “ private enter-
prise,” and at length Minister Bent
has revealed the true sentiments of
the Ministry. He has begun to
farm-out the work of maintaining
the permanent way to contractors.
One contract has been let, and
Bent’s “ boosting-up ” of the bar-
gain is significant. He trumpets it
forth that he is getting work done
by contract for ,£750 which pre-
viously cost the department £lOl7
a year. The saving of £267 will
not go far in making up for the
extra supervision that will be
needed, and then there is the ques-
tion of costs in case of accidents.
It is almost certain that under a
contract system the work will be
slummed.” Under the present
system there is only the laziness of
the men to be watched, but under
any system of contract there is
also the greed of the contractor to
be reckoned with. Bent further
declares that he intends in future
contracts of the kind to knock out
'"'' n for a minimum wage
of ?•» a day.

The Australian Leather
J OURNAL gives some further facts
about the ruin which is coming
over N.S. W. because of Fede-
ration and Protection
During the quarter ended Sept. 30
of this year the shoe imports into
N.S.W. were only £58,836, as com-
pared with £’85,035 for the corres-
ponding quarter of 1901, whilst the
exports for 1902 were £18,445, as
against £10,354 for the same period in
1901. There is thus a decrease in im-
ports for the quarter of £26,199, and
an increase in exports of £BO9l
These figures, which are supplied by
the N.S.W. Customs authorities, and
are therefore correct, would seem t»
indicate that N.S.W has succeeded
in manufacturing £34,290 more foot-
wear in one quarter this year than
in 1901, showing an average increase
of £137,160 annually.
In other words, £137,160 more
money available for wages in one
industry alone ; £137,160 more
to be spent in the shops of local
tradespeople ; and £137,160 less
going out of the country. And
Sydney Evening News last week
urged the people of N.S.W. to
arise in their might at next elec-
tion and put an end to all this.
A Maoriland correspondent
writes :
The canting coterie who are agi-
tating for the Bible in schools pro-
fess to be satisfied with the result of
the late election. But in reality they
have achieved nothing. I believe a
majority of the successful candidates
favor a referendum on the question ;
but few, if any, would vote for the
Bible-in-schools directly. If the par-
sons keep on agitating, no doubt we
shall have a referendum, as they
have had in South Australia, in which
case I predict that a huge majority
will declare in favor of keeping
popular education clear of sectarian
squabbles. Apart from the fact that
many members of all denominations
are opposed to the insidious proposal,
there are influential bodies, such as
the Jews and K.O.’s, who would
vote solidly against it. Worth re-
marking that the Bible-in-schools
agitators include a multitude of
wealthy respectables—those natural
allies of alleged Christianity who
sneer at labor legislation and support
any church which is likely to police
their vested interests. In England
the parson and the squire work to-
gether to educate the children of the poor in blind sub-
servience to the plundering- upper classes (whose game
laws and land laws are an outrage on humanity), and
doubtless they would do likewise here if they could.
May luck save us from such a damnable sectarian
wrangle as they are having in England over the Educa-
tion Bill !
The railway policy of Q. Govt, is a weird
and wondrous thing-. Owing to lack of revenue
Philp and co. close up two railways (Bowen and
Cooktown), and with the same stroke of the pen
give to the omnivorous Tram Co. two important
concessions that will, to a certainty, eat into the
suburban railway traffic. Of course, it never
struck Minister Leahy to run a few chains of
extra line so that the Government could lap up
the extra fares.

"THE EARTH IS THE LANDLORD'S—AND THE
FULLNESS THEREOF."
An aggrieved landlord writes to The Bulletin “Mr. James
Sinclair Taylor M'Gowen, leader of N.S.W. Labor party, is not a
personal friend of mine ; but I feel a sincere sympathy for him,
because I hear he is building a terrace of houses. I hope Mr!
M‘Gowen isn’t building little houses. Never become the landlord
of a terrace of small houses. If you have the abilities which will
make you a success in that line, start, instead, a lambing-down
pub. or a 300 per shent. loan-shop. I have a terrace of houses, out
of which—woe is me! —l have to get my living. There’s Mrs
Murphy. She owed eight weeks, and when called on at No. 9on
Monday for the rent always explained that, as Murphy was out of
a job, she couldn’t pay, and ‘Share, I would be aisy with them
now.’ Finally I offered to forgive her all the debt if she would
only clear out peaoeably and give someone else a show, but she told
me she really hadn’t the money to cart her furniture away, and I
had finally to give her 18s. 6d. for that purpose. Mrs. Smith is
generally half-drunk when called on. The Smiths owe seven weeks
in all, but they now and then do pay something on account. The
Jones’s pay fairly well, but are accustomed to use the verandah-
flooring for firewood when the weather is wet. Mrs. Macdougall
pays promptly, but insists on an abatement of Is. 6d. per week
before she does pay ; and the Edwards family, who already owe 11
weeks, implore me to let them remain a little longer and allow the
house-father, who is slowly dying, to pass away in peace. And so
an. And this Xmas, as I was thinking out a matter of an over-
draft, I got this picture as a ‘ valentine.’ No, don’t buy a
terrace of houses unless you are a money-lender at heart. Rather
trust to a steady job as bailiff to keep you in your old age.”
N.S.W. Works Minister Owe’Sullivan’s
letter on the great virtue of spending borrowed
money to alleviate the drought trouble, and
getting into debt to make the country rich, and
ms story about how some of his works are return-
lng 3* and 4 per cent., evoked one pointed answer
at all events. An embittered person wrote from
Oowra to say that the Owe’Sullivan department
sent up a lot of city unemployed, many of whom
nadn t the least idea how to handle a pick, to
Oanowindra, to build a weir which cost £224 ; that
the shoddy thing immediately burst; and that the
local cockles, who are hard-up enough in any case,
have been notified that they must pay £224 for
the weir which is gone. He adds that there are
local unemployed who wanted work quite as badly
as the Sydney ones, and who would build for £lOO
a weir that wouldn’t burst.
Queensland Govt, announces its decision
to close the Cooktown and Bowen railways,
though the loss last year was .£4OOO less than the
year before, therefore it is impossible to set this
move down as one of the evil results of Federa-
tion. Ihe Cooktown line is probably hopeless,
but the failure of the Bowen railway is the care-
fully-premeditated result of almost the most
villainous piece of log-rolling that even Queens-
land has ever seen. Bowen’s unfinished scrap of
line never could pay, for it leads nowhere. But
had it been extended till it joined on to the
Mughenden railway, an immense amount of trade
would have flowed there. It wasn’t allowed to
flow there simply because Townsville wanted the
trade—Towns vide whose shallow harbor isn’t one-
tenth the value of Bowen’s, and which swallows
up cartloads of public money in an effort to make
a port where Nature never intended there should
be one. But then Premier Philp is member for
Townsville.

VITADATIO
Wrightville, Cobar, N.S.W.,
Mr. S. A. PALMER,
27th January, 1902.
“ Vitadatio,”
IS4 Pitt-street, Sydney.
Dear Sir, —It gives me great pleasure to state to you,
for the benefit of others who may be suffering as I was
from that terrible complaint “ Hydatids,” my miraculous
recovery. If there is anyone who is grateful to you and
to VITADATIO, it is myself, and I feel that I cannot say
too much in its praise.
Some two years ago I was compelled to go to Sydney
to be operated upon, and there an operation was per-
formed in the Prince Alfred Hospital. The doctors
removed a large lump of Hydatid disease from my side.
It was then supposed that I was cured, but after my
return home to Emmaville, where I was then living, the
Hydatids appeared again, and my case was pronounced
to be a very bad one. Two of the doctors there would
not operate on me because the Hydatids were on my left
lung, but, thanks to the kindly interest of my uncle, Mr.
John Light, of 68 Kippax-street, Sydney, who is a great
friend of Mr. George Beyer, the popular lantern enter-
tainer, I was induced to give VITADATIO a trial. When
Mr. Light told Mr. Beyer that I was sinking fast and
could not live very long, owing to Hydatids on the lungs,
Mr. Beyer replied that he was certain VITADATIO
would cure me, but my uncle was very sceptical, and
said that he had no faith in “these quack stuffs.” Mr.
Beyer insisted, however, that VITADATIO would save
me, so my uncle called on 3011 and purchased three
bottles of VITADATIO for me.' I felt no result whatever
from the first two bottles, but the third bottle made me
feel so ill that I thought the end had come, so I went to
the doctor again, who made a thorough examination, and
he gave me some hope as he thought the “mass” was
moving. I continued taking the preparation, with the
1 esult that shortly afterwards I commenced to vomit up
the H3 - datids, and after continuing with VITADATIO till
I had taken seven bottles I threw up the “ cyst ” in three
pieces. I have put it in a bottle and send it to 3 r ou for
you to examine.
I wish .to add that to-day I am stronger than ever, and
am able to follow my occupation of a miner, and in the
interests of many sufferers like I used to be, I feel it is
1113- duty to make this miraculous cure, which was effected
by VITADATIO, widely known, and you are therefore at
liberty to make whatever use of this letter you wish.
Yours truly,
CHARLES E. EMERSON,

Melbourne, or some of it, takes a quite
lively interest in the collection of British (largely
Scottish) pictures which have been hung for a
several weeks’ season at the Vic. Art Society’s
rooms :
About 160 paintings in oil and water-color, with some
60 or 70 drawings and etchings, make up the show.
Poynter has the place of honor because he is president of
the Royal Academy and carries a handle to his name, but
Poynter’s “The Greek Dance” is only an eminently
respectable thing, after all. The floor and fittings-
differing from Alma Tadema’s marble in that it looks old
and begrimed—could hardly be better painted. The
central figure, a young girl of modern boarding-school
type, dances very lightly, as she ought to dance if she
doesn’t want to slip on the marble. The older girl
onlookers give an impression of unreality, and the gene-
ral effect is oleographic. Geo. Haite’s picture of the
Victoria Jubilee Procession is a remarkably skilful waste
of art, and the eye turns away impatiently from two or
three “ kharkee ” subjec.s, unless it is the eye of acohen-
tingenter. These S’African war paintings are only
notable now for being free from gore. “A Mid May
Morning ” (85), by Alfred Parsons, doesn’t charm every-
body, although the shrill, tall voice of Superior Criticism
states that the eminent Parsons is at least true to nature
in this English landscape. The painty superabundance
of bright green grass, and the fruit trees in fullest bloom
make a garish scene. The picture next to it is a daring
seascape by Julius Olsson—“ln St. Ives’ Bay”—and
many people will prefer the blueness of Olsson’s sea to
the greenness of the grass on the other canvas. Frank
Spenlove-Spenlove exhibits two attractive landscapes
large and small, in different styles. “ Spring in London,”
by D. G. Cameron, is an unconventional work, and
David Murray’s one picture seems beautifully truJ to its
title—“ From Sultry Day to Summer Storm (109). The
sheep in the foreground are going to get caught in the
rain. Two striking little things are Thomas Schafer’s
“ Marguerite and Mephisto” (131), and Douglas Almond’s
“An Afternoon Call,” the latter a humorous subject,
painted to a nicety. Orchardson, R.A., shows a fascinat-
ing study of Hamlet, a brown-haired, feverish Hamlet,
enfeebled by midnight encounters with a spook and other
Shakespearian circumstances, and unable to settle
the suicide question one way or other. He has
a thin, dark moustache. Lady visitors take exception to
his mo. Alexander Roche and the veteran Edwin Hayes
are well represented by sea pictures, but Albert Moore's
“Elijah’s Sacrifice” —one of his early efforts—is disap-
pointing. It looks like a half-hearted effort in the Hol-
man Hunt direction. Solomon J. Solomon is represented
by “The Birth of Love.” His Venus is a fine, delicate-
complexioned young woman, presumably arising from the
sea. A large pearly shell is vaguely situated under one
of her feet. The artist would seem to have wearied of
the job when he had painted down to her feet, but all the
rest of Venus (if she be Venus) is carefully done, also
the Cupids hovering round head and shoulders. Perhaps
Solomon meant to arrive at the Unattainable and felt that
he had missed the ’bus. Many other pictures are welcome
visitors. One or two water-colors are specially charming,
and the black-and-white work calls for admiration in
several instances.

Raspberry-vinegar manufacturers are pro-
testing against the aspersions recently cast on the
character . of their temperance lotion by Melb.
Board of Health. They state that the modicum
of cochineal employed in coloring the vinegar up
to the standard of redness required by consumers
do.es not amount to adulteration, inasmuch as it
is harmless in practice and strictly moral in prin-
ciple. If the stuff bore its natural tint the
teetotal drinker would turn from it, and probably
take to beer and other sinful substitutes. The
wail of the raspberry-vinegar men sounds reason-
able, considering that the Board of Health has
not yet made special reference to the matter of
tinned sheep-tongues and bullocks’ ditto. What
passes for the natural “jelly of the meat ” in a
tin of tongues is darkly rumored to be sometimes
magnified by the addition of melted glue, which
is not so nourishing as it looks. The glue trade
is deeply involved in the mysteries of food-adul-
teration.

Disorders of the stomach are, as a rule,
caused by a torpidity of the liver and bowels
and irregularity in living. When the diges-
tion is naturally weak, as is often the case
with people of middle age and older, too much
care cannot be used to keep the bowels
regular, for when they become constipated
the trouble is greatly aggravated. In fact,
constipation is the prime cause of nearly
all disorders of the stomach. Keeping the
bowels regular, however, will not alone cure
these disorders. Only easily digested food
should be taken, and that moderately and
well masticated, And at regular hours.
Fully six hours should elapse between meals,
so as to give ample time for digestion and
allow the stomach a period of rest before
more food is taken. The liver and whole
digestive apparatus needs toning up, for which
purpose take two Chamberlain’s Stomach
and Liver Tablets to begin with and then
one tablet each day for a few days until the
disorder is corrected, They should be taken
immediately after supper. In case one tablet
a day does not keep the bowels free, take two;
or if one each day causes the bowels to act
too freely, take only one every other day.

lain’s Stomach and Liver Tablets locally,
a package will be sent, post paid, upon re-
ceipt of 1/6, or six packets for 8/-, by
addressing Chamberlain Medicine Co., 64

The Cockie.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Immersed in dreams of other days,
With vacant stare he rode along,
The cockie man ; his haggard gaze
Was fraught with many a tale of wrong.
His acres barren were and grey,
He looked upon the dust profuse ;
His tanks were dry as Old Decay,
He spurred and muttered “ Wot’s th’ use?”
By hut and humpy did he ride,
And turned his head to look at Death,
At which his ancient cuddy shied ;
The wind was like a furnace breath.
And track and lane the cockie took,
Intent on Distance or the Deuce;
A broken man he “took his hook”
And only muttered : “ Wot’s th’ use?”
v.
WILL JARRATT.
The Book of Pember Beeves.
illiam Pember Reeves, one of the most able
Ministers Maoriland ever had, and afterwards
Agent-General for that State, has written a book
which supplies a great literary want. His “ State
Experiments in Australia and New Zealand ” is
practically the whole story of progressive legis-
lation in the seven States of Australasia. The
information it contains is essential to every poli-
tician, and every journalist who ranks above the
dog-fight order, and till W. P. Reeves wrote his
book the same information had to be sought for in
dozens of different volumes, a ton of Hansards
and Parliamentary papers, and a shipload of
newspapers. Mr. Reeves tells the story of
Australia’s factory laws, its arbitration and
conciliation statutes, minimum wage laws, early-
closing Acts, systems of dealing with the
unemployed, its land laws with all the various ex-
periments in the way of land settlement attached
thereto, its various old age pension policies, anti-
Chinese and anti-Kanaka and other anti-undesir-
able-alien enactments, its State banks, its fran-
chises, Federal and State constitutions —in fact he
tells practically the whole story of democratic
progress. And, on the whole, he tells it fairly,
honestly, and interestingly. He was himself for
some years in the thick of the battle, and Austra-
lasia always regarded Reeves as a good and ex-
ceptionally clever democrat, though he was never
strong enough to lead a great movement, and he
never influenced the masses as Ballance, Seddon,
Kingston, or even Turner did. He can in no place
be accused of over-stating his case ; in a good many
instances he understates it. Apparently this
arises solely from a desire to be absolutely fair to
his opponents ; there is no visible evidence in his
book that Mr. Reeves is any worse a democrat in
London than he was in Maoriland.
* • • «
The book calls up the ghosts of hundreds of lost,
dead and exploded prophecies. Every separate
reform whose history it tells reminds us of some-
thing that is almost forgotten, but which should
be remembered—-how the passing Tory of the day
wrote a huge “ Ichabod ” on the outer wall, and
said that now at last the country was really lost,
and nothing was left but ruin and ashes. Nowa-
days, when a new reform is proposed and the pro-
phecies delivered against the old reforms are all
exploded and dead, the Tory rises to say some-
thing like this:
I am an eminently reasonable, progressive, and liberal
man. Though you call me a Tory, I approve of (here
follows a list which varies according to circumstances).
Therefore my words should carry the more weight when
I say that the measure now proposed will ruin the
country, uproot the pillars of the Constitution, and open
the floodgates of anarchy generally.
And therein the Tory is a liar. He has declared,
when each progressive idea was mooted, that it
meant ruin, chaos and conflagration, and, when
the prophecy fell through, he has denied that he
ever prophesied at all. His prophecy was a lie,
and his after denial of it was a lie, and each time
he prophesies afresh the fact that he is a convicted
liar should be written up large so that the world
may not forget. There are any number of
measures which are intended to be progressive,
and which, unfortunately, pan out much below
expectation, but the Tory class prophesied as
strongly against the one that succeeded as against
the one that partially failed. Therefore the Tory,
as a tainted witness, should be ruled out of court.
(This is a digression.)
* * • *
There are weak spots in Reeves’s book, as well
as strong ones. He dwells a shade too much
sometimes on his own State. He does, perhaps,
scant justice to good democrats who failed, or
partially failed, iii almost hopeless circumstances.
Kingston, of South Australia, probably devised
more good democratic measures than any other
statesman in Australasia, but he had to fight with
a Legislative Council elected on a narrow fran-
chise, and could not swamp his House of Obstruc-
tion by new appointments, as Ballance and Seddon
could, and did, in Maoriland ; so the strongest
Australasian politician of his day left many things
un 'one because he struck against a set of circum-
stances that we e even stronger than himself.
George Turner, in Victoria, had to struggle with
an Upper House vastly stronger and more Tory
and more utterly unshiftable than even that of
S.A. —one. compared to which the British
House of Lords is liberalism itself. It is
questionable if even the brawny Seddon would
have achieved so much, in these apparently
hopeless circumstances, as was done by the patient
little solicitor who never called himself a demo-
crat, and hardly even took the trouble to label his
measures ‘ 1 progressive. ” Yet Kingston gets little
notice, and Turner is mentioned about three
times, not too favorably. The credit for passing
the Minimum Wage Law—at the time almost the
most advanced democratic idea on this earth,
and carried in the teeth of almost the most
unpromising Upper House on this globe—
is given wholly to Turner’s subordinate, Peacock,
though when Peacock came to stand alone he
proved himself a very subordinate person indeed.
• * * *
And nowhere in the book is there a hint at the
great truth which is even now beginning to come
home to the Labor parties in Australasia that
sound finance is a most essential democratic idea
—that every Labor party should have a financial
policy, and that the cardinal points of it should
be the abolition of deficits, the abolition
of foreign borrowing, and the redemption
of the most pawned country on earth from
the pawnshop where it is deposited. Mr.
Reeves hardly mentions the fact that the
policy of land repurchase for closer settlement is
a system whereby land is sold .for cash which is
used as revenue, and then repurchased out of loan
funds, while the revenue doesn’t refund the ori-
ginal purchase money. He scarcely indicates once
that the village settlement policy rests almost
wholly on loans, and that even old age pensions,
to a great extent, come indirectly out of loans.
Mr. Reeves apparently regards all that the
democratic party has gained as so much perma-
nent victory, whereas the great danger of Tory
re-action rests on the fact that much of it is
gained solely by the grace of ti e British money-
lender, that he holds the key of the position, and
that the key already shows signs of giving way,
and turns with more difficulty in the lock every
da y- .
Yet withal “State Experiments in Australia
and New Zealand ”is an invaluable book. Even
the author’s apparent admiration for that amazing
Maoriland Act under which land is leased for 999
years at a rental equal to 4 per cent, on its present
value and the rental can’t be increased from now
to the year 2902, no matter what happens in the
interim, isn’t sufficient to make it other
than invaluable. His book is History-a rare
and essential and difficult-to-get-at patch of His-
tory and the politician who is without it is lost—
or else he is Pumpkin Smith, which, when one
comes to think of it, is almost the same thing.
There is timely talk of disbanding Baden
Powell’s comic-opera army, the S.A.C., which,
though only about 10,000 strong, costs £1,500,000
per annum:—
“ The troopers ” it is stated ‘‘are scattered about on
the veldt, at what are understood to be ‘ posts.’ B.P. says
they are doing useful pacification work by cultivating
friendly relations with the Boer. They do this principally
by marrying the Boer maids. This practice, however, is
not confined to the S.A.C. people, as these maidens fair
are very often heiresses to large fortunes, in spite of the
way the war ruined everybody in Boerdom. They’ are
very much sought after. The latest victim is Lord
Milner’s private secretary. He is engaged to a young
lady from Orangia. Her complexion is a rich gold—
extremely rich gold—but otherwise her beauty is not
pronounced."
There has not been a more interesting
Australian book published for a long time than
“Bushman and Buccaneer” —giving the life and
writings of Harry Morant (“The Breaker”).
Every bushman will want to buy a copy for the
sake of the complete collection of “The Breaker’s”
verses which it contains. The book gives a photo-
graphic reproduction of the “ copy ” of Morant’s
last verses, written in gaol, and many illustrations,
from authentic photographs, of the Bushveldt
Carbineers’ operations. Price one shilling.
Wholesale from Edwards Dunlop and Co., Syd-
ney.-

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Charters Towers Racing Club wasn’t taking any
Mo. at its last meeting. Kicks by some committeemen
against this attitude were met with suggestions that the
‘club make a “double book,” leaving the machine for
straight-out betting, and over £4OOO passed through it.
Secy. R. Russell, of the C.T.R.C., who has done much for
N.Q. racing heads the movement against the “ books,”
: and vows that he'll rid the country of them ; and there
! is much langwidge in Israel in consequence.
* • • *
Sterling old nag Blue Metal will sport silk no
'more, but retires to a well-grassed paddock rather than
'run the risk of breaking down completely. Blue Metal
'owns an interesting history. Bred by Mr. 0. Baldwin at
Durham Court (in ’95) he brought £2O as a 2yr. old.
Messrs. Donoghue Bros., of Warwick (Q.), raced him first
and he won for them. Two successive years the A. J.O.
Summer Cup fell to Blue Metal, but a third effort in the
same event saw Caledonia beat him by a head, and in
Tattersall’s Cup immediately following he was second to
Hoop Iron. Last autumn Blue Metal annexed the Aus-
tralian Cup and on last day of that meeting appropriated
Loch Plate, beating Wakeful. He is a gelding of 8 years
and five seasons of racing.
• » *
Noted at recent Perth (W.A.) Cup meeting
that heavy betting has fallen off considerably—“ fivers ”
taking the place of “ fifties’’—whereat the be-jewelled
one was much chagrined, as he had to disgorge £7O for the
privilege of “fielding” during the four days. Profit to
the club was, however, wonderful, nearly £ll,OOO being
netted, which must be close to an Australasian best.
• • • •
Lochiel-Lady Kingborough 4-year-old Kinglock
has been sold to go to S’Africa. Price paid not known,
but ought to be high, as few Australian nags promised so
handsomely.
• * • •
Up to date, M.L. 3-yr.-old Waikiri has had six
wins and one second in seven starts, and won £1950
stake money.
• • * •
Lifebelt, winner of the 2-yr.-old race at War-
wick Farm, Saturday, is worth watching. Pedigree By
Niagara from Faith, by Vespasian from Hope, by Yat-
tendon.
• • * •
Entrants for coming Newmarket Handicap
(Australia’s biggest sprint) total 79—four less than last
year, and considerably behind the 116 of 1901. There’s a
bigger falling off in Australian Cup nominations. Only
40 were received, as against 50 last year and 52 the year
before.
* * • *
Mentone (Vic.) races, t’other day, provided
something extremely rare. For Steeple, Scotch Reel was
rushed at 4 to 1, and then went back to twenties, with
“any,price you like” offered in running. She won.
Something must have gone very much wrong somewhere.
• * • •
A Melb. paper, discussing the variegated career
of Mr. Bob Suttor Sievier, the one-time “ English book-
maker “of Australia, credited that remarkable plunger
with having married a ducheis. Sievier, divorced by his
young Melbourne wife after his return to England, didn’t
capture a duke’s widow for his second partner. The lady
who brought a title—courtesy title—into his family was
the then Marquis of Ailesbury’s only sister. Ailesbury
was a hopeless young waster, and so notoriously dis-
respectable that he never took his seat in the House of
Peers, where any ordinary noble blackguard is reckoned
an acceptable pillar of the State. The sister, whom
Sievier persuaded to jilt her previous johnnie, was a
handsome young woman. She had given offence to her
relatives by sticking to her rorty brother, and lending
him what money she possessed. So Sievier got
no fortune with his bride, Lady Mabel Brude-
nell-Bruce. After their marriage, she was sued by
the previous johnnie. He wanted his presents back
from the faithless one, but he didn’t get them. Then
Mr. Sievier and Lady Sievier passed out of notoriety
for a long time. Bob made his first big rise by backing
The Grafter (from Australia) for the City and Suburban
and from that time his luck has been phenomenal. He
played-up his Grafter winnings, trebled his capital,
bought some yearlings at record prices, and struck a gold
mine in one of them—the mare Sceptre. According to
all accounts Sievier is as obstinately foolish in some
respects as his late brother-in-law, the most noble the
Marquis of Ailesbury. He persisted in training Sceptre
on his own and the wonderful mare has lost several rich
stakes through his bad management. All the same
Sievier figures at the top of the list of winning owners for
the last English racing season, and Sceptre is still on her
legs. Fortune favors audacity, when audacity knows
how to address a lady.
• • • •
Published statement that Australian breeders
are under great disabilities compared with those of M.L.
in entering hags for that country’s “young” races is not
altogether correct. Entries for all M.L. w.f.a. and
classic events are made by breeders and no money is
charged for nomination. First forfeit is only a sov. which
is due just about when a pretty good summing up of a
youngster’s possibilities may be made. M.L. breeders
nominate liberally and to keep up with them Australian
breeders must do likewise.
• • • •
Athena, winner of 2-year-old races at A. J.C. and
Tatt.’s meets recently, promises much. She was bred at
Tocal (N.S.W.) by Mr F. Reynolds, and is by dead
Grandmaster horse Gibraltar, from Equation by Grand
Flaneur. She is shapely, and shows fine shoulders and
good bone. 'Tis told regarding the breeding of this
filly that when it was decided to send her dam to Gibral-
tar, Mr. White offered £lOO to her breeder for the chance
of a foal—if there’d been no foal Mr. W. would have lost
his money.
• • • ■
Apparently Plugger Bill feels his day is over.
He carefully abstained from coming to Sydney to compete
with Major Taylor, and conteuted himself by hanging
round Melbourne, picking up unconsidered trifles while
all the best men were in Sydney. Sort of “ any mouse is
big enough for me ” business. The sanctimonious negro
not long since in U.S.A. gave Martin 100yds. in the mile
and beat him badly But perhaps the Plugger is lying
low for a big coup.
• • • •
Third night of Major Taylor season at Sydney
Cricket Ground drew about 15,000. St. Taylor started
10 yards behind scratch in the third heat of the Summer
Wheel race, and only finished fourth. “ Special Invita-
tion ” scratch (1 lap) event saw him on the wheel again,
when Maorilander Chalmers dead-heated with him in
38 sec. The fourth heat of the same race was won by
Don Walker in 37 3-5, but in the final Taylor caught
Walker near the finish and won in 36 1-5.
• • • *
After the first day’s racing at Sydney, Major
Taylor specially picked a local man named Gudgeon as
one of the most promising local riders. Since then,
Gudgeon’s success has been remarkable, he being placed
more often than anyone else, bar Walker, which shows
that Taylor has judgment. Perhaps Gudgeon “ didn’t
know he was loaded,” so to speak, until Taylor drew his
attention to the fact.
• • • •
Doubtful if Taylor is really a better cyclist physi-
cally than Walker. Anyhow, he’s nothing like so grace-
ful, but Walker lacks heart and judgment. Once the
saintly’Murkan overtakes him Walkerseeins to lose going
power. Once Megson succeeded in beating Walker he
kept on doing it. Apparently, if Walker could instil
a little extra courage—a little ability to make one
supreme effort—into the place where he carries his
pluck, Australia might own the world’s champion
cyclist.
• * * •
Colored cyclist Taylor, in a religious paper, on
his own virtues ‘ ‘ Yes, sir ;I am glad to say that lam
a Christian, and it doesn’t make any difference who
knows it I don’t make a secret of it, though I don’t go
round sounding a trumpet. ... I attribute most of my
success entirely to the fact that I have tried to do what
was right—live fairly and squarely by every man—and
any man who follows those principles is bound to succeed.
The fact that I am brought among worldly people
all the time people who are inclined to be the
other way—makes the fact stand out, perhaps. But
I hope I have not conveyed any impression that I think
myself better than anvbody else. . There are people
who don’t know what it is to feel here (placing his hand
over his heart) that they are doing what is right; but the
man who does know would not be without it. Sometimes
they say to me, ! Major, how do you manage to get along
as you do? You never work in with any other rider on
the track ; you never seem to have a friend, and yet you
win and never have an accident.’ I reply: ‘I always
have a Friend with me, a Friend who looks after me.’’”
And this saintly cyclist is understood to be still preserving
his virtue in the blastiferous atmosphere of Sydney pro-
fessional cycling! If the Major will come round before
he leaves us and swear on The Bulletin office Bible that
he is as good as when he arrived here, this paper will
actually grovel to him.
* * » •
Glorious weather, and the absence of counter
attractions, brought some 30,000 to see the Austral
Wheel Race final at M.C. Ground last Saturday. As
usual, the very naicest sat or stood cheek by jowl with
Flossies and embryo-Flossies, while tugs and spielers
from Bourke-st. shouldered their way in with the select
cricket club members. The club’s spasm of virtue as to
prohibiting belting was very short-lived, a huge mob of
mostly unregistered Ikeys—with the aforesaid tugs—
filling the whole eastern end of the stand promenade.
These openly displayed their bags and books, and showed
what they thought of the affair by raucously yelling
“20 to 1, bar one,” then “ Any price bar one ” —said one
being, of course, Mick Lewis. Despite this, writer,
knowing that one determined goer might upset the coup,
picked the man with the best heat performance—Auger
—and went down to back him. He was offered 10’s,
then 15’s, 20’s and finally invited to name his own price,
but as he couldn’t find a bookie he knew, and the others
seemed altogether too anxious to lay, he summed up the
situation as “ crook,” and stood off. The spectacle of a
dozen Ikeys being subsequently escorted outside by
plain clothes police, and of others giving backers merely
their stakes back, enabled him to regard his omission to
plunge on Auger with equanimity. The race—well, it
was chiefly a nursing contest, with Lewis for baby ; the
latter half being run at such an easy pace that Auger—
almost on the limit—was able to reserve enough strength
for the final dash. This year’s Austral stinks, as usual,
and if the club officials take no action on the evidence of
their own eyes then—to be Hibernian for a moment—
they can’t have noses.
* • * *
The effect of this year’s Austral was absurdly
belittled by the motor-chasing of the Germans- Robl and
Dickentmann—who went 5 miles each at the rate of
about 35 miles an hour shortly before the'big event came
to pass. The Austral riders put in their 2 miles at the
rate of about 27 miles an hour only, and it was surprising
to find they travelled as fast as that. To see them •
toiling around without the excitement of a puffing motor,
anyone who wasn’t taking the times would have thought
they were even more than half a lap per minute slower
than the Germans. The race was not nearly as interest-
ing as the “struggle” of last year, when Mick Lewis,
having nobody to assist him, paced himself on his own
for 6 laps, what time the Billmartin crowd was carefully
preparing victory pre-destined Plugger.
From a turfman’s point of view, a handicap
race by cyclists is a mere mockery of the handicapper.
The fellows on the out-marks rarely attempt to take
advantage of their position. Also, the back-marks, after
benefiting by the weakness of the original leaders, who
have allowed them to come up, fail to take advantage of
their position. As soon as the field gets bunched all the
riders, individually and syndicately, appear to drop
their policies and trust to luck. Saturday’s race
was an irritating display of foolishness or roguery
—who shall say which is which in such cases?
The eight front men had the chance to confine
the race to themselves. When the pistol went off they
were placed at short intervals from 170yds. to 80yds.
ahead of the nearest of the back markers. Yet, at the
end of a mile (when five of them had covered only 7 fur-
longs, or less), the back marks were among them. After
which the favored overtakers, instead of combining to
get away from the fellows they had already outpaced,
went jigging along in their company, with the result that
Auger—a 250yds. mark—sprinted home ahead of Forbes
(50) and Mick Lewis (90). The handicapping may have
had some bearing on the result, but it certainly didn’t
provide sport on distinct handicap lines. Yet, it would
be quite easy to give an Austral all the interest of a stern,
chase. Why not allot £l5O Of the prize money to the
rider who is leading at the end of six laps?
• • * •
Forbes has the best Austral record —Ist in 1900,
2nd in 1902 (or 1903, owing to the postponement). Kellow
was 3rd in 1891 and Ist five years later. Elliott was 3rd
on two occasions. Nobody else has been placed more
than once thus far.
* * * *
A series of coincidences! The “devil’s year,”
1903, adds up to 13 ; the Austral wheel-race was run on
the 13th month following its predecessor of 1901 ; it was
numbered “event 13” on the official programme; and
was won by Frederic Auger, number 13 on the list of
competitors, and with 13 letters in his name. Mate of
the writer backed Auger, and the penciller’s ticket was
157—total 13.
. *
Robl and Dic-kentman are tall fellows, and
would be conclusive proofs that superior height gives
supremacy in cycling if their performances were not
accomplished under foreign coalitions. Their blasti-
ferous motor is a novelty to Australians, their bike (with
the front wheel smaller than the hind wheel, to admit of
the rider sheltering close to the back of rearward motor-
man) is a novelty likewise, and their machines are tyred
differently from ours. There can be no fair Australian
competition against the strangers. But if Beauchamp’s
defeat of Dickentman was bona fide, there might be a
local man capable of bustling Robl, who doesn’t seem
more than an enlarged edition of the Jack Parsons of
yore.
* * * »
The Sydney Thousand —a mile handicap to
be run at the March cycle meeting at Sydney
Cricket ground—will he the largest prize-money ever
given to a bicycle handicap in the world. It is a big
undertaking, and the N.S. W. League of Wheelmen must
anticipate a long life for the present boom. Sydney
doesn't take on to monotonous handicap-heats at a race-
meeting ; and something more than the ordinary riders
will be required, even if the prize is a thousand pounds.
The public is only' mildly 7 interested, whether the race is
worth £lOOO or a' like number of pence ; it wants variety
and sensationalism. And, judging by the Melbourne
Austral (which is worth £500), there isn’t much fun in a
handicap extending over a couple of days. Howev r, as
the Austral draws an entry 7 of nearly 7 180, it is estimated
that the entry for the Sydney race will total past the 200.
• •* f *
J. M. Hamilton represents M.L. at the forth-
coming Brisbane swimming tournament. He was picked
after winning a couple of championships ; but the meet-
ing was held in such an out-of-the-way place that few
good men turned up. Times recorded were poor, and
within a week were beaten by Claude Bullivant, a young
swimmer of great promise, who is barely 19 yet, and who
met Cavill and Lane when they last visited M.L.
• V * •
George Read’s injured knee-cap didn’t worry
him much in the 500 yards State swimming supremacy at
Balmain last week. Excepting the 250yds., during which
Craig spurted himself out, Read led all the way and beat
boy Corbett by 30yds. in 6.49 2-5. Dick Cavill was unable
to compete owing to the result of revolver practice.
» » * *
A possible aquatic champion this season is
Langdon, of Waverley 7. Originally beaten in the Eastern
Suburbs “hundred ” in 1.7, a fortnight back he did it in
1.3 4-5, and is expected by his club to still further reduce
it. Said that Langdon can carry the crawl stroke up to
220yds., and will use it at the forthcoming furlong State
championship. Langdon is short, well set, and decidedly
strong.
• • • *
Queensland 100yds. swimming supremacy saw
brothers Dan and Frank Gailey and VV. Hetherington fill
the places—first-named winning in 1.4 by 2,vds. from
Hetherington. Fact of Frank Gailey being third sur-
prises Sydney swimmers, as he represent* d Q. in Aus-
tralasian championships decided at M.L. last year. A
really good swimmer then, F.G. looked like improving
much this season.
• • • a
A specimen of splendid vitality is W. Abley, a
one-time top-notch walker who, though on the wrong
side of seventy, is still able to reel off a mile in about
7tnin. and is a familiar figure in exhibition “ walks” on
Vic. country sports-grounds. On New Year’s Day at
Apsley he left a two-mile distance behind in a trifle over
15min.

Whether by mere coincidence or not. the new
way of applying for tickets in Tattersall’s sweeps has
brought luck to S.A. The Wheat State, rarely appearing
among the winners before, has had two first horses and
two seconds within the last six months, totalling well over
£lO,OOO. First in the Perth Cup is the latest success to
an Adelaide syndicate.
• a a a
N.S.W.-S.A. cricket match began Friday, and
dragged along wearily till Tuesday afternoon. S.A.
batted first, and by Saturday had put up 412. By
Monday night N.S.W. had hit up 545, and S.A. scored 60
without losing a wicket. Tuesday afternoon saw them
all out for 134. Howell and Kelly resumed for N.S.W.,
and before they could score sundries accounted for four,
and left N.S.W. victorious by 10 wickets and three runs. ’
• * • *
Should Maclaren bring out a team next season,
it is to be managed, so far as N S.VV. is concerned, by the
local cricket association. Like most N.S.W. institutions,
the Sydney Association is strikingly slow off its mark—
never yet has it brought out unaided an English team.
N.S.W., too, is not only the leading State on the field
(apparently, with a little luck, it could beat Victoria r.nd
S.A. combined), but it supplies about 65 per cent, of the
financial support to the visiting teams ; it is on the fix-
tures on the Sydney cricket ground that the promoter*
confidently rei.v for their greatest profits. In future, the
local folk intend to keep the Sydney takings to them-
selves and the visiting teams, paying all the expenses
incurred in their State only. Any other State can do the
same, and the M.C.O. has consented to the arrangement
which, of course, means everything.
* • * •
Reversals of form in cricket are as startling
as those in gee-gee racing. Trumper, a couple of
seasons ago, failed badly in inter-State cricket, while
piling up vast scores in club cricket against bowling prac-
tically as good. Charlie Gregory is apparently in the
same position this season, having done next to nothing in
inter-State fixtures after tall scores, including a couple of
centuries, among the clubs. There may have been some
slight difference in the bowling, but it was not pro-
portionate to the difference in the scoring.
Just concluded N.S.W. - Queensland cricket
fixture resulted in a profit of £2OO to N.S.W. Cricket
Association the most profitable up to date be-
tween those States. The final destination of all these
profits is a deep mystery. Either the association should
be compelled to start a Disabled Cricketers' Fund or the
whole of the year’s surplus should be paid over to the
State Charities Dept. The men who play the cricket and
really provide the show which draws the money do not
get anything but bare expenses, and if the men who
actually earn the loot do not get it, it should certainly be
devoted to charity.
• • • •
Though Trumper-Duff 298 is a first-wicket
best for Australian batsmen it isn’t an Australian record
—that belongs to Englishmen Maclaren and Hayward
who made 314 against N.S.W. last season. Previous Aus-
tralian batsmen’s top figures (184) belonged to Darling
and Lyons, and were made for S.A. against Vic-
toria five years ago. World’s best under like conditions
was accomplished by J. T. Brown (300) and E. Tunnicliffe
(243), who gathered 554 in the interests of Yorkshire,
opposed to Derbyshire, four years back. This excels all
wicket records. In Jan., 1902, N.S.W. created a world’s
record total of 918 at the expense of S.A. in Sydney. In
this there were five partnerships (each of which brought
something in excess of 100), and five individual cen-
turies.

• * * ■
F. E. Allan, the Australian bowler of auld lang
syne, complains that inter-State cricket nowadays is
played in the same time-wasting style which had become
the' rule in England prior to the advent of the first
Australian Eleven, in 1878. Anyone who can remember
an ordinary English county match 25 years ago will
endorse what “ the bowler of a century ” says about the
awakening influence of the Australian invasion.. Tie
visitors—Allan was one of them—put new life into the
English game by setting an example of enthusiasm and
spr.vness to the lazy, mechanical pros., and the .fussy,
frilly “gentlemen amateurs” of the period.
reporters praised the Australians for their businesslike
punctuality and general alertness, the crowd became
intolerant of the delays and the careless fielding they
had so long been accustomed to, and the local men wc-ke
up. What the Australians did for cricket at’Ome is a
matter of history. All the same, veteran Allan'hasn't
discovered a new grievance. Inter-State cricket
was never more tiresome than in the early g s,
when Murdoch, and Tommy Horan, and other boys of
the old brigade, used to fool around the wicket
between their strokes and “ spar for wind,” so to speak.
But the public were more patient then. They want mere
sport for their money these times. F. E." Allans the
active cricketer of yore, has settled down to be one of the
public, and he complains with the rest of them.
* • * *
Late softgooda Senator Sargood bad the military
microbe badly, but even that worked some good, arid the
dead senator gave many prizes in money and kind to Vic-
torian riflemen. During the past couple of years he must
have given at least 300 Martini-Enfield rifles, and the
ownership of many of them is still undecided. He
founded the cadet system of the State, and at the cadets’
14th annual match recently the “ Sargood Shields ” were
competed for.
■ • « •
Concerning Sydney bowling pennant struggle,
Saturday left VVaverley and Petersham the only unbeaten
teams. Victoria Park and City, looked upon as sure
winners, have each gone under once. Victoria Park and
St. George sustained their first defeat on Saturday, while
Strathfield went under a second time and vanished from
the contest. Of the six clubs eligible to continue, the
City shows the biggest pile of points for, and the smallest
against.
• • • *
M.L. tennis championships were interesting,
and bristled with good play. Singles fell to H. A. Parker’
who defeated last year’s winner, A. J. Peacock. Subse-
quently, last-named and Fisher won the doubles
supremacy. Parker and ex-champion Gore were asso-
ciated in this, but they didn’t survive the semi-finals,
though the doubles handicap fell to them operating from’
a back mark. Ladies’ singles went to Miss Nunneley, who
has spread herself over all similar events since, and
inclusive of, ’95. In ’96, she won the ladies’ singles* top-
notchership of N.S.W., and, a year later, with Mjss
Howitt, gathered up the ladies’ doubles championship of
the same State. H. A. Parker—latest winner of M.L.
gentlemen’s premiership—owned the same honor for
years until 1900, when Victorian A. W. Dunlop beat hint.
Parker was in England in 1901, where, during 15 months
sojourn, he figured in eight tourneys and won 10 prizes.

Maoriland Rugby Union is anxious to send an-
other team to Britain, but the English governing body
refuses to guarantee the financial returns, and insists
upon treating the team as piofessionals. There is also
talk of an Australian tour by an English Association
team in 1904, under Jessop’s leadership. If it dues
eventuate, ’tig to be hoped Jessop’s toeball reputation
won’t slump like his leather-slogging one aid in this
country.
* * • *
“Cocker” Tweedie, once lightweight champ,
of Australia, and ex-Broken Hill miner Hiseutt bruise.it
Sydney Gaiety A.C. last week extended over 10 rounds,
and was mildly exciting and fairly clever. Tweedie,
anxious to retrieve his reputation, had trained well, and
instead of, as heretofore, depending mainly upon smart
evasive methods, he went in and fought, often taking a
punch that wouldn’t have landed but for his desire to
mix it. Tweedie has not achieved his old-time cleverness,
but is shaping well. Towards the finish his strength
petered out a deal—mainly through the manner in \fhich
he had forced things—but skill kept him on deck till the
tenth round when he was sent star-gazing twice. Then the
police stopped the fight, whereupon referee McDonald
gave the money to Hiseutt ; which was wrongs for
Tweedie had scored 20 to 1 better than the silver digger
and was certainly not knocked out.
* * * *
Liverpool (N.S.W.) axeman, Bill Heckenberg,
who now wears the scalps of heavyweights Smith and
Booth (each of whom at one stage of his battle looked a
sure winner), is matched against ’Murkan Jim Scanlan ;
which brings him up among the best in the country.
They fight at Sydney National 8.0., Feb. 3—a week
before the Creedon-M‘Oall contest for Australasian
heavyweight championship. As the woodchopper is a
hard nut to crack—so insensible to heavy punching,
and so full of recuperative power—Scanlan will have to
fight better than he did with “ Soldier Bill” Thompson
or Billy ” M‘Call at Newcastle, to win. Heckenberg can
take weightier blows than the Yankee serves out as a
rule, and hits like a steam-hammer.

“Nob” : Residents of Inglewood (Vic.)—M. J.
Long and D. H. Cooper—recently finished a 12 months
cnb match. They met 199 times and played
3110 games. Long won 1659. Cooper 1451. Twice
Cooper held three fives and, turning up a fourth, scored
20. Same man also had one crib of 24 while Long had
two. These were the highest scores throughout. Has
this a parallel?

One hears it said, in excuse for the muddling
ways of wheel racers : “ If the front men were to r.de
hard from the start, the back men would never get up.”
But it would be the handicapper’s business to see that
the back men and the middle men we re given a show to
catch the limit man in the last lap. Then how about the
preemus pace-making arrangements, and the necessity
for the scratch man to make his sprint on the extreme
outside of the field, if all the others were finishing in a
line t, Tact is, a bicycle race on a }-mile circular track
. esn t present itself to the professional mind as a sport-
ing contest, such as a handicap footrace ought to be, but
generally isn’t. What’s the good of a long start to a limit
man who wants someone to pace him?
FOR DOLLY AND THE KID.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Kalgoorlie Cup-night, and the hall
Is packed from stage to door ;
I hardly seem to realise
It’s me that takes the floor.
I swore to Dolly, when I left,
I’d “ chuck ” the fight for pay,
And here I’m sitting, stripped to meet
A bloke from Bulong way.
These fields ain’t all a “ Golden Mile,”
We’re not all Fords or Greys,
And men with wivqs and kids to keep ■
“ Get round ” in various ways.
I’ve pot had average specker’s luck—
Or dhueked it when I did—
It’s up to me to make a bit
For Dolly and the kid.
They say he’s clever, game and well—
I wish 1 was as fit:
Tinned-dog-meat, tea and soda-bread—
You can’trget right on it.
Still I’m not feeling much amiss,
And four-ounce gloves don’t kill.
Not frequently, at all events,
I’m chancing “ Rushing Bill."
Round One. A cautious, circling spar,
A lead from Bill, a slip—
First blood to me—a choppy left
That Bill stops with his lip.
I think he’s mine, and, mentally.
I wire a hundred quid
To 40 Sloper-street, E.M.,
For Dolly and the kid.
Round Eight. A haze of whirling forms
With several Bills in sight;
A staggering mix, my jaw collides—
I’m told —with William’s right.
A sickening grope upon the boards,
The “ cheque ” a far-off song ;
“ Six ” called, “ seven,” “ eight ” —-I reach my knees,
And then—thank God ! —the gong.
Round Nine. The ring-stakes dance and flit;
Then strength returns—l wait.
And feel that I no longer hit
At Bill in duplicate.
His savage rush is steadied down—
My left was out for gore ;
And, whoof! —a lucky right-hand swing
That sends him to the floor.
'He sleeps, does Bill. The Second’s check
Drones on from “ one ” to “ five,”
Before a twitch of William’s limbs
Announces he’s alive.
“ Six,” “seven ”he gasps; “ eight,” “ nine,” he rolls,
“Ten,” and a deafening shout,
And, in a sort of wondering dream,
I hear them yelling “out!”
We had a friendly snifter
Or two —or was it three?
For William—when you know him—
Ain’t bad, take it from me ;
! I almost felt I loved him
When I wired that hundred quid
To forty Sloper-street, E.M.,
For Dolly and the kid.

Even the common, honorary beak of
Victoria seetns to have but a poor opinion of the
wages paid in the local jam factories. On being
informed that a girl who was charged at a
suburban court with stealing half-a crown from
her brother-in-law was employed at the local
“squash,” the presiding J.P. exclaimed, “Well,
what else can you expect?” Possibly he didn’t
quite intend that insult to a brother employer,
but it’s to be hoped he did.

“ The proposed Federal City,” a pamph’et
by Mr. John Young—the eminent ex-contractor—
of Annandale, Sydney, doesn’t throw much new
light on the Federal City problem, but itcertainly
throws some other things less desirable. Tie
writer has some good and highly commendable
views as to how a model city should be built;
these are not at all new, but still, in the
main, they are excellent, and much valuable
detail is given. Mr. Young also urges that the
city should be built on a 2000 or 3000 ft. elevation
—a point which The Bulletin has strongly in-
sisted upon. But he grumbles the old, silly grumble
because one session of the Australian Parliament
has passed without the site for a capital being
selected ; yet he doesn’t seem to be any nearer
to a definite opinion as to where the city
should be than the Australian Parliament is—
perhaps not so near. He doesn’t urge with any
energy worth mentioning the necessity of re-
serving a large area (5000 sq. miles or so) for the
Federal Territory, so that the increase of
land - values created by the expenditure of
public money on a new city shall be all,
or nearly all, Federal property, and not
be an increase in the value of privately-
owned land inside the Federal Territory ; and so
that all the required water catchment area shall
be under Federal control. The absolute necessity
for making the soil of the Federal territory the
inalienable property of the Commonwealth for
ever, and of planting the Federal Territory where
it w m’t be wholly surrounded by the territory of
one State, and thus, to some extent, dominated by
that State in the matterof railway communication,
&c., is not even mentioned And the villainous
cloven hoof of syndicatism sticks out conspicuously
in John Young’s suggestion that a “ British,
American, or Australian trust or company ”
should get the job of laying out the new city,
paving, draining, lighting, bringing in water, &c.,
the reason assigned being that “ the Government
will only make a mess of it, as is not unusual in
Government undertakings.” Australia has many
recollections of British and American trusts or
companies—the Mildura Co., the Maoriland Mid-
land Railway Co., the Tasmanian Main Line Co.,
the Emu Bay Railway Co., the V.D.L. Co., the
Midland Railway Co. of W.A., the Adelaide
tramway-grab affair, the Brisbane Tramway Co.,
the Australian Agricultural Co., Hampden Plains
Co., South Australian Co., Peel River Co., the
dawdling Great Western Railway Co. of Tas-
mania, the Westralian timber companies, the
Great Southern Railway Co. of W.A., and many
others; and, after looking at the awful conse-
quences, it would rather trust to O’Sullivan.
The State is reckless, but honest, in so far as our
rulers very, very seldom do public swindles for
the benefit of their private pockets. Syndicatism
is reckless, and a hopeless swindle to boot.
