Colonel Madley and Inspector Sullivan,
chiefs of Adelaide police force, are very proud of
the fact that there was not a single arrest for
drunkenness in the city on New Year’s Eve. The
constables gave liquor considerable latitude, and
men who could carry their internal breweries and
distilleries respectably were not interfered with.
All the housebreakers and pickpockets were lured
into indiscretions and locked-up before the holi-
days so as to ensure a comfortable Xmas.
Dec. 20 a Bulletin correspondent used
the word “ tabloid ” in a par. re the provisioning
of life-boats (meaning a compressed and easily
portable form of food-preparation). Burroughs,
Wellcome and Co., the noted manufacturing
chemists, now write pointing out that “tabloid”
is, as a word, their private property, coined by
them and legally usable only as regards their pro-
ducts. Fact is that B.W. and Co.’s “tabloids”
have won so wide a world-vogue that the word
has become a part of the English language.
Whether a firm can exercise a legal remedy
against the language for this sort of robbery is a
very nice point indeed.
The newly-enfranchised women voters of
N.S.W. should read The Bulletin’s “A Policy
for the Commonwealth,” by James Edmond.
Post free from Bulletin office for 7d.
It is a common gibe that the Australian Govern-
ment is “ subservient to the Labor party.” Sup-
posing it is, what else could be reasonably
expected ? The Labor party is the only one with
an all-Australian programme and an all-Aus-
tralian organisation. It is the only one which
doesn’t split up regularly over alleged “State
rights,” and fly off the handle over State preju-
dices, and which doesn’t run amok and demand
that the whole globe shall be given to its particu-
lar State. The Queensland section isn’t liable to
leave the Government in the lurch over a demand
that the anti-Alien Law shall be suspended as
regards its State, nor does the N.S.W. section
want to know why a big marble capital hasn’t
been built in its State inside 18 months ; nor
does the S.A. section demand quite all
the waters of the Murray for its own
use. A party which knows what it wants,
and states its position exactly, which sel-
dom has parochial fits or local starts, which
doesn’t disrupt every now and then into three or
four State factions each demanding the whole
earth, which is an Australian party—-not a mass of
Kilkenny cats, is a glad thing for any Government
to meet. The Labor party is by far the nearest
resemblance to an Australian party that the Aus-
tralian Parliament possesses, and amid the chaos
of State rows any Government might well wish
that there was more of it.
The Jenkins Govt, of S. Aus. wants to get every-
thing at the bargain counter. On education it
spends, according to Coghlan, Bs. 9d, per head
of its population a year, while N.S.W. spends
12s. 4d., Queensland 11s. 3d., and Vic. 10s. 9d. ;
and still it demands it cheaper.
Already of 500 country schools 450 are officered entirely
by untrained people ; when a person of moderate edu-
cation fails in S. Aus., instead of hanging out the sign of
“ general agent ” he becomes a provisional teacher, and
in a few years, and with some luck, he may gi t. nearly £7O
a year. That is one reason why the cost is so low.
Another is the employment of large numbers of women
(on the usual woman-wage scale) to do men’s work. In
Flinders-street (Adelaide) school, the largest in the State,
the last report shows there are 10 teachers, and only two
of them males! And still the Jenkins Govt, wants it a
little cheaper ; and Ministers talked of the great burden
of the free-education system, and trembled Jest it should
“break down of its own weight”; and finally they
appointed a commission of experts to see what was wrong.
If, however, the real object, as some people suspect, was
to get an excuse to resume the collection of school
fees, and so save incomes and estates from a few ext-a
farthings of taxation, the commission must have proved
a sorry disappointment; for it reports that fees cannot
be again collected without impairing efficiency, and Govt,
has said that efficiency must not be impaired. All, in fact,
that the Commission can suggest to turn this very cheap
system into one a little cheaper still is to make the schools
and salaries in each class bear a fixed ratio to the whole.
Hitherto, as schools increased in size, they got into a
higher class, carrying with them higher salaries ; so the
number of high-class schools and salaries has been
growing. For the future, if' this new plan is adopted,
there will be a fixed percentage of the schools in each
class ; and promotion of teachers will be made on the
reports of an advisory board of inspectors. It isn’t a big
or imposing scheme, though in a few years it may make
an annual saving of £13,000 (in a total of about £150,000);
but it is worth mentioning because it aims at a few
anomalies, and almost knocks out the absurd but easy
system of wasting good men because they aren’t so old in
the service as a number of useless ones. And, best of all,
it ought to suffice to knock out the half-ashamed agita-
tion to resume the fee system.

Extract from the report of an otherwise
dry case concerning chaff, heard recently in
Sydney:—
The defendants said they never agreed, as alleged, and
denied that they would not accept or pay for the goods ;
and that the plaintiffs had not fulfilled their contract
within proper and reasonable time ; that the chaff was
not prime, bright, wheaten straw chaff ; that they were
never indebted, as alleged ; and that, before the action
was brought, they satisfied and discharged plaintiff’s
claim for payment.
No doubt it is all right in a legal sense, but to the
non-legal mind it reads that defendants didn’t
order the chaff ; that they didn’t refuse to accept
the chaff they didn’t order ; that the other party
didn’t duly fulfil the agreement which wasn’t
made; that the chaff wasn’t up to the mark
specified in the contract that didn’t exist ; that
defendants never owed for the chaff ; and that
they had already paid the money they didn’t owe.
It doesn’t seem (to the lay mind) as if much could
be piled on that defence, unless defendants added
that they were dead when they made the contract,
and that plaintiff had never been born, and that
both parties were mad and absent from the
country.
When the Sydney alderman sees an open
space, his first idea is to tip garbage on it. The
Waverley aldermen now deposit the public evi-
dence of their dirty habits on a sloping piece of
ground between Ocean and Wellington streets,
Bondi. There are houses all round, and the tip
smells on them with a deafening smell. It isn’t
fenced in ; the garbage is hardly covered at all ;
small boys and animals disturb the gruesome col-
lection and wake up its virulence; and the rains
wash sections of tip down on to the flats below
and spread them over great distances. No tip
ought to be permitted in a populated area unless
an alderman sits on it in his best clothes from
6 a.m. fofit'.m—the nlrtprmeri to take tnr r ' c \
The £lOO prize offered by the Grocers’
Guild in London for a safe oil-lamp is still going
begging. The only conditions are cheapness and
a certainty of going out when upset, but none of
the hundreds of samples submitted have passed
examination.

IAYNE’S EXPECTORANT
CURES THE WORSTCOUCHS.
The Hypocrite.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Thb man had just gone who was to be her husband
in a week’s time. She was angry with herself for
caring so very little, and with him for caring
so much. She was sick of herself, of him, of
everything. Sick to death of acting up to the
man’s ideal of her, of listening with soft smiling
eyes to words that had no meaning for her. She
roamed round the room restlessly, wondering how
she could get rid of her thoughts. The sight of
the writing-paper lying on the table suggested that
she would put them down on paper, write them
down in all their cruel hard truth :
Dear Philip,—You have just gone, and lam glad If
you only-knew how you weary me, how deadly tired lam
of hearing you sav you love me, you would never mention
it again as long as you live. To-night when you kissed
me I wished I was dead. O God !It reminded me of a
night five years ago when the man I loved held me in his
arms and kissed me. Only once. Only one instant. But
long enough to dry up all the blood round my heart and
make it like a dead thing. I knew it would never be re-
peated. .1 knew he did not care as I did, but kissed me
because of the love he read in my eyes.
You say my eyes are hard to read, that they refuse to
tell you anything. Are you blind ? Can’t you see that
they are cold because there is no fire in my heart for you •
nothing behind them when they look on you ?
You think I am shy of showing my love ; that I fear to
let vou see how great it is. And you honor me for my
modesty.
And yet they say you are clever ; that you are the most
brilliant man in your profession. Perhaps it is because
your intellect is so immense that you cannot stoop low
enough to see through a woman’s shams.
Philip, I’m going to marry you because you are rich ;
be ause I’m tired of being poor ; tired of wearing cheap
frocks and shabby boots. One day I found a white hair
in my head. That night I promised to be your wife.
You may thank the white hair for that.
In all the world to night I don’t think there is a woman
so miserable as I. And it is because of the part of me
that is dead, the part that you never knew, for it died
five years ago. The Me of then would not know the Me
who is going to marry you. The other Me would scorn
to do what I will do next week. But then I did not know
that the rosy apples were made of cinder. I believed
that life was sweet and fair, and that heart-aches and
bitterness were only the wages of sin.
Well, I’ve written what was in my heart, and, if I
rlaredj;l would let you read it. You would hate me. A
man never forgives the woman who tells him the truth.
So I must not, for I know there will be more white
hairs soon.
Through force of habit I’ll sign myself,
Your loving
Edtth.
She laughed as she read over the startling truths
she had written, and decided not to destroy the
paper till the morning. Pushing it under the
blotting-pad she went to bed.
The next day she had to go into town early to
have her wedding-dress fitted on, and was so tired
by the time the dressmaker was finished with her
that she went home earlier than she intended.
When she got in, her sister told her that
Philip was in the drawing-room, and had asked
if he might write a note that he had
forgotten. In a flash she remembered the paper
she had left under the blotting-pad, and, with a
face as white as her handkerchief, went into the
room. -Philip was sitting at the table, and he
held an open letter m his hand. He turned
quiekly when he heard her step, and dropped the
paper as lie came to meet her with outstretched
hands.
“Darling,” he said, looking at her with shining
eyes, “so you had written me a letter! I was
looking for some notepaper when I saw my name.
I did not get any further, but I’ll read it now, if I
may.”
The girl had it in her hands, and was holding it
tightly as she smiled up into his face.
“No, Phil,” she said, “I’ll read it to you. You
sit over there in that armchair, and if you move
once I’ll tear it up.” The man laughed as he did
what she said, and watched her as she stood near
the door and read :
Dear Philip,—You have only just left me, and yet I feel
I must write to you before Igo to bed. I want to tell you
how happy I am. How proud that you should choose me
from all other women to be your wife. [Oh, I don’t think
I’ll read any more. Well, you must not come near me.
or I really will tear it up.] You who are so clever and
brilliant. . . . They say that you are the most brilliant
man at the Bar ! Is it any wonder that I feel .. . shy
with you sometimes? lam frightened that you will dis-
cover what a silly little thing I am, and that ... I will
bore you. (No, don’t come over, Phil, or I’ll keep my
word!) Do you know why lam going to marry you?
Because . . . because .. . (she seemed to have lost her
breath) because I . . . love you !
The man came to her and put his arms round
her. He looked deep down into her eyes as
though he would read her very soul. What lie
saw there seemed to satisfy him, for he said,
“ You need not read any more, Edith. You have
said the words that are the sweetest in the world
for me to hear. And I will keep the letter they
were written in until lam dead.”
But the girl was fiercely tearing up the paper
and had thrown the bits into the fire before the
man could prevent her. One little fragment he
rescued, and the girl’s heart stood still as she saw
there was writing on it. “Your loving Edith,”
he read aloud. “Well, I’m glad I’ve got that
much of my letter,” he said with a laugh, “and
I’ll keep it as a memento of the dearest letter
you ever wrote me.”
ALICE MACK.


Mr. James Alston’s steel windmill factory,
South Melbourne, is the largest concern in the
Australian States for the manufacture of these
essential aids to settlement. And his work goes
outside of this country. The fame of his mills is
world-wide. They are suitable for all class of
work, from supplying a household to flooding an
irrigation field. Many a farmer and grazier in
the dry districts speaks with gratitude of the
Alston Steam Windmill.*

COMMISSIONERS OF
SAVINGS BANKS.
FIFTH ISSUE
OP
Debentures for £lOO,OOO,
at 3 2 l Per Cent.,
to nature 30th April, 1908.
To be issued under the authority of the
Acts of Parliament of Victoria,
Nos. 1481, 1722, and 1778.
CONDITIONS FOR TENDERS.
1. Tenders will be receivable at the Commissioners’
Head Office, 29 Market-street, Melbourne, for the whole
or any portion of the above amount until noon on Thurs-
day, 29th January, 1903, when tenders will be opened in
the presence of tenderers or their agents who may
attend
2. Each tender must be sealed, addressed to “The
Commissioner of Savings Banks, Melbourne,” and en-
dorsed “ Tender for Debentures.”
3. Debentures will be issued in denominations of £lOO,
or £5OO, as may be desired by successful tenderers, and’
applicants must state in their tenders the number and
value of the debentures they desire to receive.
4. Interest at 3£ per cent, per annum will be payable
half-yearly on 30th April and 31st October, the first such
payment being due on 31st October, 1903, at the Commis-
sioner’s Head Office, or at any Savings Bank in Victoria.
5. Any tender below £99 per £lOO will not be enter-
tained.
6. Tenderers of the highest premium will have pre-
ference. and to tenderers of even rates, debentures will
be allotted proportionately to the applications as nearly
as may be practicable. Tenders at a price includin', a
fraction of a shilling other than sixpence will not "be
preferentially accepted by reason of such fraction.
7. Any tender received from the Savings Banks De-
partment shall not be allowed to compete with other
tenders, but all other tenders shall have preference.
8. Tenders must be accompanied by a deposit in cash
or by a hank draft payable to the Commissioners of
Savings Banks, amounting to 10 per cent, of the face
-value of the debentures applied for, and if the tender be
accepted in full, or pro rata, the balance must be paid on
or before 31st March, 1903 or may be paid at any earlier
date. Interest at 3£ per cent, per annum will be allowed
on accepted tenderer’s lodgments of 10 per cent, and over
from date of receipt to 30th April, 1903, and will be pay-
able up to that date on the issue of the debentures.
9. The deposit of 10 per cent, will be forfeited to and
become the absolute property of the Commissioners in

'Ome papers state that Walter Howe, some years back
well-known here as an excellent “ leading man,” has left
off dabbling in the “milky way” and gone back to his
original “starring.” This recondite joke requires ex-
planation. When Howe returned to London, from Aus-
tralia, he “chucked the profesh ” and bought a milk-
walk. He has now returned to the “boards.” The
cow-fluid business seems to be rather a remarkable one
for an actor of ability to take to, but the fact that Walter
was born and brought-up on his father’s farm may account
for his lacteal proclivities.
“ J.A.0.”: The late J. F. Cathcart had a marked vocal
peculiarity which none of your correspondents seemed to
have noticed. He bleated. When Wm. Rignold came to
Australia, some years ago, Cathcart met him and, in
silence, greeted him with outstretched hand. For a
moment W.R. did not recognise his old friend, whom he
had not seen for many years. “ What, William, don’t
you know me?” came in sheeplike tones from “ Jimmy.”
“ Good Lord ! Cathcart! ” was the prompt reply. The
late veteran was certainly not eminent in his profession,
but he never spoiled anything, and in one rdle, Stukely,
in "The Gamester,” in which I saw him in the early Ws
(when he was associated with Mr. and Mrs. Chas. Kean),
he displayed a dramatic power and finesse which would
have been creditable to any actor.
Sandow got a mayoral reception in Sydney. In Maori-
land he only got a demand that he should deposit £25 as
security that he wouldn’t leave the State without paying
his income-tax.
Adelaide had never seen Cinquevalli before, and when
he did his juggling with the heavy cannon-ball the
“ gods ” reckoned it was bluff, and said so. “Chinky”
talked at them for a few minutes, and next night demon-
strated with a hen’s egg and a china plate how he
acquired the trick.
That sound and experienced musical authority, Mr. De
Cairos Rego, late musical critic of Sydney Daily Tele-
graph, writing from London to that paper, says of
Patti : “It must be that she conjures up for her old
admirers all the past glories and delights associated with
her name—a reminiscent pleasure—but to a stranger who
hears her for the first time, there is little in her perform-
ance to create admiration or enjoyment, simply astonish-
ment, that she still does so well. Yet the adulation of
the London audience could scarcely be more pronounced
when Patti was in her prime ; both voice and vocal
style, apparent!}’, were enchanting.” Dealing with a
concert at St. James’s Hall, the writer says : “ Mr. Ben
Davies’ most important contribution was Gounod’s
‘ Salve Dimora,’ and, if one may make comparisons, the
performance of the new English tenor would not rank
alongside that of Mr. Philip Newbury.” Of Florence
Sbhmidt, Mr. Rago reports: “The Queensland soprano
has attained to quite a perfect placing of the voice, and
the charm of her style, so markedly good before she left
Sydney, has gained greatly in significance. Miss Schmidt
is shortly to wed the well-known sculptor, Mr. F. Derwent
Wood.”
G. De Oairos Rego, writing to Sydney D.T. from
London, says that Paderewski has engaged to come to
Australia for a series of recitals, bearinning the first week
in July, 1904, under the management of flautist John
Lemmone. Having heard him a few nights previously at
Ipswich, Mr. Rego, who ought to know, whets the appe-
tite thus: “Power and delicacy, technique and tem-
perament, were so abundantly shown ; he wove such
meaning into the music, and he made the piano so
startlingly eloquent at times, that it is no wonder he
carried his audience away.”
The question of a Williamson and Musgrove combine
will be definitelv settled, one way or other, when Robert
Courtneidge arrives on the scene to stage-manage the
“Midsummer Night’s Dream” production, which Mus-
grove has so loTVg been talking about Courtneidge, who
represents a solid Manchester syndicate, and is now a
potentate of the first water, will have an important
say in the fixing of any new Australian “firm.” A com-
bine would be based on the scheme of a wide theatrical
circuit, including America, S’Africa, and perhaps India.
“ Haresfoot” : I find a reference to the projected Gee-
long theatre as an entirely new thing, so long has the
recollection of the Past been buried. Ooppin’s Geelong
theatre was turned into a brewery, then into the Salva-
tion Army barracks. Nesbitt M’Cron acted there, and
Brooke followed. M‘Cron lies in the old burial-ground at
Geelong.
“Melb”: The first “Whittington” panto, in Mel-
bourne was in 1857, when Fanny Morgan played the boy.
Tbe Leopold Family made its debfit in the harlequin-
ade. In the next Melb. outbreak of “ Whittington ” two
theatres presented it the same Christmas—the Royal and
the old wooden match-box, Princess’s, where Stirling
White (now of Anderson’s co.) was in the cast. Some 10
years back Billee Barlow was a smart Whittington, for
Coppin at the Royal—a big, successful production. Sub-
sequently, Maggie Moore figured in another version of
the old panto, yarn, and wore an enormous hat with
“Dick” branded on the broad rim to establish her
identity.

Pearson’s Weekly tells in graphic style
how it cost Q. Govt. £20,000 to capture the
Kenhiffs ; how Dovle and Dahlke caught Jim
Kentiiff asleep while Patrick Kenniff peppered
them from an ambush, and how the Kenniffs
began their criminal career by murdering a
Chinese teamster and plundering his waggon.
Next number of Pearson’s will probably tell how
the Kenniffs fought in armor, like Ned Kelly, and
were finally apprehended while attempting to
surround George Reid and to drown him along
with his dry dog.
The last of Senator Sargood. A Maori-
land correspondent writing to The Bulletin
before his death
Mr. Sargood, who, I believe, is fairly well-known in
Australia as a successful dealer in softgoods and a
“ toney” member of the Senate, always manages, when
over here, to illuminate the darkness of our understand-
ing through the medium of a press interviewer. He has
just been letting himself go about Commonwealth
politics, and, among other things, he assures us that the
greatest menace to social reform in the Commonwealth
is the Labor party, because (1) it is well-organised and
(2) it is uncompromisingly opposed to militarism !
Carnegie, who, whatever his shortcomings,
at least knows a good deal about accumulating
wealth, says this
The home market of America takes 96 per cent, of all
manufactured articles ; only 4 per cent, goes to foreign
markets. Even Britain’s home market takes four-fifths
of the manufactures; only one-fifth goes abroad.
Politicians give far too much attention to distant
foreign markets, which can never amount to much,
and far too little to measures for improving conditions
at home, which would increase the infinitely more im-
portant home market. If the people of the United
Kingdom could spend even £1 per head more per year,
her home commerce would be increased more than the
total value of her exports to all of Australasia, British
North America, and China combined. Truly foreign
commerce is a braggart always in evidence, home com-
merce the true king.
The Freetrader, whose little pewter joss is “ex-
Eorts and imports,” should ponder. If you make
oots, or steel rails, or silks or calicoes in Sydney
and send them to Bathurst, getting wool in ex-
change, that is just as much commerce as if
Bathurst got these things in England and sent
wool to England in exchange. And our own
people get the wages ; also our own people save
the sea-freight money.

ways have been supplied with kodaks, with
instructions to take snapshots of employees whom
they may find leaning on their shovels yarning,
displaying any lassitude, or blowing their noses
too often.
The casual Australian female might take a
lesson from one reported in recent Warwickshire
papers. She applied in a Birmingham court for a
protection-order under the Married Women’s
Property Act; and on inquiry as to the nature of
her husband’s wrongdoing she explained that she
was not married, but engaged, and wanted a pro-
tection-order “ in case he turns out a bad
husband.” ,
It was obvious from the first that there
was nothing in the drunkard’s yarn from Maori-
land that his mate committed the Gatton (Q.)
murders. The authorities in Queensland are
thoroughly sure that they know the culprits and
the reason for the murders, but are not certain as
to being able to establish a moral certainty as a
legal proof. The theory finally accepted by the
Queensland authorities was first formulated in
The Bulletin office, and grew out of a state-
ment made by one of the persons whom the
police naturally first interrogated concerning the
mysterious deed of blood.
The usual yarn ! Sydney Telegraph de-
voted half-a-column last Tuesday to a screech on
the relative progress in the 10 yeaJte from 1890 to
1900 of Victoria under Protection and N.S. W.
under Freetrade. Think of that ! And N.S. W. had
the considerably Protective tariff of Freetraders
Parkes, M'Millan, Carruthers and the two Smiths
(Bruce and Pumpkin) till the end of 1891; the
still more Protective tariff of G. Dibbs till 1896;
and the partially-Protective tariff of George Reid
after that. These, with 50 per cent. Protection
under the postal laws to Freetrade newspapers,
and £20,000,000 of borrowed money, make up ten
years of Freetrade in N. S.W. Ten years of
Freetrade, be it observed.
The bounding Wragge, who established
his weather station at Mt. Kosciusko as his own
private speculation, though he persuaded the
Reid Govt, to give him sundry grants in aid of it,
states that the present N.S.W. Ministry has “ re-
pudiated all liability” to pay the debts which
have accumulated on the show, and unless it
“recognises its obligations” in the matter he
must appeal to the press for funds. To which
Premier See responds, in effect, that N.S.W.
Govt, has paid any money it agreed to pay,
and that when batches of bills come in to it on
account of anybody’s private weather-prophecy
spec., the matter comes under the head of cheek—
not meteorology. Australia had almost forgotten
the weather establishment at Kosciusko. Nothing
of any interest has come from there, except occa-
sional reports of the struggles of the man in charge
to get up or down. Certainly the Kosciusko show
has cast no light on the drought, whereby it lost a
good chance of being interesting.
Someone whom Sydney Telegraph calls
Krakoff and S.M. Herald Krukoff, and who is a
gentleman from Russia with mediaeval ideas,
has been expressing horror because the Aus-
tralian agriculturist does not work 16 hours a
day as the Russian does, and because the Aus-
tralian women don’t toil more generally in the
fields. On which Granny Herald comments in
her cautious way :—
It is open to doubt whether it would be an advantage
to the race if our Australian women, like their Russian
sisters, were to work in the fields as long and as hard as
their men folk.
If someone proposed to re-establish the Inquisition,
or to boil a few Papists, the Herald would
merely say that “ the advisability of such a step
was open to some doubt ” ! Or else it would say
that the grilling of Dissenters should not be done
without serious consideration, and the matter wa3
not one to be entered upon with undue haste, it
being a somewhat unusual departure, which ad-
mitted of much argument and was liable to mis-
conception,
Stanley James, aged 20 years, had his left
leg snapped off from the middle of the thigli by a
big shark while bathing in Lane Cove River,
Sydney, on Saturday afternoon last. The brute
let go once, but only to make a more effectual
bite, and finally it swam off with the severed
limb. The victim showed great courage, and
when taken out of the water said to his bearers
more than once, “I fought the brute!” A
quickly-improvised tourniquet was at once ap-
plied, but the unfortunate young man soon died
of loss of blood—it being impossible for
any human being to survive a minute or
two’s draining of the femoral artery. The
Parramatta River and its tributary, being shallow
and very warm, in summer afford great attrac-
tions to sharks, numerous, large and singularly
ferocious—they have been known to leap parti-
ally out of the water for the purpose of eating
persons who happened to be dangling their legs
from low stagings. Years ago, present writer
saw a man named Rooney killed by a shark in
2ft. 6in. of water at Albert Park, Melbourne.
The brute made a wound right round and through
its victim’s thigh, actually chipping but not sever-
ing the bone. It was beaten off its prey by a
mounted man, who tackled it with a hammer-
headed whip, but the injured man died at once.
How the shark contrived to make a cut right
through the thigh-flesh as clean as the sweep of
an operating-knife without severing the bone con-
stituted a surgical puzzle.
The Governor having declined to interfere,
Patrick Kenniff was executed (by a hangman in a
false beard and goggles) at Brisbane, on Monday,
for the murder of Constable Doyle. The con-
demned man took the matter quite coolly, and,
on the scaffold, averred his absolute innocence—
he was, he said, as guiltless as the judge who tried
him. The body was lowered into a “beautiful
cedar coffin with silver mountings,” and half-an-
hour after the convict had been protesting his
innocence, his corpse was turned over to his rela-
tives and on its way to the burying-ground, where
several hundreds of people gathered to witness the
interment.

Rev. Tremayne Dunstan, who has come
baok to Sydney to explain everything and to
promise to pay everything—some day—announces
that he doesn’t want to run in opposition to the
dear old church in Pitt-st.,” but he will, nathless,

testant Hall. Mr. Dunstan probably seriously
over-estimates his powers when he reckons that

The N.S.W. Public Service Acts Consoli-
dating Act of 1901, like many of the Consolidating
Acts passed wholesale by a complacent Legisla-
ture, is not the mere matter-of-form it was thought
to be: —
It is now found that clause 62 of the ’95 Act, under
which hundreds of contributors to the Superannuation
Fund were permitted to withdraw, has bodily disappeared
from the Consolidated Act, and the Crown Law people
say that to conserve the rights of these ex-contributors a
new Act will have to be passed next session. It is not to
be supposed that Government will take any advantage of
this accident, but it is feared that any claims arising
before the new Bill is passed will be met with difficulty.
Yet another proof of the over-haste with which
Parliament dealt with the Consolidating business.
How such a vital point, of grave concern to thou
sands of persons, escaped notice, is incompre-
hensible.

Edith Sellars, in the Nineteenth Cen-
tury :
“ Were I a worn-out worker I would like to change
my nationality and become a Dane, an Austrian or a
Russian ; for of all European nations these three best
understand how to deal with the old and destitute.
Their homes are the brightest and cheeriest of resorts.
In Denmark, by a law of 1891, any old man or women who
can show a decent record is housed, fed and clothed at
the expense of the nation as an honored veteran of
industry. .... The cost per head in Danish homes
averages one shilling a day. In the most comfortless of
London work-houses it is Is. Ud It costa more
in England to make old people miserable than the other
nations spend in making their aged happy.”
France seems to be getting out of her
decrsasing-birth-rate trouble. In 1900 deaths
exceeded births by 5,988; in 1901 there was an
excess of births of 72,398.
Died at Wangaratta hospital recently,
Thomas Plunkett, one of the first twelve pioneers
who arrived at Ballarat after the discovery of
gold there, early in ’5l, and helped to develop the
first local reef—in Magpie Gully. He was known
on every goldfield in the State, where he had
roamed for 64 years, his hobby of late years being
to visit the sites of the old camp fires, and to
prospect for old leads.

gradually improved. lam glad to say that the two lots
of medicine you sent have effected a complete cure, for
which accept my very best thanks.—Yours gratefully,
J. BLAIR.

The work of removing the waste matters that result from
the digestive processes falls chiefly on the bowels, iiver, and
kidneys. If these organs become disordered and inactive,
Constipation and Dyspepsia occur, and should they stop
working altogether for a few hours, death would result from
the accumulated poisons in the body. While these poisons
are not harmful when present in a normal quantity, they
propagate disease if allowed to accumulate in the system.
Perfect action of all these eliminative organs is therefore
necessary to keep the body in sound health. The gateways
of excretion must be kept free, and the filters must be clean.
This is now readily accomplished by using KUTNOW’S
POWDER, which cleanses and stimulates the Bowels, Liver
and Kidneys, and when deranged and sluggish restores them
to a strong, healthy, active condition. As the organs regain
their ability to perform their several functions, the blood
will become purer, the nerves stronger, the appetite keener,
sleep sounder, and the skin healthier. Headache, bilious-
ness, and drowsiness will vanish, and the entire body be
rendered stronger and more vigorous.
The use of “ liver pills ” in the treatment of liver dis-
orders may in most instances be compared to the whipping
of an exhausted horse when each application of the scourge
only tends to hasten entire collapse.
KUTNOW’S POWDER is an aperient in which the
drastic, .lowering, and nauseous features of the ordinary
aperient medicine are entirely absent. It is composed of

European mineral-spring waters, scientifically blended with

KtJTNOW S POWDER does for the Stomach, Liver,

which practically renders the body proof against disease in

A Social Stereo.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Since the death of her husband, Fortune had not
once smiled on Mrs. Lister, who, from being
mistress of a modest villa and two servants, had
become the keeper of a second-rate boarding-
house, all the work of which she did unaided.
A bill-of-sale on all her household belongings
hung menacingly over Mrs. Lister’s head. She
had been forced into a money-lender’s hands, and
those hands squeezed till she would have thought
death welcome, did not three dirty, chubby faces
as insistently say “No.” She struggled on with
magnificent hopelessness, despairing, yet heroic—
a mother for her children.
Seekers after lodgings seemed to pass her by as
if they were in league with the Fates. Across the
street, Mrs. Johns, with attractions for boarders
neither better nor worse, had not to tear her
heart with struggling as she had. Two rooms, a
double and a single, were empty, and had re-
mained empty for three weeks. It was an effort
to pay rent and interest; the tradesmen’s bills
began to appear as tigers ready to spring. How
could she ever hope to appease them ?
She was mechanically dusting a chest-of-
drawers, and was full of bitter, blasphemous
thoughts, that were victoriously driving before
them the last ragged legions of hope, when she
came across a letter, a love-letter received during
the months when the world was a paradise and
the postman who brought Harry’s letter each day
.tn angel. She read it, and felt herself floating back
through the years of married life until she was
once more a fluttering sweetheart. She did not
■ear the approaching footsteps of seven-year-old
Fanny.
“ Mutnma, there’s a lady and a gentleman
wants to see you at the door.”
fhe 12 years passed in a flash. She was once
core a struggling widow with three children,
valiantly facing starvation.
“ Yes, my dear, I’m coming.”
She kissed the golden-haired mite, as once more
hope began to throb within her. At the door a
,oung man and a girl stood, embarrassed, unable,
uther of them, to express themselves. Mrs.
bister summed up the situation at a glance, and
at once sympathy, born of sweet remembrance,
sprang to life for this forlorn-looking couple.
“ I have a double room to let, ” she said.
require board as well,” the young man
After a few more words and an inspection of
the room, he paid the thirty-seven and sixpence
for the week, and then departed with his wife for
their belongings.
* • * »
“Seven pounds five and fourpence, and not a
penny for three weeks,” a surly looking man
growled.
“I know, Mr. Ganfin,” Mrs. Lister replied
but what am I to do?”
Mr. Ganfin growled back that that was her
business. If she wanted him to serve her with
meat, he must have his money. She would have
liked to reply that she di 1 not want him to serve
her. He was an unsatisfactory tradesman, and
she would have dealt with him no longer than she
could help. But she was heavily in debt to him.
“ Well, can’t you pay it in instalments—say,
two ten now, and another two-ten on Friday ? ”
Mrs. Lister said she did not at that moment
possess fifty shillings. Would thirty shillings be
enough ?
“Let us have it then.”
The payment was made. When Ganfin had
gone, she knew she had made a mistake in parting
with the money. She should have delayed him a
little longer, for, although the butcher’s impor-
tunities were hard to bear, she was not at his
instant mercy, as was the case with the money-
lender with his bill-of-sale. To excuse herself,
she reflected that she was fortunate in having any
money at all that day.
The widow and the wife had been exchanging
the usual feminine confidences. The wife had
said: “Of course, no one is supposed to know
that we are married. If it were known, he would
lose his billet. It is the rule of the bank.”
Mrs. Lister paid hardly any attention to this.
The dreadful cloud of the bill-of-sale had come
over her mind. She had expected the money-
lender’s collector that day, but he had so far not
put in an appearance. Perhaps, she tried to hope,
she might the following day be better prepared
for his coming. But he might still come to-day.
A knock at the door turned her heart to ice, and,
whei} she foupd it was not the collector, she nearly
fainted from the spasm of relief.'
“Why, it’s Dick,” cried Mrs. Fanning, with
the joyful trill of the young wife in her voice,
Fanning entered the room, pale and worn, look-
ing 10 years older than when he had gone away in
the morning.
“They have found out,” he said. “ They dis-
missed me at once. I have been looking all day
for a billet. I thought I might come home with
good news after all. But I ”
A look of consternation spread over the wife’s
face. She felt miserable because her Dick was
miserable rather than at the prospect of a bitter
struggle with poverty. She fell to re-assuring
him, and decided that he would surely be success-
ful to-morrow.
The prevailing feeling of Hopefulness infected
Mrs. Lister, who a few minutes later was called
away by another knock at the door. Her dread
was gone, for it was too late now for the collector.
A middle-aged man of respectable appearance
wanted a room. He inspected the single room,
but it was too small and cramped. He would
have preferred the double room. Unable to suit
himself, he went away. If the Fannings, she
thought . . .
Next day Dick Fanning returned home with
good news. He had obtained a positiqn at fifty
shillings a week, five shillings in advance of what
he had received at the bank. Elsie was like a
bird iq spring, and joyful that, since it was
Tuesday, she would have Dick all to herself until
Monday, when he was to re-coinmence work. He
felt relieved now that there would be no need to
leave Mrs. Lister until he and Elsie could set up
house for themselves.
* • « *
The money-lender’s collector was a far more
truculent and disagreeable brute than the butcher.
“I should have called yesterday, and as I
didn’t, you ought to part-up better for it.”
Mrs. Lister asked for an extension of time.
“ I can’t let you off any longer. To-morrow I
must put the bill-of-sale into execution. I must
have my money.”
Mrs. Lister pleaded as she had never before
pleaded to anyone on this earth. The voices of
the children playing upstairs were heard all the
while. Their prattle seemed mightier than the
voice of God ; and, thus inspired, her appeal was
successful. The collector relented.
“Till Monday. Then the execution,” were bis
last words.
* * * ♦
Monday morning. The last pennies had gore
to buy bread. But there was the Fannings’
money to come. That would suffice to secuie
another postponement from the money-lender,
who would take the money, and, without deduct-
ing either from principal or interest, call it a
“tine.”
Dick Fanning entered the kitchen, where Mrs.
Lister sat drying cups and saucers.
“Through changing billets,” he said, “I have
lost a week’s work and a week’s pay, and cannot,
therefore, pay you until next Monday.”
Mrs. Lister managed to reply :
“Yes, Mr. Fanning, I suppose I can wait.”
The bill-of-sale glared before her eyes like a
flaming sword. She could feel it already severing
her heart-strings. She could not weep, for the
flaming sword had seared the fountains of her
eyes at their source. A palsy began to creep over
her. She felt it stealing upwards from her feet
to her knees, and she had to resume her seat.
Then it stole upwards to her heart; and once
more upwards the terrible, consuming weakness
went, upwards, upwards
• * * *
The evening papers had a better sale than usual
that night. There had been an “appalling tragedy
in a boarding-house.” A woman had killed her
three children and then herself.
LEON BRODZKY.
A Wrinkle in Crime.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Yes,” said my acquaintance, the confirmed
criminal, reflectively, “ modern fiction in its
dealings with crime and criminals is lamentably
crude and misinformed. I read all that kind of
stuff, and I find it most amusing in its inaccurate
simplicity. Now I could put pounds in the way
of a writer who wanted new ideas, new situations,
and the proper criminal atmosphere. Here is one
idea —you are welcome to it.
“Suppose you and your friend have a big
scheme in hand—we will say a wholesale swindle
on one cf the banks. We will suppose that the
only weak spot in the arrangements is the danger
of one of you getting collared on his presenting a
certain document at the bank ; you are both fully
alive to the risk, but resolved to take it. It fall’s
to your partner’s lot to present the document, the
worst happens, and he is arrested. Remember,
the scheme is a big one, and large interests are
involved.
“Well, you have arranged with your partner
beforehand what is to be done in case of accident,
and you are both prepared. Your partner is, say,
Beresford. He is arrested at 3 p.m. on Tuesday.
What follows ? He is taken to the Central Police
Station, charged, searched and locked up in a cell
to await the opening of the court at 10 a.m. on
Wednesday. Meanwhile you are aware of your
partner’s misfortune—you watched him being
arrested. So you at once set about your arrange-
ments which will only cost a fiver, all told. About
8 p.m. a third man—call him Nokes—is arrested
in Pitt-street for ‘ insulting behaviour ’ and duly
locked up at the Central, possibly in Beresford’s
cell, but that is immaterial.
“In the morning, at 10, Nokes is fined 405., in
default 7 days; and Beresford, the sensational
forger, is remanded for a week, bail being
refused. Both prisoners are removed from the
dock to their cells, to await the arrival of the
prison-van at 2.30 p.m. At that hour a policeman
calls the names of his passengers from his 50m-
mittal warrants. As the names are called, their
owners step into the van, and are driven off up
Oxford-street to Darlinghurst Gaol. There the
door is opened, and the prisoners, male and
female, form up in line as the chief warder takes
over the warrants from the policeman in charge.
The chief tallies the number of warrants with the
number of prisoners. ‘ All correct.’ For the
present the police are done with Nokes, Beresford
and the others.
“The line of new arrivals is now marched to a
certain subterranean passage, and again ‘ ranked
up.’ The warder in charge calls the name on
warrant No. I—a1 —a woman ! Ladies first, even
there ! She steps forward, and has her ‘descrip-
tion ’ taken ; color of hair, eyes, &c-., duly recorded
in the gaol register, her weight and height ascer-
tained, and she is marched off to the particular
‘ wing ’ that is to shelter her. Presently the name
‘ Beresford ’is called. The real Beresford never
moves a muscle, but Nokes quietly and promptly
steps forward. He is duly measured, weighed,
and ‘described,’ and marched off to the remand
yard. Then comes the call ‘ Nokes,’ and Beres-
ford blandly steps forward and answers for him.
The substitution is unsuspected, and Beresford,
in his role of street brawler, is led off to the fines
and short-sentence department to work out his
7 days. That evening at 5, however, the £2 fine
is paid at the gaol gate, and Beresford is unques-
tioningly set at liberty. Meanwhile the fictitious
Beresford is calmly awaiting events in the remand
wing. Perhaps he makes a fuss next morning,
and demands to know whether or not his £2 fine
has been paid. Or, possibly, if his fee has been
big enough, he waits till the week’s remand is up
and then demands to be set at liberty, and the
substitution is then detected.”
“Yes,” murmured my friend, “ much more can
be done by brains than brute force.”
W.A.
B.L.
New Admiral Fanshawe, who comes to
boss the Australian squadron, divulged before he
left England that he was in the general con-
spiracy to saddle these parts with a big share of
the dear old Motherland’s naval burden. He told
a London banquet that he would “ do everything
possible to encourage the natural development of
the naval spirit in Australia.” But what is the
naval spirit of Australia, and \yhen was it dis-
covered ? It is notorious that Australians are
not given to seamanship. Their ships are
manned mostly by “Dutchmen ” and “ Dagqes ”
and a few incidental Britishers aqd Yanks.
It is tolerably safe ‘tq say that half the little
cqasting vessels that heat about our rivers
and harbqrs dq nqt carry an Australian aboard,
and the larger passenger-oum-cargo boats are
manned either by Maorilanders, or the Dutchmen,
Britishers and Yanks aforementioned. As for the
interest of our population in its alleged squadron,
it would be safe to bet on getting a blankly
ignorant response from 29 out of every 30 Aus-
tralians who might be casually asked how that
make-believe flotilla is made up, where it is
mostly stationed at any time other than the Melb.
Cup carnival, how it is maintained, what the name
of its admiral is, or what the concern does to
justify its existence, or could do if called upon at
any juncture. British admirals seem to found
their notion of our “naval spirit” upon the fact
that a large percentage of the Sydney population
goes out yachting or dingy-sailing or fishing on
Sundays.

Box On!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Life is a ring that’s a heap too small,
With the World for a referee;
And there’s never a man can dodge his call.
Or can hope for a fair decree.
For every kid, when he sees the light,
No matter his class or weight,
Must don the mitts, for a finish-fight
With a cove that’s known as Fate.
You’ve never a chance to fight it through.
You’re done from the starting-gong;
He’s a heavier, cleverer lad than you,
And he’s always young and strong.
And it’s tough to fight at a lightning rate^
When your nerves are badly shook,
With a hostile hall and a paltry gate
And a referee that’s crook.
Your gloves he’ll fake and your seconds square,
And he’ll rough you anyhow;
You’ve no more chance of a fight that’s fair
Than you have in a push’s row.
He’ll land you down and he’ll lift his knee,
He’ll sling and clinch and clout;
And there’s never a scrap where the referee
Is game to foul him out.
Some drop their bag when they spot him scowl,
Some start with a heap of pluck,
Some cock their tails and they toss the tow*!
Ere ever a blow is struck.
Some strike him times when his temper’s strong,
And, as soon as they start the bout,
A merciful boshter comes along
And woodens them fairly out.
But most of us have the luck to hit
On a score of rounds or more,
With our eyes bunged up and our noses split,
And our hands cut up and sore.
With the pain and the blood and the shaking knee,
And the thought that your chance is gone,
You clinch for time, when the referee
Gives the terrible word “ Box on ! ”
There are times when you think you’ve got him
done;
He’ll stagger and kid and paw,
But when you reach for a mighty one,
He’ll counter across your jaw.
You’re dead to the world ; and the gamest funk,
When they fail in every joint;
And you might as well punch at an ironbark trunk
As land on his ugly point.
“Box on ! Box on ! Box on ! Box on ! ”
The words fall stern and quick.
Your pluck is fled; your strength is gone ;
Your breathing’s hard and thick ;
Your head is banged from side to side;
The lights grow dim that shone ;
The while the words ring loud and wide,
“ Box on ! Box on ! Box on 1 ”
“ Box on ! ” —and, helpless on the ropes,
You take his fiendish blows.
“ Box on ! ” —buoyed up by foolish hopes,
You weakly clinch and close.
At last, half dazed, with failing breath,
You swing; he counters —then
You fall, and grim timekeeper Death
Ticks off the seconds ten.
CIRO.
Piety’s Monument.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The old man gathered in his contemplation from
elsewhere, and fixed his pink-edged eyes on mine.
I coughed, and then began: “Didn’t old Piety
work the kilns above here ? What sort of lime did
he burn?” And I offered the old fellow my
flask.
He picked up a battered jam-tin and gazed
thoughtfully into it. Then he poked his finger
in. Lastly, he emptied all my whisky into it,
and drank it swiftly and suspiciously. “Eh?”
he said. Then something began to work in the
old man; his reserve seemed to crack and come
to pieces; he burst-up slowly, as it were, and
crumbled into speech. “What should you know
about old Piety ? ” he asked, uneasily, in a sorrow-
ful, whining tone “ Oh, he could burn lime,” he
went on, “leastways, he thought he could. But
there was as much difference between his kilns and
mine as between oysters an’ cheese. Well! well!
You knowed him?” he whispered, stroking the
bricks in the chimney beside him, thoughtfully.
“No,” I replied, “only heard of him.”
“ And heard wrong,” said the old man, simply.
“ Now I’ll tell you the faeks.” Then he went off
in a low, quavering whine : “ Piety owned these
here kilns, an’ Piety burnt lime. Leastways, he
said he did, but it was me as done it. But when
Piety put his finger in, the kiln was all stone. He
worrited here, he worrited there, muddling and
mulling every blessed kil’ till he had me nigh
crazed, and my fingers raw picking out stone.
‘Jus’ give me the contrack,’ I’d say—‘the con-
track to burn lime at so much a bushel.’ ‘ No, you
don’t,’ says he. An’ that’s all I ever got out of
him ; ‘No you don’t! not in my day,’ and then he
went and killed the kangaroo,”
“The kangaroo?” I asked. “What kangaroo?”
“Bill’s kangaroo* he replied, querulously.
“Bill had a pet kangaroo—Bill, the old man’s
grandson, as owns these here kilns now. They
didn’t get on, the old man and Bill, and lived in
different huts. Bill was an orphan. Well, the
kiln was about half full and burning—old Piety,
he was great at half-kilns—when the kangaroo
hopped onto the kiln bank and ate old Piety’s
dinner, bread and butter and what not. Oho ! he
was wild when he found it out. Then the beast
came and rooted at him with its fore-paws, playing
like. With that, he kicks it; but, lor’, the beast
thought he was playing with it, so it just turns
back upon him, a-twiddling its paws. Then he
goes for it real spiteful, and gives it a tremenjous
kick that sends it flying, right into the burning
kiln.”
The old man/paused bere and stared into space,
mumbling. Then he went on, monotonously:
“Lari ! You would have larfed to see that kan-
garoo jump. But, lor’! there’s no jumping out of
a kiln.' D’ye see the pint? But Bill, he didn’t
larf when he finds it all out. There was the last
of the kangaroo smoking in the blue flame a-fcop
o’ the kiln. ‘Oh, you old devil!’ he says to
Piety; ‘it would serve you right to go through
the kiln yourself ! ’ Then he cleared right away,
and wouldn’t speak to nobody. But it was the
ehilder as did it.”
“ Did what ? ” I asked.
“Well, it was like this,” he explained. “My
fingers was red-raw with the half-burnt stone, and
I was a-tying them up in rags, and abusing of old
Piety. ‘ Couldn’t burn charcoal, let alone lime 1 ’
I says. And the ehilder they took it round to
Piety hisself. Well! the kiln was emptied, and
we was going to start another, when he comes
toddling down. ‘ Clear out o’ this! ’he squeals at
me. * You old weather-beaten windbag, you !’ he
cries. ‘ It’s you that knows what spoils the kilns !’
he says; ‘ just you clear out. I’ll put the kiln in
myself this time.’ ‘ And welcome,’ says I, *if yoo
draw it yourself,’ says I.
“ ‘ None of your cheek! ’ he says, coming at me
with the sieve, and so I clears away, larfing to
myself at the mess he’d make of it, and waiting
for his tantrum to blow over. Well, I camps here
in the hut, day by day, and hears them bumbling
away up the valley, putting in the kiln and light-
ing it. Then when it’s time to draw it, I goes up
to the kiln, but no Piety.
“ ‘ He’s not turned up,’ says the quarryman.
“ ‘ And not likely, says I, ‘ with a kil’ful o’
stone. I’d be ashamed to look a man in the face,’
I says. So I sets to work and draws the kiln as
usual. But no Piety. And what’s more, he
wasn’t about his hut, nor nowhere’s. Then I
begins to think and think, and I draws that kiln
slow and steady. But at last I draws a sort of
bit o’ white stick—lime, like the rest. I has a
good look at it, and then says to myself, says I”——•
Here the maundering old man paused, looking
into space, then pulling hirnself together went on :
“ I says to myself : ‘ Here he comes, feet first.’
I thought as much ! It was the shin-bone.”
“ Whose shin-bone? ” I demanded.
“Why old Piety’s, of course,” he replied.
“ What ?—he’d fallen into the kiln ? ” I asked,
horrified.
The old man turned viciously upon me for
damaging his story.
“There you go,” he cried—“blurting the thing
out ! Why, of course he’d fell in the kiln—what
else would he do? There was his tracks to the
kiln edge, and a few bricks gone where he lost his
footing. Well, I jus’ leaves it there and put a
bag over it. Then I goes down to Bill—a-digging
in the orchard—and says to him : ‘ About this
here contrack. Will you ’gree to let me have the
contrack after the old man’s dead ? ’
“ ‘That’s looking ahead with a vengeance,’ he
says, grinning.
“ ‘Never mind,’ I says ; ‘Good onderstandings
makes long fren’s,’ I says.
“ ‘ Oh, just as you like,’ he says, digging away ;
‘ I’m not too pertickler ! ’
“ ‘ That’s a bargain ?’ I asks.
“ ‘ That’s a bargain,’ he says, just to be rid o’
me. Then I says to him : ‘ Come up to the kiln,
I want yer to help me a while.’ So he comes.
Then I takes the bag off the bones. ‘ Look at
that! ’ I says.
“ ‘ Well,’ says he, ‘and what about it?’
“‘Well,’ I says, ‘That’s your respeoted
gran’father; leastwise all that’s left of him. ’
“You could have knocked him down with a
feather. When he comes to see it all, he took on
dreadful.
“‘Good Gawd,’ he says, and ‘’Orrible!
’Orrible !’
“ Then says I: ‘Be a man ! It might have been
wuss. It might have bin me,’ I says—‘ or yerself,’
Isays. ‘lt might have bin me —and then who’s
to burn the lime ? ’ But he took-on all the same.
He took that dreadful a view of it. ‘ Oho ! ’
quavered the old fellow, wagging his head. ‘He
took a gloomby view of it, a very gloomby view of
it!’
“ ‘ Now you go away !’ I says to him, ‘and I’ll
draw this lime, and bag it.’
“ ‘ Bag that lime!’ says he, ‘ and my gran’father
amongst it! ’
“ ‘ My wages is in it,’ I says, firm, ‘ but I’ll get
what I can of him out,’ and so I did, and put the
bone or two on the shed roof away from the
ehilder. Put ’em there not thinking, for there
came a shower of rain while I was away for the
cart, and when I got back the bones had all slacked
up.
“ But look ye here,” went on the old man con-
fidently, “I had my own little idee so as old
Piety’d not be lost sight of altogether. I takes
about harf the lime the bones made—real lime,
mind you—and makes up a bit of mortar for this
here chimbly—which I was building at the time.
S o you see these here bricks and that bit mortar ?
Well, I reckon that’s old Piety’s monument. But
as for the rest, Bill he buried it in the orchard,
and when the Perlice came up there was nothing
left but the buttons.”
POINT BLANK
Martyrdom for the faith in Kalgoorlie :
We had our warmest day for the season on Sunday
last, the mercury registering 107 in the shade. The
gospel service was consequently not a large one. The
chapel is of iron, unlined and unwhitened ; the warmth
can thus be imagined.
A Maoriland correspondent:—
There is a gratifying slump in jingoistic militarism
here; in fact, not even the shrewdest anti-Mafficker
could have anticipated so sudden a reaction. Even
“ King ” Seddon manages to make speeches without
cheap sneers at old Dad Kruger or egging on the Maoris
to a Boer blood-bath. During the late election, candi-
dates of all parties, including those of the most approved
Seddonian brand, declared that it was time to slow-down,
and I heard some speeches which, if uttered by a Bond
orator in Cape Colony just now, would be dubbed
“ treasonable.” The only exception was one Chisholm,
a pious person who sought election for Scotchbyterian
Dunedin and who “ran” as a Jingo-Bible-in-Schools
advocate, but came below little “pro-Boer" Barclay.
For Christchurch, “ Tommy ” Taylor, who has all
along denounced the late war as the work of
a gang of social brigands, topped the poll, and the little
war-trumpet, Collins, found himself unseated ; while for
Auckland City, Napier, the uniformed “ captain ” who
complained to the commandant when some sentries at
the vestibule of Parliament House omitted to salute him,
was badly defeated. Our much-vaunted expansion policy
in the South Pacific has got “ no forrader ” than the Cook
Islands fiasco, and since Napier’s defeat the Auckland
papers have ceased to shout about “the tyranny in Fiji.”
Napier was the chief of the little scheming clique at
Auckland which encouraged the patriot Berkeley in his
plan for bringing Maoriland into the squabbles of Fiji.
The probability is that Seddon’s attempt to set up a rival
Commonwealth will stop short wi th the inclusion of the few
atolls and the leper island which comprise the Cook group.
The plain unpolished truth is that the public has at last
come to its senses. The scandalous intolerance shown by
the “ loyal ” Imperialists towards all who dared to differ
from them is now forgotten by everyone except the
courageous minority who refused to kow-tow to the
apologists for the Rhodesian buccaneers. Nor can
thoughtful, self-respecting citizens forget readily the
shameful orgies when Barnum Powell got himself
“ relieved ” and when peace was patched up with
the Dutch farmers. It was cabled recently that a
private emissary named Wyatt has left Hingland for
the “ Kawlinies,” where he proposes persuading the
people to share the Imperial military burdens. Per-
sonally I would rejoice seeing Wyatt at work, for he
would be trying to wheel a barrow up a rather too ateep
hill.
